The Locus of Superiority and Ordering
Loci of superiority and ordering for identity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the loci of superiority (al-awla) and ordering (al-tartib) to determine whether two things are identical in dialectical reasoning.

Core Idea
If one thing is not more worthy of being itself, it is not identical; equal superiority implies identity.

Another set of loci relies on the notion of being 'more worthy' (أولى) and ordering (ترتيب). The argument from superiority states that if one thing is no more worthy of being itself than another, they are not identical. The ordering locus examines whether two things are equally greater or better in a given ranking; if so, they are one.

Guided Path
1
Understand the superiority argument
The passage says: 'if something is not more worthy of being `is it itself`, then what is not more worthy is not this.' This means that if A is not more worthy of being identical to B than B is, then A is not B.
This is a negative test: lack of asymmetry suggests non-identity.
2
Apply the ordering criterion
If each of two things is 'more and better than all others' in an ordering, they are one. But if one is more than the other, or more than all else, they are not identical.
Equality in superiority indicates identity.
3
Check concomitants
The ordering must be one: if the ordering of one differs from the other, they are not identical.
This reinforces the identity condition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم من المواضع مواضع التصريف كما إذا كانت العدالة بعينها الشجاعة؛ فالشجاع عدل، والعدل شجاع، وبالعكس إن كان العدل هو بعينه الشجاع. فالشجاعة عدالة، والعدالة شجاعة ويخالف المواضع في باب العرض فإنه لا يجب هناك الهو هو بل هناك حمل فقط . وكذلك في المتقابلات والفاسدات والأسباب الفاعلة وغيرها. وكذلك من طريق الأولى أنه لم يكن ما هو هو أولى بأن يكون هو هو فليس ما ليس أولى فهو هذا. وأيضًا إن كان كل واحد منهما في ترتيب أكثر وأفضل فإنه إذا كان كل واحد منهما أكثر وأفضل من جميع الغير في ذلك الترتيب فهما واحد. وأما إذا كان أحدهما أكثر في ذلك من الآخر أو كان أكثر من كل ما سواه فليس أحدهما هو الآخر؛ ثمَّ من اللوازم أن يكون الترتيب واحدا. ومن المواضع أيضًا أن ينظر هل يختلف في الأقوال العارضة، وأيضًا هل يرتقي إلى مقولات مختلفة، وهل إن كان جنسها واحدًا ففصولها كذلك؛ وأمثال هذه المواضع مواضع الإبطال. وأيضًا هل كل واحد منهما إذا أضيف إلى ثالث يحصل مجموعا هو بعينه مجموع الآخر. وأيضًا هل يتساويان مما يلزم وضع الشيء ورفعه أو يلزمه رفع الشيء ووضعه كان ذلك حقا وباطلا؛ فإن الشرطية ليس صدقها في صدق المقدم أو التالي بل فيصدق اللزوم. ومثاله أنه إن كان الهواء والخلاء واحدًا فما يلزم من رفع الهواء كما يلزم من رفع الخلاء، وما يلزم من وضع الهواء يلزم من وضع الخلاء. وما يلزمه الهواء أو يلزم رفعه عند وضعه أو رفعه كذلك يلزمه الخلاء / أو رفعه لكنا إذا توهمنا رفع الهواء بقي الخلاء عند أصحاب الخلاء؛ وليس يلزم عند رفع الهواء أن يبقى وضع الخلاء فليس الهواء والخلاء واحدًا. وأيضًا هل يختلف الأمران في المحمولات؛ وهذا كالمكرَّر بالقوة وموضع في قوة هذا وهو إن لم يكونا واحدًا بالجنس إن كان لهما جنس ، أو لم يكونا واحدًا بالنوع إن كان لهما نوع لم يكونا واحدًا بالعدد بالوجه الذي مرَّ : وهذه المواضع كثير؛ منها مواضع الإثبات والإبطال المطلقين يستعمل في محمول مخصوص وهو الهو هو وكثير منها يخص الهو هو ؛ ويجب أن ننظر كي لا يكون أحدهما ليس محمولا على الآخر بما قدمناه من مواضع الإثبات والإبطال والتعرض لهذه المواضع التي مرَّ ذكرها كلا وجملة لأجل أنها مستعملة في القياس الجدلية وما يتعلق بها، ولنقتصر على هذا القدر فإن التعرُّض للزوائد من الزوائد وإن كانت لا يخلو من الفوائد.
Then among the loci are the loci of convertibility, as when justice is identical to courage; so the courageous person is just, and the just person is courageous, and conversely if the just person is identical to the courageous person. So courage is justice, and justice is courage. It differs from the loci in the category of accident, because there 'is it itself' is not required, but only predication. Similarly in the case of opposites, corruptions, efficient causes, and others. Also from the argument from superiority: if something is not more worthy of being 'is it itself', then what is not more worthy is not this. Also if each of them is in an ordering more and better, then if each of them is more and better than all others in that ordering, they are one. But if one of them is more in that than the other or is more than all else, then one of them is not the other. Then from the concomitants, the ordering must be one. Also among the loci is to consider whether they differ in accidental predications, and also whether they ascend to different categories, and whether if their genus is one their differentiate are also one; and such loci are loci of falsification. Also whether each of them, when added to a third, produces a sum identical to the sum of the other. Also whether they are equal in that positing the thing and removing it necessitate or its removal and positing necessitate, whether that is true or false; for the conditional's truth is not in the truth of the antecedent or consequent, but in the truth of the implication. An example: if air and void are one, then what follows from the removal of air follows as from the removal of void, and what follows from the positing of air follows from the positing of void. And what air necessitates or its removal necessitates upon its positing or removal, likewise void necessitates it or its removal. But if we imagine the removal of air, void remains according to proponents of the void; and it does not follow upon the removal of air that the positing of void remains, so air and void are not one. Also whether the two matters differ in attributes; and this is like a repeated potentiality and a locus 'in the power of this', and if they are not one in genus (if they have a genus), or not one in species (if they have a species), they are not one in number in the manner mentioned. These loci are many; among them are the loci of absolute affirmation and falsification used in a specific predicate, which is 'is it itself', and many of them pertain to 'is it itself'. And we must consider so that one of them is not predicated on the other by what we have previously mentioned from the loci of affirmation and falsification. And covering these loci mentioned generally and in detail is because they are used in dialectical syllogism and what pertains to it. And we will restrict ourselves to this amount, for engaging with superfluities is superfluous, though not devoid of benefits.
Source pages p. 570

Commentary
This passage is a classification of various loci (topics) used in dialectical reasoning to determine whether two things are identical ( هو هو ) or different. The author begins with the locus of convertibility (التصريف): if two attributes are identical in essence, then they are convertible; e.g., if justice is courage, then every just person is courageous and vice versa. This differs from accidental predication (باب العرض), where 'is it itself' is not required. The author then discusses the argument from superiority (من طريق الأولى): if one thing is not more worthy of being itself than another, they are not identical. Also, if two things are equally more and better in an ordering, they are one; otherwise not. Another locus involves consdering whether they have the same genus and differentiate; if not, they are different. The author gives an example using the hypothetical identity of air and void: if they were identical, the entailments of positing or removing one would match the other, but since removing air does not remove void (according to proponents of the void), they are not identical. The passage also mentions loci of comparison in terms of composition (adding to a third) and conditional entailment (اللزوم). Finally, the author notes that these loci are used in dialectical syllogisms and that only the essential ones are mentioned.

Key Terms
طريق الأولى
ترتيب
أكثر وأفضل
لازم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when are two things considered one based on the ordering locus?
A) When one is more than the other in the ordering.
B) When each is more and better than all others in the same ordering.
C) When their orderings are different.
D) When one is more than all else.

Key Takeaway
The loci of superiority and ordering provide criteria for identity based on comparative excellence and ranking equality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider two divine attributes: al-Raḥmān (the Merciful) and al-Raḥīm (the Compassionate). Apply the superiority argument: Is one more worthy of being merciful than the other? If not, can we conclude they are identical? Use the ordering locus to test if they are equally superior in the ranking of divine attributes.

The Locus of Accidents, Categories, and Genus-Differentiae
Loci of falsification through accidents, categories, and differentiate

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to use the loci of accidents, categories, and genus-differentiae to falsify identity claims in dialectical reasoning.

Core Idea
Differences in accidental predicates, categories, or differentiate indicate non-identity.

The author lists several loci that serve to falsify (إبطال) identity claims. If two things differ in their accidental predicates (الأقوال العارضة), ascend to different categories (مقولات), or have different differentiate despite sharing a genus, then they are not identical. These are powerful tools for disproving a claim of essential sameness.

Guided Path
1
Examine accidental predications
Check if the two things have different accidental attributes. If they do, they cannot be identical. The passage says: 'هل يختلف في الأقوال العارضة' (whether they differ in accidental predications).
Accidents vary while essence remains the same, but if accidents differ, identity is impossible.
2
Check category ascent
Determine whether the two things belong to different categories (مقولات). If they do, they are not identical. The passage: 'هل يرتقي إلى مقولات مختلفة' (whether they ascend to different categories).
Different categories mean different essences.
3
Analyze genus and differentiate
If the genus is the same but the differentiate differ, then they are not identical. The passage: 'إن كان جنسها واحدًا ففصولها كذلك' (if their genus is one, their differentiate are also one).
Differentiate distinguish species within a genus; different differentiate implies different species.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم من المواضع مواضع التصريف كما إذا كانت العدالة بعينها الشجاعة؛ فالشجاع عدل، والعدل شجاع، وبالعكس إن كان العدل هو بعينه الشجاع. فالشجاعة عدالة، والعدالة شجاعة ويخالف المواضع في باب العرض فإنه لا يجب هناك الهو هو بل هناك حمل فقط . وكذلك في المتقابلات والفاسدات والأسباب الفاعلة وغيرها. وكذلك من طريق الأولى أنه لم يكن ما هو هو أولى بأن يكون هو هو فليس ما ليس أولى فهو هذا. وأيضًا إن كان كل واحد منهما في ترتيب أكثر وأفضل فإنه إذا كان كل واحد منهما أكثر وأفضل من جميع الغير في ذلك الترتيب فهما واحد. وأما إذا كان أحدهما أكثر في ذلك من الآخر أو كان أكثر من كل ما سواه فليس أحدهما هو الآخر؛ ثمَّ من اللوازم أن يكون الترتيب واحدا. ومن المواضع أيضًا أن ينظر هل يختلف في الأقوال العارضة، وأيضًا هل يرتقي إلى مقولات مختلفة، وهل إن كان جنسها واحدًا ففصولها كذلك؛ وأمثال هذه المواضع مواضع الإبطال. وأيضًا هل كل واحد منهما إذا أضيف إلى ثالث يحصل مجموعا هو بعينه مجموع الآخر. وأيضًا هل يتساويان مما يلزم وضع الشيء ورفعه أو يلزمه رفع الشيء ووضعه كان ذلك حقا وباطلا؛ فإن الشرطية ليس صدقها في صدق المقدم أو التالي بل فيصدق اللزوم. ومثاله أنه إن كان الهواء والخلاء واحدًا فما يلزم من رفع الهواء كما يلزم من رفع الخلاء، وما يلزم من وضع الهواء يلزم من وضع الخلاء. وما يلزمه الهواء أو يلزم رفعه عند وضعه أو رفعه كذلك يلزمه الخلاء / أو رفعه لكنا إذا توهمنا رفع الهواء بقي الخلاء عند أصحاب الخلاء؛ وليس يلزم عند رفع الهواء أن يبقى وضع الخلاء فليس الهواء والخلاء واحدًا. وأيضًا هل يختلف الأمران في المحمولات؛ وهذا كالمكرَّر بالقوة وموضع في قوة هذا وهو إن لم يكونا واحدًا بالجنس إن كان لهما جنس ، أو لم يكونا واحدًا بالنوع إن كان لهما نوع لم يكونا واحدًا بالعدد بالوجه الذي مرَّ : وهذه المواضع كثير؛ منها مواضع الإثبات والإبطال المطلقين يستعمل في محمول مخصوص وهو الهو هو وكثير منها يخص الهو هو ؛ ويجب أن ننظر كي لا يكون أحدهما ليس محمولا على الآخر بما قدمناه من مواضع الإثبات والإبطال والتعرض لهذه المواضع التي مرَّ ذكرها كلا وجملة لأجل أنها مستعملة في القياس الجدلية وما يتعلق بها، ولنقتصر على هذا القدر فإن التعرُّض للزوائد من الزوائد وإن كانت لا يخلو من الفوائد.

Similarly in the case of opposites, corruptions, efficient causes, and others. Also if each of them is in an ordering more and better, then if each of them is more and better than all others in that ordering, they are one. But if one of them is more in that than the other or is more than all else, then one of them is not the other. Then from the concomitants, the ordering must be one. Also among the loci is to consider whether they differ in accidental predications, and also whether they ascend to different categories, and whether if their genus is one their differentiate are also one; and such loci are loci of falsification. Also whether each of them, when added to a third, produces a sum identical to the sum of the other. Also whether they are equal in that positing the thing and removing it necessitate or its removal and positing necessitate, whether that is true or false; for the conditional's truth is not in the truth of the antecedent or consequent, but in the truth of the implication. How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider two contracts: bayʿ (sale) and ijāra (lease). Check if they differ in accidental predications (e.g., possession vs. usufruct), ascend to different categories of transactions, or have different differentiate within the genus of contracts. Use the falsification loci to argue whether they are identical or not.

The Locus of Composition and Conditional Entailment
Loci of composition and conditional entailment for identity testing

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the loci of composition (adding to a third) and conditional entailment (luzum) to test identity in dialectical arguments.

Core Idea
If two things are identical, their sums with a third are identical, and their conditional entailments (positing/removing) must match.

Two more sophisticated loci are presented: the composition locus, which checks whether adding each of two things to a third yields the same sum, and the conditional entailment locus, which examines whether the implications of positing or removing one thing are the same as for the other. The author illustrates the latter with the example of air and void: if they were identical, removing one would remove the other, but it does not, so they are distinct.

Guided Path
1
Apply the composition test
Check whether adding each of the two things to a third yields an identical sum. The passage says: 'هل كل واحد منهما إذا أضيف إلى ثالث يحصل مجموعا هو بعينه مجموع الآخر' (whether each when added to a third produces a sum identical to the sum of the other).
If the sums are different, they are not identical.
2
Formulate the conditional entailment test
For any proposition p, if A and B are identical, then positing A entails positing B, and removing A entails removing B. The passage: 'هل يتساويان مما يلزم وضع الشيء ورفعه' (whether they are equal in what necessitates positing a thing and its removal).
This is a necessary condition for identity.
3
Apply to the air-void example
The author tests identity of air and void: if they were identical, then removing air should remove void, but void remains according to proponents of the void. Hence they are not identical.
This concrete example demonstrates the locus in action.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم من المواضع مواضع التصريف كما إذا كانت العدالة بعينها الشجاعة؛ فالشجاع عدل، والعدل شجاع، وبالعكس إن كان العدل هو بعينه الشجاع. فالشجاعة عدالة، والعدالة شجاعة ويخالف المواضع في باب العرض فإنه لا يجب هناك الهو هو بل هناك حمل فقط . وكذلك في المتقابلات والفاسدات والأسباب الفاعلة وغيرها. وكذلك من طريق الأولى أنه لم يكن ما هو هو أولى بأن يكون هو هو فليس ما ليس أولى فهو هذا. وأيضًا إن كان كل واحد منهما في ترتيب أكثر وأفضل فإنه إذا كان كل واحد منهما أكثر وأفضل من جميع الغير في ذلك الترتيب فهما واحد. وأما إذا كان أحدهما أكثر في ذلك من الآخر أو كان أكثر من كل ما سواه فليس أحدهما هو الآخر؛ ثمَّ من اللوازم أن يكون الترتيب واحدا. ومن المواضع أيضًا أن ينظر هل يختلف في الأقوال العارضة، وأيضًا هل يرتقي إلى مقولات مختلفة، وهل إن كان جنسها واحدًا ففصولها كذلك؛ وأمثال هذه المواضع مواضع الإبطال. وأيضًا هل كل واحد منهما إذا أضيف إلى ثالث يحصل مجموعا هو بعينه مجموع الآخر. وأيضًا هل يتساويان مما يلزم وضع الشيء ورفعه أو يلزمه رفع الشيء ووضعه كان ذلك حقا وباطلا؛ فإن الشرطية ليس صدقها في صدق المقدم أو التالي بل فيصدق اللزوم. ومثاله أنه إن كان الهواء والخلاء واحدًا فما يلزم من رفع الهواء كما يلزم من رفع الخلاء، وما يلزم من وضع الهواء يلزم من وضع الخلاء. وما يلزمه الهواء أو يلزم رفعه عند وضعه أو رفعه كذلك يلزمه الخلاء / أو رفعه لكنا إذا توهمنا رفع الهواء بقي الخلاء عند أصحاب الخلاء؛ وليس يلزم عند رفع الهواء أن يبقى وضع الخلاء فليس الهواء والخلاء واحدًا. وأيضًا هل يختلف الأمران في المحمولات؛ وهذا كالمكرَّر بالقوة وموضع في قوة هذا وهو إن لم يكونا واحدًا بالجنس إن كان لهما جنس ، أو لم يكونا واحدًا بالنوع إن كان لهما نوع لم يكونا واحدًا بالعدد بالوجه الذي مرَّ : وهذه المواضع كثير؛ منها مواضع الإثبات والإبطال المطلقين يستعمل في محمول مخصوص وهو الهو هو وكثير منها يخص الهو هو ؛ ويجب أن ننظر كي لا يكون أحدهما ليس محمولا على الآخر بما قدمناه من مواضع الإثبات والإبطال والتعرض لهذه المواضع التي مرَّ ذكرها كلا وجملة لأجل أنها مستعملة في القياس الجدلية وما يتعلق بها، ولنقتصر على هذا القدر فإن التعرُّض للزوائد من الزوائد وإن كانت لا يخلو من الفوائد.
Then among the loci are the loci of convertibility, as when justice is identical to courage; so the courageous person is just, and the just person is courageous, and conversely if the just person is identical to the courageous person. So courage is justice, and justice is courage. It differs from the loci in the category of accident, because there 'is it itself' is not required, but only predication. Similarly in the case of opposites, corruptions, efficient causes, and others. Also from the argument from superiority: if something is not more worthy of being 'is it itself', then what is not more worthy is not this. Also if each of them is in an ordering more and better, then if each of them is more and better than all others in that ordering, they are one. But if one of them is more in that than the other or is more than all else, then one of them is not the other. Then from the concomitants, the ordering must be one. Also among the loci is to consider whether they differ in accidental predications, and also whether they ascend to different categories, and whether if their genus is one their differentiate are also one; and such loci are loci of falsification. Also whether each of them, when added to a third, produces a sum identical to the sum of the other. Also whether they are equal in that positing the thing and removing it necessitate or its removal and positing necessitate, whether that is true or false; for the conditional's truth is not in the truth of the antecedent or consequent, but in the truth of the implication. An example: if air and void are one, then what follows from the removal of air follows as from the removal of void, and what follows from the positing of air follows from the positing of void. And what air necessitates or its removal necessitates upon its positing or removal, likewise void necessitates it or its removal. But if we imagine the removal of air, void remains according to proponents of the void; and it does not follow upon the removal of air that the positing of void remains, so air and void are not one. Also whether the two matters differ in attributes; and this is like a repeated potentiality and a locus 'in the power of this', and if they are not one in genus (if they have a genus), or not one in species (if they have a species), they are not one in number in the manner mentioned. These loci are many; among them are the loci of absolute affirmation and falsification used in a specific predicate, which is 'is it itself', and many of them pertain to 'is it itself'. And we must consider so that one of them is not predicated on the other by what we have previously mentioned from the loci of affirmation and falsification. And covering these loci mentioned generally and in detail is because they are used in dialectical syllogism and what pertains to it. And we will restrict ourselves to this amount, for engaging with superfluities is superfluous, though not devoid of benefits.
Source pages p. 570

Commentary
This passage is a classification of various loci (topics) used in dialectical reasoning to determine whether two things are identical ( هو هو ) or different. The author begins with the locus of convertibility (التصريف): if two attributes are identical in essence, then they are convertible; e.g., if justice is courage, then every just person is courageous and vice versa. This differs from accidental predication (باب العرض), where 'is it itself' is not required. The author then discusses the argument from superiority (من طريق الأولى): if one thing is not more worthy of being itself than another, they are not identical. Also, if two things are equally more and better in an ordering, they are one; otherwise not. Another locus involves consdering whether they have the same genus and differentiate; if not, they are different. The author gives an example using the hypothetical identity of air and void: if they were identical, the entailments of positing or removing one would match the other, but since removing air does not remove void (according to proponents of the void), they are not identical. The passage also mentions loci of comparison in terms of composition (adding to a third) and conditional entailment (اللزوم). Finally, the author notes that these loci are used in dialectical syllogisms and that only the essential ones are mentioned.

Checkpoint
In the air and void example, what demonstrates that they are not identical?
A) Adding air to a third gives a different sum than adding void.
B) Removing air does not remove void.
C) They have different accidental predicates.
D) They belong to different categories.

Key Takeaway
Identity requires that the conditional entailments of positing and removal are identical; otherwise, identity is refuted.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take two concepts from Islamic cosmology: al-ʿAql (the Intellect) and al-Nafs (the Soul). Apply the composition test: Does adding each to a third (e.g., the body) produce the same sum? Apply the conditional entailment test: If the Intellect and Soul are identical, then removing the Intellect should remove the Soul. Evaluate whether this holds.
?
Construct a conditional syllogism using the air-void example: Major premise: 'If air and void are identical, then removing air removes void.' Minor premise: 'Removing air does not remove void (since void remains).' Conclusion: 'Therefore, air and void are not identical.' Identify the form as modus tollens.

The Three Essential Tasks of the Questioner in Dialectic
Author explains the three essential tasks for the questioner in dialectical debate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list and explain the three essential tasks of the questioner in a dialectical debate, and distinguish them from the problem (al-mas'ala).

Core Idea
Dialectic is about securing premises — the problem is external.

In dialectical debates, the questioner plays a crucial role in obtaining premises from the answerer. This passage outlines the three essential tasks that every questioner must master. Understanding these tasks is key to effective disputation in Islamic scholarly traditions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three essential matters
Find in the Arabic the three things the author says must come together for the questioner. They are listed in the passage after the statement that the problem is external.
The three matters are introduced with أما الأول, الثاني, الثالث.
2
Understand the first task: preparing the locus
The first task is إعداد الموضع الذي يأخذ المقدمة لقياسه. This means the questioner must decide where to look for the premise that supports his syllogism.
This task is shared with the philosopher.
3
Understand the second task: arranging the method
The second task involves two parts: arranging the method to obtain the premise from the answerer and the method of following up on the one who denies it. This is about persuasion and handling objections.
This is specific to the dialectician.
4
Understand the third task: articulating
The third task is التصريح بما أعد في النفس مخاطبة به للغير. The questioner must clearly express the prepared premise when addressing the opponent.
This is also specific to the dialectician.
5
Distinguish dialectician from philosopher
The philosopher does not need to secure premises from an opponent; he takes truth in itself. Therefore, tasks 2 and 3 are unnecessary for him. The dialectician, however, relies on obtaining premises from an interlocutor.
This distinction highlights the social nature of dialectic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : وهنا بحث آخر من الأبحاث الجدلية وذلك في وصايا السائل والمجيب على سبيل الخصوص والعموم. أما السائل فقد يكون في المقدمات، وقد يكون في الغير؛ والمجادلة عمدتها السؤال، فنقول: إن عمدة الأمور التي يجب أن يلتئم للسائل ثلاثة؛ والمسألة شيء خارج منها وهي كالعرض، والجدل نحو المشير إليه فلذلك ليست المسألة جزءًا من الجدل. أما الأول من الثلاثة فهو إعداد الموضع الذي يأخذ المقدمة لقياسه والثاني ترتيب كيفية التوسل في نفسه إلى تسلمها، وكيفية التتبع على منكرها. والثالث هو التصريح بما أعد في النفس مخاطبة به للغير. والوجه الأول منها مشترك بين الجدلي» والفيلسوف» بخلاف الثاني والثالث؛ إذ لا حاجة للفيلسوف إلى تسلم شيء وهو يأخذ ما هو حق في نفسه لا بالتسلم ولا النظر الحكمي متعلق بالمخاطبة وبالجهاد فيها؛ فلا يبالي في الخطاب التعليمي أن لا تسلم مقدمة بعد أن تكون المقدمات مناسبة
Section: Here is another discussion of dialectical discussions, namely concerning the advice to the questioner and the answerer in particular and in general. As for the questioner, he may be concerned with the premises or with other matters; the core of disputation is questioning. So we say: The essential matters that must come together for the questioner are three; the problem (al-mas'ala) is something external to them, like an accident, and dialectic (al-jadal) is directed toward that which is pointed to, so the problem is not a part of dialectic. As for the first of the three, it is preparing the locus from which he takes the premise for his syllogism. The second is arranging the method of approaching in himself to obtain it, and the method of following up on the one who denies it. The third is articulating what he has prepared in his mind to address to others. The first aspect is shared by the dialectician and the philosopher, unlike the second and third; for the philosopher has no need to obtain something, as he takes what is true in itself, not by obtaining nor by judicial consideration pertaining to addressing and striving therein. So he does not care in instructional discourse whether a premise is granted, as long as the premises are appropriate.
Source pages p. 571

Commentary
This section introduces a new topic in dialectic: advice for the questioner (السائل) and answerer (المجيب). The author focuses on the questioner, who may deal with premises or other matters. The core of disputation (المجادلة) is questioning. The author states that the questioner must have three essential matters (عمدة الأمور). The problem (المسألة) itself is external to these, like an accident; dialectic is directed toward the point, so the problem is not integral to dialectic. The three matters are: 1) preparing the locus (الموضع) from which to take the premise for the syllogism; 2) arranging the method to obtain the premise and to follow up on the denier; 3) articulating what has been prepared. The author notes that the first aspect is shared by the dialectician and the philosopher, but the second and third are not. The philosopher does not need to obtain premises from an opponent; he takes truth in itself, without concern for whether a premise is granted by an interlocutor, as long as the premises are appropriate for teaching.

المجيب
الجدل
المقدمة
الجدلي
الفيلسوف

Checkpoint

C) Articulating what has been prepared in the mind.
D) Defining the problem precisely.

Key Takeaway
The questioner's three tasks — preparing, arranging, and articulating — form the backbone of effective dialectical reasoning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Choose a theological claim (e.g., 'The world is created') and practice the three tasks: first, identify a key premise you need (e.g., 'Everything that changes is temporal'); second, plan how to get the opponent to accept it; third, articulate the question clearly.

Four Purposes of Non-Necessary Premises in Jadal
Author lists four purposes of non-necessary premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four purposes of non-necessary premises in jadal and explain how each serves the dialectician's strategic goals.

Core Idea
Non-necessary premises are tactical tools for concealment, reinforcement, and persuasion.

In jadal, not every premise needs to be strictly necessary for the conclusion. The dialectician employs non-necessary premises for specific strategic reasons. This lesson unpacks the four purposes outlined by the author: reinforcement in induction, division, facilitating understanding, and concealing the conclusion. Understanding these purposes reveals the artistry behind dialectical argumentation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four purposes
Find the four purposes listed in the Arabic: الاستظهار في الاستقراء, القسمة والاستظهار في تفهيم القول, الاجتهاد في إخفاء النتيجة, التكلُّف في إيضاح القول.
The author lists four purposes; note that some combine two ideas.
2
Analyze each purpose's function
For each, determine the goal: reinforcement in induction makes premises more acceptable; division structures the argument; concealing the conclusion prevents early rejection; clarifying the statement aids comprehension.
These purposes are strategic, not for establishing truth.
3
Connect to broader jadal context
Understand that these purposes aim to win acceptance from an opponent, not to demonstrate necessity. The dialectician uses them to manage the flow of argument.
Reflect on how these purposes differ from demonstrative logic.
4
Evaluate rhetorical devices
The author defends using praise, poetry, and flattery in jadal when facing stubborn opponents. Discuss when such devices cross into sophistry.
Legitimacy depends on context and intent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يحتاج فيه إلى أن يخفي قربها من النتيجة الخاصة التي للسائل الجدلي لأنه يجتهد في إخفاء ما يلزم من تلك المقدمات؛ لئلا يتعشر في تسليمها والقضاء بالشيء يستعلمها الجدلي إما ضرورية وإما غير ضرورية والضرورة داخلة في نفس القول الموجب للمطلوب قياسا كان أو استقراء وغير الضروري إنما يورد لأغراض أربعة؛ وهي الاستظهار في الاستقراء، والقسمة والاستظهار في تفهيم القول، والاجتهاد في إخفاء النتيجة، والتكلُّف في إيضاح القول. ثم الاستقراء إنما يستعمل في الجدل على وجوه ثلاثة؛ أحدها في أن يصحح منه المطلوب نفسه. والثاني لأن تصحح به المقدمة الضرورية في المطلوب. والثالث للاستظهار فهو أن تكون المقدمات غير بعيدة عن التسليم بل يسلمها الخصم إذا ظهر من أحوالها أنها محمودة أو مسلمة، والاستقراء كما مر من قبل بوجوه؛ منها ما تكون مقدماته ضرورية فيحصل منه الغرض قطعا. والقسمة قد تورد على مقتضى الضرورة، وقد تورد لتحسين الكلام فيما لا يحتاج إليه فيقال مثلا إن العلم قد يكون أشرف من علم آخر لقوة برهانية، وإما لشرف موضوعه، وإما لكذا وكذا حيث يكون النافع مثلا أن يبين أن العلم شريف. ثم يتعداه إلى وجوه شرفه من غير حاجة إليه فأحد الوجوه الأربعة أن تورد المقدمات للاستقراء الاستظهار دون الضروري، والقسمة التي لا ضرورة إليها. والوجه الثاني أن يورد لبسط القول؛ والتوسع فيه إما لمدح كلامه واستجادة مذهبه، وإما للتعجب ممن ينكر مأخذه، وإما بالأمثلة والاستشهادات بأقاول الناس، وإما بالتصرف في تبديل العبارات للبيان والفصاحة بأن يعبر عن قصة واحدة بعبارات مختلفة كما هو من عادة فصحاء العرب، وإما باستعطاف المخاطب واستمالته إلى التسليم تارة، وتارة بمدحه ونسبته إلى الإنصاف وليس لقائل أن يقول إن بعض هذه المواضع وفسطائية وخطابية فإن تلك المواضع ربَّما احتيج إليها في الجدل؛ وذلك عند المكارة والامتناع عن تسليم ما يكون مشهورًا، والثالث خلط ما يحتاج إليه بما لا يحتاج إليه إخفاء النتيجة وما يقرب منها الحجج والرابع لا يكون للضرورة ولا للحيلة بل للإيضاح والكشف بتبديل الألفاظ وتكرير الأمثلة. فالوجه الثاني يقصد فيه التصديق ؛ وهذا الوجه يقصد فيه تصور (۱)، وقد يستعان فيه بألفاظ الشعراء والأمثال المعروفة. وأما الوصية التي هي في الوجه الأول فبأن لا تذكر المقدمة الضرورية في أول الأمر، ولا يطلب تسليمها صراحًا، فبما لم يسلم بل يجب أن ينتقل عنها إلى تسليم ما هو أعم منها إلى عقد القياس أو الاستقراء؛ وبالجملة إلى عقد ما يقابل وضعه. وأما الوصية في الوجه الذي هو لإخفاء النتيجة فبأن ينظر إن كانت النتيجة تبين قياسات بعيدة؛ فيجب أن يبتدئ بأبعد المقدمات عن النتيجة وتسليمها. فينتقل عنها إلى القريبة فيحسن أن لا يسأل عنها على الترتيب القياسي بل يسأل عن الأول في الترتيب الطبيعي ثانيا، وعن الثاني أولا لئلا يسمع الحد الأوسط بل يسمع تارة في طرف، وتارة في طرف آخر. وقد يحلل بينهما فاصل. وكذلك يجب أن يحلل بين مقدمة ومقدمة بما هو الفاصل بينهما إخفاء للأقرب فإن فيه إخفاء النتيجة. وبالجملة فله أن يتكلف فيما يكون نافعا لإخفاء النتيجة؛ فإن ظهور النتيجة بعد تسليم المقدمات مشتمل على المصالح وكيف ولا يمكن ردها حينئذ؛ وقد كان تسليمها في ضمن تسليم المقدّمات : فإذا أقرَّ بها فكأنه أقرّ بالنتيجة؛ والإنكار بعد الإقرار لا يعتبر لا عقلا ولا شرعًا ولا عادة. (۱) النسخة التصوير وما أثبتناه هو الصواب كما هو الظاهر من السياق.
And it is not necessary in it to conceal the closeness of those premises to the specific conclusion of the dialectical questioner, because he strives to conceal what follows from those premises, lest the opponent become difficult in accepting them. The dialectician uses each thing either as necessary or as non-necessary. The necessary is inherent in the statement that necessitates the required conclusion, whether it be a syllogism or induction. The non-necessary is introduced only for four purposes: reinforcement in induction, division, reinforcement in making the statement understood, striving to conceal the conclusion, and taking trouble to clarify the statement. Then induction is used in dialectic in three ways: first, to correct the required conclusion itself; second, to correct the necessary premise in the required conclusion; third, for reinforcement, so that the premises are not far from acceptance but the opponent accepts them when it appears from their circumstances that they are praiseworthy or accepted. Induction, as previously mentioned, has ways: among them are those whose premises are necessary, so the purpose is achieved definitively. Division may be introduced as required by necessity, or it may be introduced for beautifying speech where it is not needed, for example saying: knowledge may be nobler than another knowledge because of its demonstrative strength, or because of the nobility of its subject, or because of such-and-such, where the benefit is to show that knowledge is noble. Then he goes beyond to its aspects of nobility without need for it. So one of the four purposes is to introduce premises for reinforcement in induction without the necessary, and division without need. The second purpose is to introduce expansion of speech; its extension is either for praising one's speech and considering one's madhhab good, or for expressing wonder at one who denies one's source, or through examples and testimonies from people's sayings, or through manipulation in changing expressions for clarity and eloquence, so that one expresses a single story with different expressions as is the custom of the eloquent Arabs, or through winning over the addressee and inclining him to acceptance sometimes, and sometimes by praising him and attributing fairness to him. And no one can say that some of these places are sophistical and rhetorical, for those places might be needed in dialectic, namely when there is stubbornness and refusal to accept what is well-known. The third is mixing what is needed with what is not needed to conceal the conclusion and what is close to it of proofs. The fourth is not for necessity nor for trickery, but for clarification and uncovering by changing expressions and repeating examples. The second purpose intends assent; this purpose intends conception (1), and it may be assisted by the words of poets and well-known proverbs. As for the advice concerning the first purpose: it is that one should not mention the necessary premise at the beginning, nor demand its acceptance explicitly, as long as it has not been accepted, but one must move from it to the acceptance of what is more general than it, to the formation of the syllogism or induction; in short, to the formation of what corresponds to its position. And as for the advice concerning the purpose that is to conceal the conclusion: it is that one should consider: if the conclusion is made clear by distant syllogisms, then one must begin with the most distant premises from the conclusion and have them accepted. Then one moves from them to the closer ones. It is good not to ask about them in the syllogistic order, but rather ask about the first in the natural order second, and about the second first, so that the middle term is not heard, but is heard sometimes on one side and sometimes on another. And one may interpose a separator between them. Likewise, one must interpose between premise and premise what is a separator between them to conceal the closer, for in that is concealment of the conclusion. In short, one may undertake what is beneficial for concealing the conclusion, for the appearance of the conclusion after acceptance of the premises contains benefits, and how can it be rejected then? And its acceptance was implied in the acceptance of the premises. So if he acknowledges them, it is as if he acknowledged the conclusion. And denial after acknowledgment is not considered rationally, legally, or customarily. (1) The copy is photographic and what we have established is correct as is apparent from the context.
Source pages p. 572

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition on the art of dialectical reasoning (jadal), explaining how a dialectician uses premises and other tools to achieve his goals. The author begins by stating that the dialectician does not need to hide the closeness of premises to the conclusion; rather, he strives to conceal what follows from those premises to avoid the opponent's reluctance to accept them. The premises used are either necessary (i.e., they directly necessitate the required conclusion) or non-necessary. Necessary premises are inherent in the argument itself. Non-necessary premises are introduced for four purposes: (1) reinforcement in induction, (2) division, (3) reinforcement in making the statement understood, (4) striving to hide the conclusion, and (5) taking trouble to clarify the statement. The author then focuses on induction, explaining its three uses in jadal: to correct the desired conclusion itself, to correct a necessary premise in the desired conclusion, and for reinforcement—to make premises more acceptable by appearing praiseworthy. He notes that some inductive arguments have necessary premises, achieving certainty. Division can be used out of necessity or for beautifying speech without need. An example is given: showing that knowledge is nobler due to demonstrative strength or subject nobility, even if not needed. The second purpose of expansion includes praising one's speech, expressing wonder, using examples, varying expressions for eloquence, and flattering the opponent. The author defends these rhetorical devices, saying they are not sophistical but needed in cases of stubbornness. The third purpose is mixing needed and unneeded premises to hide the conclusion. The fourth is pure clarification without trickery. The second purpose aims at tasdiq (assent), while the first aims at tasawwur (conception), and may use poetic words. The author then gives practical advice (wasiyya) for the first purpose: do not mention the necessary premise first; instead, get acceptance for more general premises first. For the purpose of hiding the conclusion, if the conclusion requires distant syllogisms, start with the farthest premises, then move closer. Do not ask in logical order but reverse the natural order to hide the middle term. Interpose separators between premises to further conceal. The author concludes that once premises are accepted, the conclusion is implied, and denial after acknowledgment is invalid rationally, legally, and customarily.

Key Takeaway
The dialectician's skill lies not only in using necessary premises but also in deploying non-necessary premises for strategic advantage.

Three Uses of Induction in Jadal
Author details three uses of induction in dialectic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three purposes of induction in jadal and apply them to strengthen a dialectical argument.

Core Idea
Induction in jadal corrects the conclusion, corrects necessary premises, and reinforces acceptance.

Induction (istiqra') serves multiple roles in dialectical reasoning beyond simple enumeration. The passage identifies three distinct uses: to directly correct the required conclusion, to correct a necessary premise within that conclusion, and to reinforce the argument by making premises more acceptable. This lesson explores each use with examples from Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
List the three uses of induction
Extract the three uses from the Arabic: (1) تصحيح المطلوب نفسه (correcting the conclusion), (2) تصحيح المقدمة الضرورية في المطلوب (correcting a necessary premise), (3) الاستظهار (reinforcement).
These are distinct purposes, not mutually exclusive.
2
Differentiate each use
The first directly establishes the desired claim; the second establishes a key supporting premise; the third does not prove but makes premises more palatable.
Only the first two aim at proof; the third is rhetorical.
3
Relate to necessary premises
The author notes that some inductive arguments have necessary premises, achieving certainty. But in jadal, induction is often used for persuasion, not demonstration.
Necessary induction yields yaqin (certainty); non-necessary yields zann (opinion).
4
Apply to a theological example
Consider a jadal argument about the eternality of the world: induction could be used to correct the conclusion (by listing temporary entities), or to correct a premise (e.g., 'everything that begins has a cause'), or for reinforcement (citing many examples of created things).
Identify which use is most appropriate in context.

ولا يحتاج فيه إلى أن يخفي قربها من النتيجة الخاصة التي للسائل الجدلي لأنه يجتهد في إخفاء ما يلزم من تلك المقدمات؛ لئلا يتعشر في تسليمها والقضاء بالشيء يستعلمها الجدلي إما ضرورية وإما غير ضرورية والضرورة داخلة في نفس القول الموجب للمطلوب قياسا كان أو استقراء وغير الضروري إنما يورد لأغراض أربعة؛ وهي الاستظهار في الاستقراء، والقسمة والاستظهار في تفهيم القول، والاجتهاد في إخفاء النتيجة، والتكلُّف في إيضاح القول. ثم الاستقراء إنما يستعمل في الجدل على وجوه ثلاثة؛ أحدها في أن يصحح منه المطلوب نفسه. والثاني لأن تصحح به المقدمة الضرورية في المطلوب. والثالث للاستظهار فهو أن تكون المقدمات غير بعيدة عن التسليم بل يسلمها الخصم إذا ظهر من أحوالها أنها محمودة أو مسلمة، والاستقراء كما مر من قبل بوجوه؛ منها ما تكون مقدماته ضرورية فيحصل منه الغرض قطعا. والقسمة قد تورد على مقتضى الضرورة، وقد تورد لتحسين الكلام فيما لا يحتاج إليه فيقال مثلا إن العلم قد يكون أشرف من علم آخر لقوة برهانية، وإما لشرف موضوعه، وإما لكذا وكذا حيث يكون النافع مثلا أن يبين أن العلم شريف. ثم يتعداه إلى وجوه شرفه من غير حاجة إليه فأحد الوجوه الأربعة أن تورد المقدمات للاستقراء الاستظهار دون الضروري، والقسمة التي لا ضرورة إليها. والوجه الثاني أن يورد لبسط القول؛ والتوسع فيه إما لمدح كلامه واستجادة مذهبه، وإما للتعجب ممن ينكر مأخذه، وإما بالأمثلة والاستشهادات بأقاول الناس، وإما بالتصرف في تبديل العبارات للبيان والفصاحة بأن يعبر عن قصة واحدة بعبارات مختلفة كما هو من عادة فصحاء العرب، وإما باستعطاف المخاطب واستمالته إلى التسليم تارة، وتارة بمدحه ونسبته إلى الإنصاف وليس لقائل أن يقول إن بعض هذه المواضع وفسطائية وخطابية فإن تلك المواضع ربَّما احتيج إليها في الجدل؛ وذلك عند المكارة والامتناع عن تسليم ما يكون مشهورًا، والثالث خلط ما يحتاج إليه بما لا يحتاج إليه إخفاء النتيجة وما يقرب منها الحجج والرابع لا يكون للضرورة ولا للحيلة بل للإيضاح والكشف بتبديل الألفاظ وتكرير الأمثلة. فالوجه الثاني يقصد فيه التصديق ؛ وهذا الوجه يقصد فيه تصور (۱)، وقد يستعان فيه بألفاظ الشعراء والأمثال المعروفة. وأما الوصية التي هي في الوجه الأول فبأن لا تذكر المقدمة الضرورية في أول الأمر، ولا يطلب تسليمها صراحًا، فبما لم يسلم بل يجب أن ينتقل عنها إلى تسليم ما هو أعم منها إلى عقد القياس أو الاستقراء؛ وبالجملة إلى عقد ما يقابل وضعه. وأما الوصية في الوجه الذي هو لإخفاء النتيجة فبأن ينظر إن كانت النتيجة تبين قياسات بعيدة؛ فيجب أن يبتدئ بأبعد المقدمات عن النتيجة وتسليمها. فينتقل عنها إلى القريبة فيحسن أن لا يسأل عنها على الترتيب القياسي بل يسأل عن الأول في الترتيب الطبيعي ثانيا، وعن الثاني أولا لئلا يسمع الحد الأوسط بل يسمع تارة في طرف، وتارة في طرف آخر. وقد يحلل بينهما فاصل. وكذلك يجب أن يحلل بين مقدمة ومقدمة بما هو الفاصل بينهما إخفاء للأقرب فإن فيه إخفاء النتيجة. وبالجملة فله أن يتكلف فيما يكون نافعا لإخفاء النتيجة؛ فإن ظهور النتيجة بعد تسليم المقدمات مشتمل على المصالح وكيف ولا يمكن ردها حينئذ؛ وقد كان تسليمها في ضمن تسليم المقدّمات : فإذا أقرَّ بها فكأنه أقرّ بالنتيجة؛ والإنكار بعد الإقرار لا يعتبر لا عقلا ولا شرعًا ولا عادة. (۱) النسخة التصوير وما أثبتناه هو الصواب كما هو الظاهر من السياق.
And it is not necessary in it to conceal the closeness of those premises to the specific conclusion of the dialectical questioner, because he strives to conceal what follows from those premises, lest the opponent become difficult in accepting them. The dialectician uses each thing either as necessary or as non-necessary. The necessary is inherent in the statement that necessitates the required conclusion, whether it be a syllogism or induction. The non-necessary is introduced only for four purposes: reinforcement in induction, division, reinforcement in making the statement understood, striving to conceal the conclusion, and taking trouble to clarify the statement. Then induction is used in dialectic in three ways: first, to correct the required conclusion itself; second, to correct the necessary premise in the required conclusion; third, for reinforcement, so that the premises are not far from acceptance but the opponent accepts them when it appears from their circumstances that they are praiseworthy or accepted. Induction, as previously mentioned, has ways: among them are those whose premises are necessary, so the purpose is achieved definitively. Division may be introduced as required by necessity, or it may be introduced for beautifying speech where it is not needed, for example saying: knowledge may be nobler than another knowledge because of its demonstrative strength, or because of the nobility of its subject, or because of such-and-such, where the benefit is to show that knowledge is noble. Then he goes beyond to its aspects of nobility without need for it. So one of the four purposes is to introduce premises for reinforcement in induction without the necessary, and division without need. The second purpose is to introduce expansion of speech; its extension is either for praising one's speech and considering one's madhhab good, or for expressing wonder at one who denies one's source, or through examples and testimonies from people's sayings, or through manipulation in changing expressions for clarity and eloquence, so that one expresses a single story with different expressions as is the custom of the eloquent Arabs, or through winning over the addressee and inclining him to acceptance sometimes, and sometimes by praising him and attributing fairness to him. And no one can say that some of these places are sophistical and rhetorical, for those places might be needed in dialectic, namely when there is stubbornness and refusal to accept what is well-known. The third is mixing what is needed with what is not needed to conceal the conclusion and what is close to it of proofs. The fourth is not for necessity nor for trickery, but for clarification and uncovering by changing expressions and repeating examples. The second purpose intends assent; this purpose intends conception (1), and it may be assisted by the words of poets and well-known proverbs. As for the advice concerning the first purpose: it is that one should not mention the necessary premise at the beginning, nor demand its acceptance explicitly, as long as it has not been accepted, but one must move from it to the acceptance of what is more general than it, to the formation of the syllogism or induction; in short, to the formation of what corresponds to its position. And as for the advice concerning the purpose that is to conceal the conclusion: it is that one should consider: if the conclusion is made clear by distant syllogisms, then one must begin with the most distant premises from the conclusion and have them accepted. Then one moves from them to the closer ones. It is good not to ask about them in the syllogistic order, but rather ask about the first in the natural order second, and about the second first, so that the middle term is not heard, but is heard sometimes on one side and sometimes on another. And one may interpose a separator between them. Likewise, one must interpose between premise and premise what is a separator between them to conceal the closer, for in that is concealment of the conclusion. In short, one may undertake what is beneficial for concealing the conclusion, for the appearance of the conclusion after acceptance of the premises contains benefits, and how can it be rejected then? And its acceptance was implied in the acceptance of the premises. So if he acknowledges them, it is as if he acknowledged the conclusion. And denial after acknowledgment is not considered rationally, legally, or customarily. (1) The copy is photographic and what we have established is correct as is apparent from the context.

Strategic Advice for Concealing the Conclusion
Author gives practical advice on ordering premises and hiding the conclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the strategies for concealing the conclusion in a dialectical debate and explain why they are effective.

Core Idea
Concealing the conclusion until premises are accepted forces the opponent to commit logically.

One of the most subtle skills in jadal is hiding the intended conclusion from the opponent until the premises are already conceded. The author provides detailed advice: start with the most distant premises, reverse the natural order, and interpose separators to obscure the middle term. This lesson unpacks these tactics and their rationale.

Guided Path
1
Understand the goal of concealment
The aim is to prevent the opponent from rejecting the conclusion by making it obvious. Once premises are accepted, the conclusion follows inevitably.
Denial after acknowledgment is invalid rationally, legally, and customarily.
2
Learn the first strategy for necessary premises
Do not mention the necessary premise first; move from general to specific, getting acceptance for broader claims before the crucial premise.
This gets the opponent to concede without realizing the implication.
3
Learn the second strategy for hiding the conclusion
If the conclusion requires distant syllogisms, start with the farthest premise. Reverse the natural order of questioning so the middle term is not perceived.
Interpose separators between premises to further obscure the link.
4
Apply the strategies to a sample debate
Take a theological claim like 'The world is created.' Construct a dialogue using these strategies to get premises accepted before revealing the conclusion.
Use both strategies: avoid direct order and begin with distant premises.

And it is not necessary in it to conceal the closeness of those premises to the specific conclusion of the dialectical questioner, because he strives to conceal what follows from those premises, lest the opponent become difficult in accepting them. The dialectician uses each thing either as necessary or as non-necessary. The necessary is inherent in the statement that necessitates the required conclusion, whether it be a syllogism or induction. Then he goes beyond to its aspects of nobility without need for it. So one of the four purposes is to introduce premises for reinforcement in induction without the necessary, and division without need. The second purpose is to introduce expansion of speech; its extension is either for praising one's speech and considering one's madhhab good, or for expressing wonder at one who denies one's source, or through examples and testimonies from people's sayings, or through manipulation in changing expressions for clarity and eloquence, so that one expresses a single story with different expressions as is the custom of the eloquent Arabs, or through winning over the addressee and inclining him to acceptance sometimes, and sometimes by praising him and attributing fairness to him. And no one can say that some of these places are sophistical and rhetorical, for those places might be needed in dialectic, namely when there is stubbornness and refusal to accept what is well-known. The third is mixing what is needed with what is not needed to conceal the conclusion and what is close to it of proofs. The fourth is not for necessity nor for trickery, but for clarification and uncovering by changing expressions and repeating examples. The second purpose intends assent; this purpose intends conception (1), and it may be assisted by the words of poets and well-known proverbs. As for the advice concerning the first purpose: it is that one should not mention the necessary premise at the beginning, nor demand its acceptance explicitly, as long as it has not been accepted, but one must move from it to the acceptance of what is more general than it, to the formation of the syllogism or induction; in short, to the formation of what corresponds to its position. And as for the advice concerning the purpose that is to conceal the conclusion: it is that one should consider: if the conclusion is made clear by distant syllogisms, then one must begin with the most distant premises from the conclusion and have them accepted. Then one moves from them to the closer ones. It is good not to ask about them in the syllogistic order, but rather ask about the first in the natural order second, and about the second first, so that the middle term is not heard, but is heard sometimes on one side and sometimes on another. And one may interpose a separator between them. Likewise, one must interpose between premise and premise what is a separator between them to conceal the closer, for in that is concealment of the conclusion. In short, one may undertake what is beneficial for concealing the conclusion, for the appearance of the conclusion after acceptance of the premises contains benefits, and how can it be rejected then? And its acceptance was implied in the acceptance of the premises. So if he acknowledges them, it is as if he acknowledged the conclusion. And denial after acknowledgment is not considered rationally, legally, or customarily. (1) The copy is photographic and what we have established is correct as is apparent from the context.
Source pages p. 572

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition on the art of dialectical reasoning (jadal), explaining how a dialectician uses premises and other tools to achieve his goals. The author begins by stating that the dialectician does not need to hide the closeness of premises to the conclusion; rather, he strives to conceal what follows from those premises to avoid the opponent's reluctance to accept them. The premises used are either necessary (i.e., they directly necessitate the required conclusion) or non-necessary. Necessary premises are inherent in the argument itself. Non-necessary premises are introduced for four purposes: (1) reinforcement in induction, (2) division, (3) reinforcement in making the statement understood, (4) striving to hide the conclusion, and (5) taking trouble to clarify the statement. The author then focuses on induction, explaining its three uses in jadal: to correct the desired conclusion itself, to correct a necessary premise in the desired conclusion, and for reinforcement—to make premises more acceptable by appearing praiseworthy. He notes that some inductive arguments have necessary premises, achieving certainty. Division can be used out of necessity or for beautifying speech without need. An example is given: showing that knowledge is nobler due to demonstrative strength or subject nobility, even if not needed. The second purpose of expansion includes praising one's speech, expressing wonder, using examples, varying expressions for eloquence, and flattering the opponent. The author defends these rhetorical devices, saying they are not sophistical but needed in cases of stubbornness. The third purpose is mixing needed and unneeded premises to hide the conclusion. The fourth is pure clarification without trickery. The second purpose aims at tasdiq (assent), while the first aims at tasawwur (conception), and may use poetic words. The author then gives practical advice (wasiyya) for the first purpose: do not mention the necessary premise first; instead, get acceptance for more general premises first. For the purpose of hiding the conclusion, if the conclusion requires distant syllogisms, start with the farthest premises, then move closer. Do not ask in logical order but reverse the natural order to hide the middle term. Interpose separators between premises to further conceal. The author concludes that once premises are accepted, the conclusion is implied, and denial after acknowledgment is invalid rationally, legally, and customarily.

Key Terms
وصية
مقدمة ضرورية
نتيجة
الحد الأوسط
فاصل

Key Takeaway
The art of jadal includes orchestrating the sequence of premises so that the conclusion becomes inevitable without the opponent realizing it.

The Four Aspects of Argumentation in Dialectic
Author enumerates four faces of dialectical argumentation with their purposes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish the four aspects of dialectical argumentation described in the passage and apply them to real debates in Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
Dialectic uses four aspects: praise, assent, concealment, and clarification – each serves a strategic purpose.

In this passage, the author outlines four distinct aspects (وجوه) of argumentation used in jadal (dialectic). These aspects are not merely rhetorical tricks; they are necessary tools for navigating debates, especially against obstinate opponents. By understanding each aspect's purpose, you will be better equipped to analyze and construct dialectical arguments in Islamic scholarly discourse.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first aspect: praise as an opening
The passage begins by mentioning 'praise and attributing to fairness'. Although not numbered, this is the first aspect: using praise to establish goodwill and frame the discussion positively.
This is common in Islamic scholarly works (e.g., praising the truthfulness of the opponent’s school before criticism).
2
Distinguish the second aspect: seeking assent (tasdiq)
The second aspect aims at 'tasdiq' – getting the opponent to assent to a proposition. The author notes that it may use poetic language and well-known proverbs to persuade.
Tasdiq is one of the two goals of argumentation (the other being tasawwur – conception).
3
Recognize the third aspect: concealment through mixing
The third aspect involves mixing needed and unneeded material to hide the conclusion and the premises leading to it. This is used when the opponent is stubborn and refuses to accept commonly known premises.
The author explicitly links this to 'obstinacy and refusal to accept what is well-known'.
4
Understand the fourth aspect: clarification through rephrasing
The fourth aspect is purely for clarification: changing words and repeating examples to make the argument clearer. It is not for trickery or necessity but for illumination.
This shows that dialectic is not solely about winning but also about teaching and clarifying.

Source Text

سوم المنظوم بمدحه ونسبته إلى الإنصاف وليس لقائل أن يقول إن بعض هذه المواضع وسفسطائية وخطابية فإن تلك المواضع ربَّما احتيج إليها في الجدل؛ وذلك عند المكابرة والامتناع عن تسليم ما يكون مشهورًا، والثالث خلط ما يحتاج إليه بما لا يحتاج إليه إخفاء النتيجة وما يقرب منها الحجج والرابع لا يكون للضرورة ولا للحيلة بل للإيضاح والكشف بتبديل الألفاظ وتكرير الأمثلة. فالوجه الثاني يقصد فيه التصديق؛ وهذا الوجه يقصد فيه التصور، وقد يستعان فيه بألفاظ الشعراء والأمثال المعروفة.
He began the poem with his praise and attributing it to fairness, and no one can say that some of these topics are sophistical and rhetorical, for these topics are sometimes needed in dialectic: that is in cases of obstinacy and refusal to accept what is well-known. The third [aspect] is mixing what is needed with what is not needed to hide the conclusion and the premises close to it. The fourth is not for necessity nor for trickery, but for clarification and revelation by changing words and repeating examples. So the second aspect aims at assent; this aspect aims at conception, and it may be aided by the words of poets and well-known proverbs.

Commentary
This passage defines the four aspects (وجه) of argumentation in dialectical contexts. The author begins by noting that one might object that some of these topics are sophistical or rhetorical, but he defends their necessity in dialectic, especially against obstinate opponents who refuse to accept commonly known premises. He then lists the aspects: the first (implied) is praise and attributing fairness; the second aims at assent (تصديق); the third involves mixing needed and unneeded material to hide the conclusion; the fourth is purely for clarification by rephrasing and repeating examples. The second aspect specifically aims at assent, while another (possibly the third) aims at conception (تصور) and may use poetic language and proverbs. This classification helps the dialectician choose the appropriate method.

Hiding the Conclusion in Dialectical Argument
Author gives techniques for concealing the conclusion by ordering premises strategically.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the dialectical techniques of hiding the conclusion and ordering premises as described by the author.

Core Idea
Strategic concealment of the conclusion forces the opponent to admit premises that logically entail it, making denial impossible.

How do you persuade an opponent who might reject your conclusion? The classical dialecticians developed sophisticated strategies for ordering premises to conceal the logical structure. In this passage, the author offers two key pieces of advice: avoid starting with necessary premises, and scramble the order of premises to hide the middle term. Let's explore these techniques step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two pieces of advice
The passage gives advice for two aspects: the first about not starting with a necessary premise, the second about hiding the conclusion. Locate these in the Arabic.
The first advice is 'أن لا تذكر المقدمة الضرورية في أول الأمر' (do not mention the necessary premise at the beginning). The second is about hiding the conclusion.
2
Analyze the technique for hiding the conclusion
Notice that the author recommends beginning with the farthest premises and then moving closer, but not in logical order. He stresses scrambling the order so the middle term is not heard clearly.
The author says to ask about the first in natural order second, and vice versa, 'لئلا يسمع الحد الأوسط' (so that the middle term is not heard).
3
Understand the use of separators
The author suggests inserting a filler or separator (فاصل) between premises to further distract from the logical connection.
This is described as 'يحلل بينهما فاصل' (a separator may be placed between them).
4
Recognize the rationale
The goal is to make the conclusion appear only after the premises are accepted, so the opponent cannot deny it. The author argues that denial after admission is invalid rationally, legally, and customarily.
The conclusion being 'في ضمن تسليم المقدمات' (within the acceptance of the premises) means once premises are accepted, the conclusion follows inevitably.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الوصية التي هي في الوجه الأول فبأن لا تذكر المقدمة الضرورية في أول الأمر، ولا يطلب تسليمها صراحًا، فبما لم يسلم بل يجب أن ينتقل عنها إلى تسليم ما هو أعم منها إلى عقد القياس أو الاستقراء؛ وبالجملة إلى عقد ما يقابل وضعه. وأما الوصية في الوجه الذي هو لإخفاء النتيجة فبأن ينظر إن كانت النتيجة تبين قياسات بعيدة؛ فيجب أن يبتدئ بأبعد المقدمات عن النتيجة وتسليمها. فينتقل عنها إلى القريبة فيحسن أن لا يسأل عنها على الترتيب القياسي بل يسأل عن الأول في الترتيب الطبيعي ثانيا، وعن الثاني أولا لئلا يسمع الحد الأوسط بل يسمع تارة في طرف، وتارة في طرف آخر. وقد يحلل بينهما فاصل. وكذلك يجب أن يحلل بين مقدمة ومقدمة بما هو الفاصل بينهما إخفاء للأقرب فإن فيه إخفاء النتيجة. وبالجملة فله أن يتكلف فيما يكون نافعا لإخفاء النتيجة؛ فإن ظهور النتيجة بعد تسليم المقدمات مشتمل على المصالح وكيف ولا يمكن ردها حينئذ؛ وقد كان تسليمها في ضمن تسليم المقدّمات : فإذا أقرَّ بها فكأنه أقرّ بالنتيجة؛ والإنكار بعد الإقرار لا يعتبر لا عقلا ولا شرعا ولا عادة.
As for the advice concerning the first aspect: that you do not mention the necessary premise at the beginning, nor demand its acceptance explicitly, since it is not accepted; rather, you must move from it to accepting what is more general, then to forming the syllogism or induction; in summary, to forming what corresponds to its position. As for the advice concerning the aspect that aims to hide the conclusion: that he consider if the conclusion is clear from distant syllogisms; then he must begin with the premises farthest from the conclusion and get them accepted. Then he moves from them to the closer ones. It is good not to ask about them in syllogistic order, but to ask about the first in natural order second, and about the second first, so that the middle term is not heard; rather, it is heard once at one end and another time at the other end. A separator may be placed between them. Likewise, he should place a separator between one premise and another as a divider between them to hide the closer, for in that is hiding the conclusion. In summary, he may undertake what is beneficial for hiding the conclusion; for the appearance of the conclusion after the acceptance of the premises contains benefits, and how can it be refuted then? And its acceptance was within the acceptance of the premises: so if he admits them, it is as if he admitted the conclusion; and denial after admission is not considered rationally, legally, or customarily.
Source pages p. 573

Commentary
This passage provides two pieces of advice for the dialectician. The first: do not state a necessary premise (مقدمة ضرورية) outright at the beginning if it is not accepted; instead, start with a more general premise that the opponent will accept, then move to the specific. The second advice, specifically for hiding the conclusion, involves a strategic ordering: if the conclusion is to be proven by distant syllogisms, start with the farthest premises. Do not ask for premises in logical order (ترتيب قياسي); instead, scramble the order so the middle term (حد أوسط) is not obvious. Insert fillers (فاصل) between premises to distract. The goal is to make the conclusion appear only after all premises are accepted, so the opponent cannot easily deny it, as denial after admission is invalid rationally, legally, and customarily.

Key Terms

حد أوسط
فاصل

Key Takeaway
Strategic ordering and concealment of the middle term are key dialectical techniques that prevent premature rejection and secure the conclusion's acceptance.

Choosing Qiyas or Istiqra' in Dialectic and Clarifying Ambiguous Terms
Author advises when to use syllogism vs. induction and how to clarify ambiguous terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to choose between syllogism and induction based on the opponent's level and clarify ambiguous terms to ensure effective dialectical reasoning.

Core Idea
Dialectic uses common premises: adapt your method to the audience and define ambiguous terms.

In a dialectical debate, the questioner must choose the right argumentative method for the audience. This passage gives practical advice: use syllogism with skilled dialecticians and induction with common people. It also highlights a key obstacle—ambiguous terms without a name—and provides a solution: define the term before inducing. Understanding this helps you manage dialectical exchanges effectively, especially in Islamic theological and legal debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the audience
Determine whether your opponent is a skilled dialectician or a common person. The author advises using syllogism (qiyas) with the former and induction (istiqra') with the latter.
Syllogism yields necessary conclusions; induction relies on commonly accepted premises.
2
Check for ambiguous terms
If the key meaning (ma'na mutashabih) lacks a specific name, the transition from induction to universal premise becomes difficult. The speech confuses the questioner.
Ambiguous terms are those that are not clearly defined or named.
3
Clarify the ambiguous meaning
The questioner must delineate (rasm) that meaning and assign it a name. This provides a clear basis for induction.
Rasm here means a descriptive definition, not a full logical definition (hadd).
4
Perform induction and point out ambiguity
After naming and clarifying, the questioner induces (gathers particular instances) and points out where the ambiguity occurred. The respondent must then either accept the premise or bring something contradictory; refusal is unjust.
This is a procedural rule in dialectic: the respondent cannot simply reject without counter-argument.
5
Recognize the nature of dialectical premises
Dialectic does not require necessary premises; it works with what is well-known (mashhur). A premise becomes dialectical through wide acceptance, not inherent necessity.
This contrasts with demonstrative syllogism which demands certain premises.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم من الوصايا المخصوصة بالسائل ما يكون في أحوال القياس والاستقراء؛ وينبغي أن يستعمل مع أصحاب الجدل القياس، ومع الذين هم أشبه بالعوام الاستقراء. وإذا لم يكن للمعنى المتشابه اسم صعب فيه الانتقال من الاستقراء إلى المقدِّمة الكلية فيتشوّش الكلام على السائل بل يجب أن يرسم ذلك المعنى ويوضع له اسم؛ فإذا استقرَّ السائل ودل على ما وقع التشابه كان امتناع المجيب عن التسليم ظلما بل عليه أن يأتي بما يناقضه أو يسلم، وهذا بحسب الجدل فقط لأن الاستقراء جدلي؛ وليس من شرط الجدل أن يكون ما يورد فيه من القول موجبًا للمطلوب بالضرورة بل بحسب المشهور. ولهذا ما كان كثير من المواضع الغير الواجبة يجوز أن تصير جدلية إذا قيلت واشتهرت، ويجوز أن لا تكون جدلية إذا لم يشتهر
Then among the advices specific to the questioner are those concerning the conditions of syllogism and induction. He should use syllogism with the practitioners of dialectic, and with those who are like common people, induction. If the ambiguous meaning does not have a name, the transition from induction to the universal premise becomes difficult, so the speech confuses the questioner. Rather, he must delineate that meaning and assign it a name. Then when the questioner induces and points out where the ambiguity occurred, the respondent's refusal to accept is unjust. Rather, he must bring something that contradicts it or accept. This is only according to dialectic because induction is dialectical; and it is not a condition of dialectic that the statement presented necessarily produce the required result, but rather according to what is well-known. For this reason, many non-necessary topics become dialectical if they are stated and become well-known, and they may not be dialectical if they are not well-known.
Source pages p. 574

Commentary
This passage gives advice to the questioner (السائل) in a dialectical debate. He should choose the method based on the opponent: use syllogism (قياس) with skilled dialecticians and induction (استقراء) with common people. If an ambiguous meaning (معنى متشابه) lacks a specific name, moving from induction to a universal premise becomes difficult and confuses the questioner. To solve this, the questioner should define the meaning and give it a name. Once he induces and shows the ambiguity, the respondent must either contradict or accept; refusal is unjust. The author emphasizes that induction is dialectical (جدلي) by nature, and dialectic does not require necessary proof but only commonly accepted premises. Thus, a statement can become dialectical if it gains wide acceptance (اشتهار), regardless of its inherent necessity.

Key Terms
استقراء
معنى متشابه
مشهور

Key Takeaway
Effective dialectic requires adapting your method to the opponent and resolving ambiguities by defining terms before inducing.
