Choosing Qiyas or Istiqra' Based on Audience
Advice on audience-appropriate argument method

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to decide whether to use qiyas or istiqra' in a dialectical debate based on the audience's expertise.

Core Idea
Match your argument type to your opponent's level.

In dialectical debate, the questioner must select the appropriate method. The author advises using qiyas (syllogism/analogy) with trained dialecticians, and istiqra' (induction) with laymen. This lesson examines the strategic thinking behind this advice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two methods
Recognize qiyas as a structured logical argument moving from premises to conclusion, and istiqra' as reasoning from specific examples to a general principle.
Qiyas requires abstract reasoning; istiqra' relies on concrete instances.
2
Analyze the audience
Dialecticians are trained to follow intricate logical chains, so qiyas is effective. Laymen struggle with abstraction but grasp concrete examples, so istiqra' works better.
3
Apply the advice
Given a debate scenario with a specific opponent background, choose the method and justify your choice.
Consider real debates in kalam or fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم من الوصايا المخصوصة بالسائل ما يكون في أحوال القياس والاستقراء؛ وينبغي أن يستعمل مع أصحاب الجدل القياس، ومع الذين هم أشبه بالعوام الاستقراء. وإذا لم يكن للمعنى المتشابه اسم صعب فيه الانتقال من الاستقراء إلى المقدِّمة الكلية فيتشوّش الكلام على السائل بل يجب أن يرسم ذلك المعنى ويوضع له اسم؛ فإذا استقرَّ السائل ودل على ما وقع التشابه كان امتناع المجيب عن التسليم ظلما بل عليه أن يأتي بما يناقضه أو يسلم، وهذا بحسب الجدل فقط لأن الاستقراء جدلي؛ وليس من شرط الجدل أن يكون ما يورد فيه من القول موجبًا للمطلوب بالضرورة بل بحسب المشهور. ولهذا ما كان كثير من المواضع الغير الواجبة يجوز أن تصير جدلية إذا قيلت واشتهرت، ويجوز أن لا تكون جدلية إذا لم يشتهر ؛ وذلك مثل قولهم لو جاز كذا لجاز كذا إذا كان نظيرا له. فإن هذا غير واجب في نفس الأمر فإنه ليس إذا جاز في شيء حكم جاز في نظيره، وكذلك قولهم إن لزمني هذا فقد يلزمك أيضًا فإن هذا غير واجب، ولا إذا لزم الآخر كما يلزم الأول يجب أن يترك بحكم الأول. ثمَّ إنه وإن لزم في هذه الصور من هذا ومن ذلك فالكلام على هذا النسق بمعزل عن التوجه، وكذلك المعارضة للحجة بحجة أخرى؛ وليس في ذلك خروج عن عمدة الحجة التي أوردت ؛ إذ المعارضة في مقام المناقضة بطريق غير مسموعة لما مر أن البحث بطريق المناقضة منصب شخص، وبطريق المعارضة منصب شيخ آخر ، فهذا في الجملة غصب المنصب. وأما قولهم بأنها إذا اشتهرت صارت طرفًا جدلية فذلك ضعيف؛ إذ الباطل لا يصير من الطرق الجدلية. وأما المناقضة باشتراك الاسم فهي باطلة أيضًا كما إذا قيل كل حيوان حساس فيقال لا يكون كذلك فإن المصوّر حيوان؛ وليس بحساس. وبالجملة فالحق فيها وفي المعارضة مفتقر إلى تحرير المبحث فقيل ذلك لا يكون حقا لا هذا ولا ذلك، ولنقتصر على هذا القدر فإن الباقي يعرف بالتأمل فإن رعايته مراتب الكلام تحصيلا للغرض. والتفادي عما يحل بالغرض من جملة الأمور العقلية؛ فمراتب الناس فيها على مراتب عقولهم.
Then among the special advice for the questioner is what concerns the conditions of analogy and induction: it is fitting to use analogy with the people of dialectic, and induction with those who are more like the common folk. If the similar meaning has no name, the transition from induction to the universal premise becomes difficult, and speech becomes confused for the questioner. Rather, he must delineate that meaning and assign a name to it. When the questioner has induced and indicated where the similarity occurred, the respondent's refusal to concede is injustice; rather, he must either bring what contradicts it or concede. This is only according to dialectic, because induction is dialectical. It is not a condition of dialectic that the statement adduced in it necessarily necessitates the required conclusion, but rather according to the well-known. For this reason, many places that are not necessary may become dialectical if they are expressed and become well-known, and they may not be dialectical if they are not well-known. That is like their saying: 'If this is permissible, then that is permissible, if it is analogous to it.' For this is not necessary in itself, because it is not the case that if a judgment is permissible for one thing, it is permissible for its analogue. Similarly, their saying: 'If this is incumbent on me, then it may also be incumbent on you' is not necessary, nor if it is incumbent on the other as it is incumbent on the first, must it be left by the judgment of the first. Moreover, even if it is incumbent in these forms from this and that, speech in this pattern is far from being directed. Similarly, countering an argument with another argument does not depart from the pillar of the argument adduced, because counter-argument in the position of contradiction is impermissible by way, since research by way of contradiction pertains to one person, and by way of counter-argument to another person. Thus, in summary, this is usurpation of the position. As for their saying that if they become well-known, they become dialectical terms, that is weak, for falsehood does not become among the dialectical ways. As for contradiction by synonymy, it is also false, as when it is said: 'Every animal is sentient' and he says: 'It is not so, for the image is an animal and not sentient.' In summary, the truth regarding it and regarding counter-argument requires the investigation of the topic; so it is said that this is not true, neither this nor that. Let us suffice with this amount, for the rest is known by reflection, because observing the levels of speech in achieving the purpose and avoiding what harms the purpose is among the intellectual matters; and the levels of people in it are according to their intellects.
Source pages p. 574

Commentary
This passage begins a section of advice specific to the questioner in dialectical debate. The author instructs that the questioner should use qiyas (syllogism/analogy) with skilled dialecticians, and istiqra' (induction) with common people. Induction is easier for the layman, but it has a problem: if the common meaning (the similitude) lacks a name, moving from induction to a universal premise becomes hard. The speaker must then define that meaning and give it a name. Once the questioner induces and shows the similarity, the respondent cannot refuse to concede without injustice; he must either contradict or concede. This is specific to dialectic because induction is a dialectical tool. Dialectic does not require necessary premises, only well-known (mashhur) ones. Therefore, non-necessary statements can become dialectical if widely accepted, like the example of arguing from analogy in permissibility. However, such arguments are not logically necessary. The author then criticizes certain faulty dialectical moves: using a counter-argument against the opponent's evidence, which is a usurpation of the role (since contradiction and counter-argument are roles of different participants), and claiming that false statements become dialectical if well-known (falsehood does not become a valid dialectical method). Contradiction based on equivocation (e.g., 'animal' includes both sentient beings and images) is also invalid. The author concludes that the truth in these matters requires careful investigation, and one should suffice with this amount, as further details are known through reflection, and people's levels in this correspond to their intellects.

Key Terms
السائل
القياس
الاستقراء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which method should be used with common people?
A) Qiyas
B) Istiqra'
C) Mu'arada
D) Baht

Key Takeaway
The choice between qiyas and istiqra' depends on the opponent's level of dialectical training.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You are a mutakallim debating whether the world is temporally originated. Your opponent is a philosopher. Would you use qiyas or istiqra'? Justify using the author's advice.

Naming the Similitude for Induction
The necessity of naming the similitude in induction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a name for the similitude is essential when using induction to derive a universal premise.

Core Idea
An unnamed similitude blocks the move from induction to a universal.

The author identifies a critical obstacle in induction: if the common meaning (similitude) among instances is not named, it becomes difficult to articulate the universal premise. He advises the questioner to delineate and name that meaning. This lesson explores this problem and its solution.

Guided Path
1
Understand the problem
Induction collects similar cases, but to form a universal premise, the common element must be explicitly named. Without a name, the universal is vague.
Example: from several specific rulings on drunkards, the common 'illa 'intoxication' must be named to form a universal rule.
2
Identify the need for definition
The questioner must 'delineate that meaning' by defining it clearly and assigning a term. This makes the universal premise usable in argument.
3
Apply to Islamic examples
In fiqh, jurists used istiqra' from specific cases to induce a general principle. They named the 'illa (e.g., 'riba' for unequal exchange). Consider how naming facilitates the universal.

Dialectical Premises: Well-Known (Mashhur) Not Necessary
Well-known premises in dialectic

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between necessary and well-known (mashhur) premises and identify when a statement qualifies as dialectical.

Core Idea
Dialectic runs on well-known opinions, not logical necessities.

The author clarifies that dialectical arguments do not require premises that are logically necessary; they operate on well-known (mashhur) statements widely accepted by the community. This allows many non-necessary claims to be used in debate, but falsehood cannot become dialectical simply by popularity. This lesson unpacks this distinction.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish necessary and well-known
Necessary truths are self-evident or proven by demonstration. Well-known opinions are accepted by a group. Dialectic relies on the latter.
Example: 'The world is created' is well-known among Muslims, not a priori necessary.
2
Identify the condition for dialectical status
A statement becomes dialectical if it is expressed and becomes well-known. The author later stresses that falsehood cannot gain dialectical validity no matter how widespread.
3
Apply to examples
Analyze the author's example: 'if this is permissible, then its analogue is permissible.' This is not logically necessary, but if widely accepted, it can be used dialectically. Critique this from the author's perspective.

The Error of Counter-Argument (Mu'arada) in Dialectic
Critique of counter-argument in dialectic

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the improper dialectical move of mu'arada (counter-argument) and explain why it is considered usurpation.

Core Idea
Counter-argument is usurping the respondent's role; proper response is contradiction or concession.

The author criticizes the practice of mu'arada, where the respondent counters the questioner's argument with a different argument instead of directly addressing the original. He argues that this constitutes a usurpation of the position, as contradiction (munaqada) and counter-argument are roles of different participants. This lesson examines this rule.

Guided Path
1
Define the roles in jadal
The questioner (sa'il) proposes arguments. The respondent (mujib) must either contradict the argument or concede. The contradiction must directly address the specific argument.
2
Understand why mu'arada is invalid
The author states that contradiction pertains to one person, and counter-argument to another person. Mu'arada mixes these roles, thus it is 'usurpation of the position'.
3
Apply the rule
Given a debate exchange, determine if a response is a valid contradiction or an invalid mu'arada.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم من الوصايا المخصوصة بالسائل ما يكون في أحوال القياس والاستقراء؛ وينبغي أن يستعمل مع أصحاب الجدل القياس، ومع الذين هم أشبه بالعوام الاستقراء. وإذا لم يكن للمعنى المتشابه اسم صعب فيه الانتقال من الاستقراء إلى المقدِّمة الكلية فيتشوّش الكلام على السائل بل يجب أن يرسم ذلك المعنى ويوضع له اسم؛ فإذا استقرَّ السائل ودل على ما وقع التشابه كان امتناع المجيب عن التسليم ظلما بل عليه أن يأتي بما يناقضه أو يسلم، وهذا بحسب الجدل فقط لأن الاستقراء جدلي؛ وليس من شرط الجدل أن يكون ما يورد فيه من القول موجبًا للمطلوب بالضرورة بل بحسب المشهور. ولهذا ما كان كثير من المواضع الغير الواجبة يجوز أن تصير جدلية إذا قيلت واشتهرت، ويجوز أن لا تكون جدلية إذا لم يشتهر ؛ وذلك مثل قولهم لو جاز كذا لجاز كذا إذا كان نظيرا له. فإن هذا غير واجب في نفس الأمر فإنه ليس إذا جاز في شيء حكم جاز في نظيره، وكذلك قولهم إن لزمني هذا فقد يلزمك أيضًا فإن هذا غير واجب، ولا إذا لزم الآخر كما يلزم الأول يجب أن يترك بحكم الأول. ثمَّ إنه وإن لزم في هذه الصور من هذا ومن ذلك فالكلام على هذا النسق بمعزل عن التوجه، وكذلك المعارضة للحجة بحجة أخرى؛ وليس في ذلك خروج عن عمدة الحجة التي أوردت ؛ إذ المعارضة في مقام المناقضة بطريق غير مسموعة لما مر أن البحث بطريق المناقضة منصب شخص، وبطريق المعارضة منصب شيخ آخر ، فهذا في الجملة غصب المنصب. وأما قولهم بأنها إذا اشتهرت صارت طرفًا جدلية فذلك ضعيف؛ إذ الباطل لا يصير من الطرق الجدلية. وأما المناقضة باشتراك الاسم فهي باطلة أيضًا كما إذا قيل كل حيوان حساس فيقال لا يكون كذلك فإن المصوّر حيوان؛ وليس بحساس. وبالجملة فالحق فيها وفي المعارضة مفتقر إلى تحرير المبحث فقيل ذلك لا يكون حقا لا هذا ولا ذلك، ولنقتصر على هذا القدر فإن الباقي يعرف بالتأمل فإن رعايته مراتب الكلام تحصيلا للغرض. والتفادي عما يحل بالغرض من جملة الأمور العقلية؛ فمراتب الناس فيها على مراتب عقولهم.
Then among the special advice for the questioner is what concerns the conditions of analogy and induction: it is fitting to use analogy with the people of dialectic, and induction with those who are more like the common folk. If the similar meaning has no name, the transition from induction to the universal premise becomes difficult, and speech becomes confused for the questioner. Rather, he must delineate that meaning and assign a name to it. When the questioner has induced and indicated where the similarity occurred, the respondent's refusal to concede is injustice; rather, he must either bring what contradicts it or concede. This is only according to dialectic, because induction is dialectical. It is not a condition of dialectic that the statement adduced in it necessarily necessitates the required conclusion, but rather according to the well-known. For this reason, many places that are not necessary may become dialectical if they are expressed and become well-known, and they may not be dialectical if they are not well-known. That is like their saying: 'If this is permissible, then that is permissible, if it is analogous to it.' For this is not necessary in itself, because it is not the case that if a judgment is permissible for one thing, it is permissible for its analogue. Similarly, their saying: 'If this is incumbent on me, then it may also be incumbent on you' is not necessary, nor if it is incumbent on the other as it is incumbent on the first, must it be left by the judgment of the first. Moreover, even if it is incumbent in these forms from this and that, speech in this pattern is far from being directed. Similarly, countering an argument with another argument does not depart from the pillar of the argument adduced, because counter-argument in the position of contradiction is impermissible by way, since research by way of contradiction pertains to one person, and by way of counter-argument to another person. Thus, in summary, this is usurpation of the position. As for their saying that if they become well-known, they become dialectical terms, that is weak, for falsehood does not become among the dialectical ways. As for contradiction by synonymy, it is also false, as when it is said: 'Every animal is sentient' and he says: 'It is not so, for the image is an animal and not sentient.' In summary, the truth regarding it and regarding counter-argument requires the investigation of the topic; so it is said that this is not true, neither this nor that. Let us suffice with this amount, for the rest is known by reflection, because observing the levels of speech in achieving the purpose and avoiding what harms the purpose is among the intellectual matters; and the levels of people in it are according to their intellects.
Source pages p. 574

Commentary
This passage begins a section of advice specific to the questioner in dialectical debate. The author instructs that the questioner should use qiyas (syllogism/analogy) with skilled dialecticians, and istiqra' (induction) with common people. Induction is easier for the layman, but it has a problem: if the common meaning (the similitude) lacks a name, moving from induction to a universal premise becomes hard. The speaker must then define that meaning and give it a name. Once the questioner induces and shows the similarity, the respondent cannot refuse to concede without injustice; he must either contradict or concede. This is specific to dialectic because induction is a dialectical tool. Dialectic does not require necessary premises, only well-known (mashhur) ones. Therefore, non-necessary statements can become dialectical if widely accepted, like the example of arguing from analogy in permissibility. However, such arguments are not logically necessary. The author then criticizes certain faulty dialectical moves: using a counter-argument against the opponent's evidence, which is a usurpation of the role (since contradiction and counter-argument are roles of different participants), and claiming that false statements become dialectical if well-known (falsehood does not become a valid dialectical method). Contradiction based on equivocation (e.g., 'animal' includes both sentient beings and images) is also invalid. The author concludes that the truth in these matters requires careful investigation, and one should suffice with this amount, as further details are known through reflection, and people's levels in this correspond to their intellects.

Key Terms
المعارضة
المناقضة
السائل
المجيب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is mu'arada (counter-argument) considered usurpation?
A) Because it repeats the same argument
B) Because it confuses the roles of contradiction and counter-argument
C) Because it is weaker than the original
D) Because it is not based on well-known premises

Key Takeaway
Mu'arada is an invalid dialectical move because it mixes the roles of the respondent and a third party; proper response is contradiction or concession.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In a debate about divine justice, the questioner argues: 'God's justice requires that He reward the obedient.' The respondent says: 'But God may do as He wills, and here is my argument that He is not constrained by justice.' Identify whether this is mu'arada. What should the respondent have done?

Invalid Moves: Falsehood and Equivocation
Invalid dialectical moves: falsehood and equivocation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify two invalid dialectical moves: claiming falsehood becomes dialectical when well-known, and contradiction by equivocation.

Core Idea
Falsehood never becomes dialectical, and equivocal terms make contradiction invalid.

The author refutes two erroneous practices in dialectic. First, some think that a false statement can become a valid dialectical premise if it becomes well-known. The author rejects this: falsehood does not become a dialectical way. Second, contradiction based on equivocation (ishtirak ism) is also invalid, as exemplified by the word 'animal' used differently. This lesson examines these fallacies.

Guided Path
1
Understand the falsehood claim
The author states that a false statement cannot become a legitimate dialectical premise simply by being widely known. Dialectic requires acceptability, but not falsehood.
Example: a widely held but false belief like 'the earth is flat' is not a valid dialectical premise.
2
Identify equivocation
The example: 'Every animal is sentient' is countered by 'the picture is an animal but not sentient'. This fails because 'animal' is equivocal (living being vs. representation). Recognize this fallacy.
Equivocation makes the contradiction invalid because the terms are not used consistently.
3
Apply the rules
Given examples of dialectical exchanges, determine if a premise is false but well-known, or if a contradiction uses equivocation.

The Two Aspects of Skilled Debate: Action and Power in Jadal
Author explains the two aspects (act and power) of a skilled questioner and respondent in dialectical debate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two aspects (action and power) of a skilled respondent in jadal and apply them in a simulated debate.

Core Idea
Skill in jadal is not just rejecting unfamous premises—it is strategically using commitment and distinction to defend your position.

In Islamic dialectics (jadal), being a skilled respondent (mujīb) requires more than just knowing the rules—it demands strategic thinking. The classical logicians distinguished between two types of skill: the act (fi'l) of conceding only famous premises, and the power (qudra) to manage commitments and introduce distinctions that protect your position without conceding the conclusion. This passage gives practical advice on both.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two aspects of respondent skill
In the Arabic, find where the author splits respondent skill into two: جهة الفعل (action) and جهة القدرة (power). Underline these terms.
2
Understand action: the gate of concession
The respondent's action is to refuse what is not mashhūr (famous) and concede what is. Why is this strategic? Because famous premises are hard for the opponent to attack.
3
Understand power of commitment: iltizām
The respondent may commit to a premise but must ensure it does not lead to contradiction. He can even show that the commitment strengthens his own position.
4
Understand power of disputation: jidāl
The respondent can introduce distinctions (furuq) and conditions (shurūṭ) that render a famous statement unfamous in a specific context, allowing him to refuse it.
5
Apply the distinction between goal-oriented and practice-based dialogues
In goal-oriented debate, the respondent seeks to avoid being forced; in practice-based, the aim is exploration. Why does the author caution against boastful repelling of commitment?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: في وصايا المجيب واعلم بأن كل واحد من المجيب والسائل قد يكون مجيدا، وقد لا يكون، والسائل إنما يكون مجيدًا من جهتين؛ إحديهما جهة الفعل، والأخرى جهة القدرة: إما من جهة الفعل فإنه يأتي بقياس من مقدمات هي أشهر، وإما من جهة القدرة فإن يكون ساق كلامه مساقه يضطر المجيب إلى أن لزمه مقابل الوضع عن مقدمات ليست بمحمودة والمجيب أيضًا إنما يكون مجيدا من جهة الفعل وهي أن يكون ممتنعا عن تسليم ما ليس بمشهور ومتوجها إلى تسليم ما هو مشهور، أو من جهة القدرة وذلك باعتبار الانقطاع والالتزام، أو باعتبار الجدل؛ وإنما يكون مجيدا باعتبار الالتزام أن يكون الالتزام لا يأتيه مناقضه بل إذا سيل عن طرفي النقيض ليتسلم عنه مقدمات القياس المسوق نحو مقابل وضعه. وله أن يتعرض لما يظهر منه أن الالتزام ليس يضعفه بل يضعف ما يحفظه. وأما باعتبار الجدال والمجاهدة فهو أن يكون مع تسليمه للمشهورات يمتنع أن يساق إلى النتيجة أو أن يكون قادرًا أن يورد من الفروق والشروط ما يجعل القول المشهور مطلقا بحيث إذا لم يراع ما أورده من الشرائط صار غير مشهور فيكون له أن لا يسلمها؛ وهذا كله في المفاوضات الموجهة بمعنى العلية وهي المحاورات الجهادية التي تكون قصارى سعي السائل أن يفعل، وقصارى سعي المجيب أن لا ينفعل. وأما المحاورات الارتياضية فينبغي أن لا يصرف من الهمم إلى الاختيال لدفع الالتزام بل إلى استكشاف المعاني لاستيضاح الرجحان؛ والرجوع إلى الأولى ارتياضًا بالمشاركة.
Chapter: On advice for the respondent. Know that each one of the respondent and the questioner may be skilled and may not be. The questioner is only skilled from two aspects: one from the aspect of action and the other from the aspect of power. As for the aspect of action, he brings a syllogism from premises that are more famous. As for the aspect of power, he conducts his speech in a manner that forces the respondent to necessarily accept the opposite of his position from premises that are not praiseworthy. The respondent also is only skilled from the aspect of action, namely that he refrains from conceding what is not famous and directs himself to conceding what is famous, or from the aspect of power, that being with respect to [causing] disconnection and commitment, or with respect to disputation. He is only skilled with respect to commitment in that the commitment does not befall him with a contradiction; rather, when asked about the two sides of a contradiction so that he concedes premises of the syllogism directed toward the opposite of his position, he may present what shows that the commitment does not weaken him but rather weakens what he preserves. As for with respect to disputation and struggle, he, while conceding famous statements, prevents himself from being led to the conclusion, or he is able to introduce distinctions and conditions that make the famous statement absolute such that if what he introduced of conditions is not observed it becomes unfamous, so he may not concede them. All this is in goal-oriented discussions in the sense of causality, which are struggle-based dialogues where the extreme effort of the questioner is to act and the extreme effort of the respondent is not to be acted upon. As for practice-based dialogues, one should not divert efforts to boasting for repelling commitment, but rather to exploring meanings for clarifying preponderance; and returning to the first as practice through participation.
Source pages p. 575

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical instruction on the two aspects of being a skilled participant in jadal: the aspect of action (fi'l) and the aspect of power (qudra). The author begins by noting that both questioner and respondent can be skilled or not. For the questioner, skill in action means bringing a syllogism from famous premises (mashhurat). Skill in power means phrasing the argument so that the respondent is forced to accept a premise that is not praiseworthy (ghayr mahmuda) to avoid a worse position. For the respondent, skill in action is refusing to concede unfamous premises and only conceding famous ones. Skill in power has two sub-aspects: (1) commitment (iltizam) — the respondent ensures that whatever he commits to does not lead to a contradiction; if pressed with a dilemma, he can show that the commitment actually weakens the opponent's position. (2) disputation (jidal/mujahada) — he concedes famous premises but prevents the opponent from reaching the desired conclusion, or introduces distinctions (furuq) and conditions (shurut) that render a superficially famous statement unfamous when conditions are not met. The author then distinguishes two types of dialogue: goal-oriented (ta'jihi) which is a struggle (jihadi) where the questioner aims to act (get a conclusion) and the respondent aims not to be acted upon; and practice-based (irtiyadi) where the aim is not to show off but to explore meanings and discover preponderance. This passage teaches the strategic thinking in jadal.

Key Terms
مجيب
سائل
مشهور
فعل
قدرة
التزام
جدل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the respondent's skill in the aspect of action (جهة الفعل)?
A) He brings a syllogism from famous premises.
B) He refrains from conceding what is not famous and directs himself to conceding what is famous.
C) He forces the questioner to accept an unfamous premise.
D) He introduces distinctions to avoid conceding.

Key Takeaway
A skilled respondent in jadal masters two dimensions: selectively conceding only famous premises (action) and strategically managing commitments and distinctions (power) to protect his position.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In a debate about whether the world is pre-eternal or originated, the questioner asks: 'Do you concede that anything that changes requires a cause?' As the respondent, apply the skill of action: would you concede this premise? Why or why not? Then, using the power of commitment, show how conceding it does not force you to accept that the world is originated.
?
Identify an example from kalam literature where a theologian uses a distinction (farq) to render a famous premise unfamous in a specific context. (Hint: Look at debates on qadā’ wa qadar.)

Categorizing Respondents and Managing Premises in Jadal
Author categorizes respondents by the type of position they defend and explains how to handle famous vs. shocking premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a respondent's position as famous, shocking, or neutral and determine which premises to concede or avoid in a dialectical debate.

Core Idea
In jadal, your right to concede premises depends on whether your position is famous, shocking, or neutral.

The art of dialectical debate (jadal) requires the respondent to strategically choose which premises to concede. This passage from al-Kabir categorizes respondents by the nature of the position they defend and provides rules for handling famous (mashhur) and shocking (shani') premises. Understanding these rules is essential for any mutakallim engaged in theological disputation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the respondent's position type
Look at the nature of the claim being defended: is it widely accepted (mashhur), shocking (shani'), or neither? The passage classifies every respondent into one of these three categories.
2
Apply the concession rule based on position
For a famous position, concede only famous premises; for a shocking position, avoid conceding famous premises; for a neutral position, both types are permissible.
3
Recognize the opposite relation
The opposite of a famous claim is usually shocking, and vice versa. This determines the questioner's desired conclusion (maṭlūb), which is often the opposite of the respondent's position.
4
Handle ambiguous premises
The respondent may pause and require clarification before conceding any premise that is unclear. He is not obligated to answer what he does not understand.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكل مجيب فإما أن يحفظ وضعًا مشهورًا أو شنيعا أو لا هذا ولا ذلك بل غير مما، وكل واحد من هذه الثلاثة وإن كان قد ينتج من غير جنسه فالأولى أن ينتج من جنسه. وبالجملة فالأكثر هو أن المشهور لا ينتج الشنيع، والشنيع ينتج المشهور. وإذا أنتج المشهور من الشنيع لم يكن ذلك طريقة جميلة لأنه إنما يجب إنتاج شيء عما هو أعرف فينبغي أن يكون إنتاج المشهور عما هو أشهر. وكل واحد من المشهور والشنيع لا يتوصل منه بسرعة إلى ما هو بعيد عن التفطن. وأيضًا فإن مقابل المشهور في الأكثر شنيع. اللهم إلا أن يكون المشهور ليس مطلقا بل بحسب قوم وقوم، ووقت ووقت؛ ومقابل الشنيع مشهور فإذا كان الوضع مشهورًا كان ما يقابله وهو مطلوب السائل شنيعا، وبالعكس. وإذا كان ليس بمشهور ولا بشنيع فمطلوب السائل كذلك فينبغي أن يخاط المجيب المتقلد نصرة وضع مشهور فيمتنع عن تسليم ما يكون شنيعا لأن نقيض وضعه وهو مطلوب السائل في معرض الشناعة؛ ولا يكاد ينتج عن المشهورات بل المشهورات أقرب إلى أن ينفع في نصرة وضعه من أن يناقض بها وضعه. وإن كان متكفلا نصرة شنيع لم يسلم المشهورات؛ ونقول إني بعد أن أسلم مثلا أن الخير والشر متقابلان فلستُ أسلم أن العلم والجهل متقابلان وإن كان متكفّلا نصرة وضع ليس بمشهور ولا بشنيع؛ فلتسلم المشهورات والشنيعات فإن المسافة منها إلى إيضاح الأمر الذي لا رضى فيه في المشهورات أن تكون مسافة بعيدة، والأصل أن لا يسلم أصلا ما يوهم أن يبطل وضعه الذي ينصره ولكن إنما يسلمه لئلا يظهر عدوله عن الإنصاف وللمجيب أن يتوقف في ابتداء الأمر عن تسليم ما يراد منه تسليمه إذا كان فيه غموض فلا يسلمه إلا بعد الاستكشاف فإنه لا يلزمه أن يجيب عما لا يفهمه؛ ولا عارَ عليه أن يقول فيما لا يفهمه إنه لا يفهمه فإن عدم الفهم قد
Every respondent either defends a famous position, or a shocking one, or neither of these but something else. And each one of these three, although it may produce from outside its genus, the first is that it produces from its genus. In general, most often is that the famous does not produce the shocking, and the shocking produces the famous. If the famous produces from the shocking, that is not a beautiful method because it is only necessary to produce a thing from what is more known, so the production of the famous should be from what is more famous. And each of the famous and the shocking cannot be quickly reached from what is far from understanding. Also, the opposite of the famous is mostly shocking, unless the famous is not absolute but relative to different groups and times. And the opposite of the shocking is famous. So if the position is famous, its opposite which is the questioner's desire is shocking, and vice versa. If it is neither famous nor shocking, then the questioner's desire is likewise. Therefore, the respondent who undertakes to support a famous position should refrain from conceding what is shocking, because the opposite of his position, which is the questioner's desire, is in the realm of shock. And it is hardly produced from famous premises; rather, famous premises are more likely to benefit in supporting his position than to contradict it. If he undertakes to support a shocking [position], he should not concede famous premises. We say: after I concede, for example, that good and evil are opposites, I do not concede that knowledge and ignorance are opposites. And if he undertakes to support a position that is neither famous nor shocking, then let him concede both the famous and the shocking, because the distance from them to clarifying the matter in which there is no satisfaction in the famous is that it is a great distance. The principle is that he should not concede at all what suggests that it invalidates his position which he supports, but he concedes it only lest he appear to deviate from fairness. The respondent may pause at the beginning about conceding what is sought from him to concede, if there is ambiguity in it, and not concede it except after investigation, for he is not obligated to answer what he does not understand. And there is no shame on him to say about what he does not understand that he does not understand it, for lack of understanding has [been mentioned].
Source pages p. 576

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical instruction categorizing respondents by the nature of the position (waḍʿ) they defend: famous (mashhūr), shocking (shanīʿ), or neither. The author explains the principles governing which premises a respondent should concede based on his position's status. A famous position should be defended by conceding famous premises, because famous premises normally produce a famous conclusion; if they produce a shocking one, that is inelegant. The opposite of a famous position is shocking, so the respondent defending a famous position must avoid conceding shocking premises, as those would lead to the questioner's desired shocking conclusion. Conversely, a respondent defending a shocking position should not concede famous premises, as those would produce a famous conclusion contradictory to his position. A defender of a neutral position can concede both, as the distance from those to his goal is great. The author then gives an example: after conceding that good and evil are opposites, one need not concede that knowledge and ignorance are opposites, because that concession may be harmful. Finally, the respondent has the right to pause and not concede ambiguous premises until clarification, as he is not obliged to answer what he does not understand. This passage provides concrete strategies for premise management in jadal.

Key Terms
وضع (waḍʿ): The position or thesis that the respondent undertakes to defend in a dialectical debate. [Note: No significant shift.]
شنيع (shanīʿ): A premise or conclusion that is shocking, repugnant, or contrary to common acceptance; the opposite of mashhūr. [Note: No significant shift.]
مطلوب (maṭlūb): The desired conclusion that the questioner aims to extract from the respondent; often the opposite of the respondent's position. [Note: No significant shift.]

Checkpoint
What should a respondent do if he is defending a shocking position?
A) Concede famous premises to appear fair.
B) Refuse to concede famous premises.
C) Concede both famous and shocking premises.
D) Only concede premises that are neither famous nor shocking.

Key Takeaway
In dialectical debate, the respondent's concession strategy is determined by whether his position is famous, shocking, or neutral.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In a debate, you are defending the position that 'knowledge is acquired' (a famous position). The questioner asks you to concede that 'all acquired knowledge requires effort.' Would you concede this premise? Use the passage's rule.
?
Classify the following positions as mashhūr, shanīʿ, or neutral in the context of Islamic theology: (1) The world is created, (2) God has a physical body, (3) Prophecy is possible.

Three Types of Positions and Respondent Strategy in Jadal
Author classifies positions and advises respondent strategy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a respondent's position as mashhur, shani', or neutral and apply the appropriate strategic rules in a dialectical debate.

Core Idea
The respondent's strategy depends on whether his position is well-known, shameful, or neutral.

In dialectical disputation (jadal), the respondent's choice of which premises to accept or reject is not arbitrary—it is governed by the nature of his position. This passage from our text classifies positions into three types and provides clear strategic guidelines for the respondent. Understanding these rules will sharpen your ability to defend a position effectively in a structured debate.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of positions
Find in the Arabic the three categories: mashhur (well-known), shani' (shameful), and 'neither' (neutral). Note that the author uses 'la hadha wa la dhalika' to indicate neutrality.
2
Determine opposites of each position
The text states that the opposite of a mashhur is mostly a shani', and vice versa, unless the mashhur is relative. Confirm this relationship. For a neutral position, the opposite is also neutral.
3
Apply the respondent's strategy
If the respondent defends a mashhur position, he should avoid accepting shani' premises because they contradict his position. If defending shani', he should avoid mashhur premises. If defending neutral, he may accept both.
The author gives an example: when defending a shameful position, after accepting that good and evil are opposites, he should not accept that knowledge and ignorance are opposites.
4
Recognize the principle of 'producing from its genus'
The author notes that although a position can be proven from premises outside its category, it is best to prove it from premises of the same category. For example, mashhur should be proved from mashhur premises.
5
Acknowledge the right to pause and seek clarification
The respondent may refuse to accept an ambiguous premise and can honestly say he does not understand it. The text says there is no shame in that, as the lack of understanding may be due to the questioner's faulty argument.

Source Text

وكل مجيب فإما أن يحفظ وضعًا مشهورًا أو شنيعا أو لا هذا ولا ذلك بل غير مما، وكل واحد من هذه الثلاثة وإن كان قد ينتج من غير جنسه فالأولى أن ينتج من جنسه. وبالجملة فالأكثر هو أن المشهور لا ينتج الشنيع، والشنيع ينتج المشهور. وإذا أنتج المشهور من الشنيع لم يكن ذلك طريقة جميلة لأنه إنما يجب إنتاج شيء عما هو أعرف فينبغي أن يكون إنتاج المشهور عما هو أشهر. وكل واحد من المشهور والشنيع لا يتوصل منه بسرعة إلى ما هو بعيد عن التفطن. وأيضًا فإن مقابل المشهور في الأكثر شنيع. اللهم إلا أن يكون المشهور ليس مطلقا بل بحسب قوم وقوم، ووقت ووقت؛ ومقابل الشنيع مشهور فإذا كان الوضع مشهورًا كان ما يقابله وهو مطلوب السائل شنيعا، وبالعكس. وإذا كان ليس بمشهور ولا بشنيع فمطلوب السائل كذلك فينبغي أن يخاط المجيب المتقلد نصرة وضع مشهور فيمتنع عن تسليم ما يكون شنيعا لأن نقيض وضعه وهو مطلوب السائل في معرض الشناعة؛ ولا يكاد ينتج عن المشهورات بل المشهورات أقرب إلى أن ينفع في نصرة وضعه من أن يناقض بها وضعه. وإن كان متكفلا نصرة شنيع لم يسلم المشهورات؛ ونقول إني بعد أن أسلم مثلا أن الخير والشر متقابلان فلستُ أسلم أن العلم والجهل متقابلان وإن كان متكفّلا نصرة وضع ليس بمشهور ولا بشنيع؛ فلتسلم المشهورات والشنيعات فإن المسافة منها إلى إيضاح الأمر الذي لا رضى فيه في المشهورات أن تكون مسافة بعيدة، والأصل أن لا يسلم أصلا ما يوهم أن يبطل وضعه الذي ينصره ولكن إنما يسلمه لئلا يظهر عدوله عن الإنصاف وللمجيب أن يتوقف في ابتداء الأمر عن تسليم ما يراد منه تسليمه إذا كان فيه غموض فلا يسلمه إلا بعد الاستكشاف فإنه لا يلزمه أن يجيب عما لا يفهمه؛ ولا عارَ عليه أن يقول / فيما لا يفهمه إنه لا يفهمه فإن عدم الفهم قد يكون بفساد ما يأتي به السائل.
And every respondent either defends a well-known (mashhur) position, or a shameful (shani') one, or neither this nor that but something else. And each one of these three, though it may be produced from something other than its genus, the best is that it be produced from its genus. In general, mostly the well-known does not produce the shameful, and the shameful produces the well-known. And if the well-known is produced from the shameful, that is not a good method, because something should only be produced from what is more known, so the well-known should be produced from what is more famous. And each of the well-known and shameful does not quickly reach what is far from discernment. Also, the opposite of the well-known is mostly shameful, unless the well-known is not absolute but relative to a group and group, and time and time; and the opposite of the shameful is well-known. So if the position is well-known, its opposite, which is the questioner's demand, is shameful, and vice versa. And if it is neither well-known nor shameful, then the questioner's demand is likewise. So the respondent, when undertaking to support a well-known position, should refrain from accepting what is shameful, because the opposite of his position, which is the questioner's demand, is in the context of shamefulness; and it hardly ever results from well-known premises, rather the well-known premises are more likely to help support his position than to contradict it. And if he is undertaking to support a shameful position, he should not accept well-known premises; and we say: after I accept, for example, that good and evil are opposites, I do not accept that knowledge and ignorance are opposites. And if he is undertaking to support a position that is neither well-known nor shameful, then let him accept both well-known and shameful premises, for the distance from them to clarifying a matter that is not satisfactory in the well-known is a far distance. The principle is that he should not accept at all what might nullify his position which he supports, but he accepts it only so as not to appear to deviate from fairness. And the respondent may pause at the beginning about accepting what he is asked to accept if there is ambiguity in it, so he should not accept it except after investigation, for he is not obliged to answer what he does not understand; and there is no shame on him to say about what he does not understand that he does not understand, for lack of understanding may be due to corruption in what the questioner brings.
Source pages p. 576

Commentary
This passage discusses the strategic considerations of a respondent (mujib) in dialectical disputation (jadal). The author classifies the positions (wad') a respondent may defend into three types: well-known (mashhur), shameful (shani'), and neutral (neither). He explains that although each type may be proven from premises outside its category, it is preferable to prove it from premises of the same category. For example, a well-known claim should be supported by premises that are even more famous, while a shameful claim can be produced from well-known premises (since the shameful produces the well-known, and vice versa as opposites). However, producing a well-known conclusion from shameful premises is not commendable because the conclusion should be more known than the premises. The author then provides practical advice: if the respondent supports a well-known position, he should avoid accepting shameful premises because they would contradict his position (since the opposite of a well-known is shameful). If he supports a shameful position, he should not accept well-known premises. If his position is neutral, he may accept both. The respondent also has the right to pause and not accept a premise if it is ambiguous, and he may honestly admit he does not understand it, as the lack of understanding may stem from flaws in the questioner's argument.

Key Terms
مجيب
وضع
مشهور
شنيع

Key Takeaway
The respondent's choice of which premises to accept depends on whether his position is well-known, shameful, or neutral, and he may always pause if a premise is unclear.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Respondent's Strategy in Dialectical Debates
Author advises respondent on handling judgments, opposing premises, and refuting syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the respondent's dialectical strategy to identify which premise to deny in a categorical syllogism and how to handle mixed true/false premises.

Core Idea
In debate, target the major premise when both premises are false; never obstinately reject a true premise.

In Islamic dialectic (jadal), the respondent must carefully evaluate the premises presented by the questioner. This passage from al-Qazwini's logic handbook provides tactical guidance: when faced with a syllogism that seems to refute your position, you must diagnose which premises are false and respond accordingly, avoiding accusations of obstinacy while preserving your argument.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of questioner's premises
Determine whether the premises are presented as a syllogism (qiyas), induction (istiqra'), or analogy (tamthil). The respondent's approach differs: for induction, if he accepted the particulars, he can only reject by contradiction.
2
Distinguish proximate premises from remote ones
The respondent may object to the immediate premises (muqaddima qariba) or to the premises that produce them. Knowing which level to attack can be more effective.
3
Evaluate the truth status of each premise
Determine whether each premise is true in all cases, false in all, or mixed. This follows from the general nature of judgments (hukm).
4
Apply the rule for one false and one true premise
If one premise is false and the other true, do not reject the true premise (to avoid being labeled obstinate). Instead, refute the false premise.
Example: If the major is true and the minor false, deny the minor.
5
Apply the rule for both false premises
If both premises are false, the best strategy is to deny the major premise (kubra), which contains the universal statement. Denying the major invalidates both the syllogism and the conclusion, whereas denying the minor only invalidates the syllogism.
Use the categorical example: 'Every J is B, every B is A' but if nothing of B is A, denying the major shows the conclusion cannot hold.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والحكم قد يكون صادقًا في جميع المعاني، وقد يكون كاذبا كذلك، وقد يكون صادقا في البعض منها كاذبا في البعض؛ فيجب أن يتوقف إلى أن يتقرر بالمطالبة ما يكون وارداً عليه. ثم المجيب إذا مانع السائل فإما أن يمانعه في المقدمات القريبة، وإما أن يمانعه في المقدّمات المنتجة للمقدمات القريبة. والسائل إما أن يأخذ تلك المقدّمات على أنها بعد نحو القياس، أو على أنها بعد نحو الاستقراء والتمثيل ؛ فإذا كان أخذها للاستقراء وكان المجيب قد يسلم للجزئيات لم يكن له أن يمتنع عن القبول إلا بالمناقضة. وإنه أي المجيب إذا تقلد وضعا صادقا في المشهور فقيس عليه بقياس ينتج نقيضه في الظاهر، فمعلوم أن فيه كذبًا؛ فإن كانت إحدى المقدمتين كاذبة، والأخرى صادقة. فلا ينبغي أن يأخذ الامتناع عن تسليم الصادقة في المشهور فينسب إلى التعشف خصوصا وله عنه محيض بل أن يتعرض للنقيض من جهة إبطال الكاذبة. وأما إذا اجتمع فيها جميعا الكذب فإنه وإن كان إنكاره واقعًا للقياس فأولى ما ينكره هو الكبرى المشتملة على القول الكلي. فإنه إذا دل عل كذبها بالضد فقد بطل القياس وأبطل أيضًا صدق النتيجة في نفسه مثل ما إذا قيل كل ج ب»، وكل «ب» أ، وكان لا يمكن أن يكون شيء من ب أ) امتنع القياس. وامتنع أيضًا صدق إن كان «ج أ»؛ وذلك لأنه إذا كان كل ج ب»، وكان لا شيء من «ب أ» وجب أن لا يكون شيء من (ج) أ»، وأما إنكار الصغرى فإنه وإن كان رفيعًا للقياس فلا يكون رفيعًا للقياس؛ والنتيجة معا. ثم المجيب إذا تعدد عليه إن اندفع كلام السائل بحسب وضعه أولا فله أن يغير ذلك الوضع بوجه يكون مسموعا عند أهل الجدل؛ فنقول نعني به كذا أو كذا، وكلام السائل مما يتبع كلامه فلا يبعد إذن أن يكون كلامه مصادرة
And a judgment may be true in all meanings, or false likewise, or true in some and false in some; so one must pause until it is determined by inquiry what applies to it. Then, if the respondent raises an objection to the questioner, he either objects to the proximate premises, or to the premises that produce the proximate premises. And the questioner either takes those premises as being according to the form of syllogism (qiyas), or according to the form of induction (istiqra') and analogy (tamthil). If he takes them for induction and the respondent had accepted the particulars, he may not refuse acceptance except by contradiction. And indeed, if the respondent undertakes a position that is true in the well-known, and a syllogism is constructed against it that apparently produces its opposite, then it is known that there is falsehood in it. If one of the two premises is false and the other true, he should not refuse to accept the true premise among the well-known, lest he be accused of obstinacy, especially when he has a way out; rather, he should address the opposite by refuting the false one. As for when both are false, then even if his denial applies to the syllogism, the best thing for him to deny is the major premise containing the universal statement. For if he proves its falsehood by its opposite, then the syllogism becomes invalid, and also the truth of the conclusion itself is invalidated. For example, if it is said: 'Every J is B', and 'Every B is A', and it is impossible that anything of B is A, then the syllogism is prevented, and also the truth of 'if J is A' is prevented. That is because if every J is B and nothing of B is A, then necessarily nothing of J is A. As for denying the minor premise, even if it nullifies the syllogism, it does not nullify the syllogism and the conclusion together. Then, if the respondent is faced with multiple [objections], if the questioner's speech is repelled by his own position initially, he has the right to change that position in a way that is acceptable to the people of dialectic; we say 'we mean by it such-and-such', and the questioner's speech follows his speech, so it is not unlikely that his speech is a petition of the point (musadara).
Source pages p. 576

Commentary
This passage provides further strategic guidance for the respondent in a dialectical debate. First, it discusses the nature of judgments (hukm) which can be true in all cases, false in all cases, or a mixture; the respondent should pause and investigate. Then it explains how the respondent can object to premises: either to the immediate premises (muqaddimat qariba) or to the premises that produce those immediate premises. The questioner's premises may be presented as a syllogism (qiyas), or as induction (istiqra') or analogy (tamthil). If the questioner uses induction and the respondent previously accepted the particulars, he cannot refuse the generalization except by pointing out a contradiction. The core of the passage concerns a scenario where the respondent holds a position that is true according to well-known opinion, and the questioner brings a syllogism that appears to prove the opposite. The respondent should identify which premise is false. If one premise is false and the other true, he should not reject the true premise (to avoid being seen as obstinate), but rather attack the false one. If both premises are false, he should deny the major premise (the universal statement) because denying it invalidates both the syllogism and the conclusion. An example is given with categorical terms: 'Every J is B, and every B is A', but if nothing of B is A, then the syllogism fails and the conclusion 'some J is A' is false. Denying the minor premise would only invalidate the syllogism but not necessarily the conclusion's truth. Finally, the respondent may reinterpret his position to defuse the questioner's argument, as long as the reinterpretation is dialectically acceptable. The last sentence notes that the questioner's speech may be a petition of the point (musadara), i.e., begging the question.

Key Terms
حكم
مقدمة قريبة
كبرى
صغرى
مصادرة

A) The minor premise (sughra)
B) The major premise (kubra)
C) The conclusion
D) Both premises equally

Key Takeaway
In dialectics, denying the major premise is most effective when both premises are false, as it invalidates both the syllogism and its conclusion.

Apply the passage's strategy: which premise do you deny, and why?

Objecting to Premises: Proximate vs Remote and Induction
Author on types of objections and induction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate between objecting to proximate premises and remote premises, and apply the contradiction strategy when responding to induction.

Core Idea
The respondent must object at the right level and cannot reject particulars without a counterexample.

In dialectical debate, the respondent has specific tools to object to the questioner's premises. This lesson covers the first distinctions: whether to object to the immediate premises or the ones that produced them, and how to handle inductive arguments using contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of objections
The author distinguishes objecting to the proximate premises (المقدمات القريبة) versus the premises that produce them. Determine which level the respondent should target.
Proximate premises are the immediate ones leading to the conclusion.
2
Determine the basis of the questioner's premises
The questioner may present premises as syllogistic (قياس), inductive (استقراء), or analogical (تمثيل). Identify which type is used to decide the response.
Induction and analogy require different handling than syllogism.
3
Handle induction with contradiction
If the questioner uses induction and the respondent has already accepted the particular instances, the only valid objection is to provide a contradictory example (مناقضة). The respondent cannot simply reject the induction without evidence.
This preserves the rules of fair debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم المجيب إذا مانع السائل فإما أن يمانعه في المقدمات القريبة، وإما أن يمانعه في المقدّمات المنتجة للمقدمات القريبة. والسائل إما أن يأخذ تلك المقدّمات على أنها بعد نحو القياس، أو على أنها بعد نحو الاستقراء والتمثيل؛ فإذا كان أخذها للاستقراء وكان المجيب قد يسلم للجزئيات لم يكن له أن يمتنع عن القبول إلا بالمناقضة. وإنه أي المجيب إذا تقلد وضعا صادقا في المشهور فقيس عليه بقياس ينتج نقيضه في الظاهر، فمعلوم أن فيه كذبًا؛ فإن كانت إحدى المقدمتين كاذبة، والأخرى صادقة. فلا ينبغي أن يأخذ الامتناع عن تسليم الصادقة في المشهور فينسب إلى التعشف خصوصا وله عنه محيض بل أن يتعرض للنقيض من جهة إبطال الكاذبة. وأما إذا اجتمع فيها جميعا الكذب فإنه وإن كان إنكاره واقعًا للقياس فأولى ما ينكره هو الكبرى المشتملة على القول الكلي. فإنه إذا دل على كذبها بالضد فقد بطل القياس وأبطل أيضًا صدق النتيجة في نفسه مثل ما إذا قيل كل ج ب، وكل ب أ، وكان لا يمكن أن يكون شيء من ب أ امتنع القياس. وامتنع أيضًا صدق إن كان ج أ؛ وذلك لأنه إذا كان كل ج ب، وكان لا شيء من ب أ وجب أن لا يكون شيء من ج أ، وأما إنكار الصغرى فإنه وإن كان رفيعًا للقياس فلا يكون رفيعًا للقياس؛ والنتيجة معا. ثم المجيب إذا تعدد عليه إن اندفع كلام السائل بحسب وضعه أولا فله أن يغير ذلك الوضع بوجه يكون مسموعا عند أهل الجدل؛ فنقول نعني به كذا أو كذا، وكلام السائل مما يتبع كلامه فلا يبعد إذن أن يكون كلامه مصادرة على المطلوب الأول؛ ولا عار عليه حينئذ فإن ذلك باعتبار كلام المجيب. ثمَّ الكلام في المنع يعتبر من حيث إنه نحو القول تارة، ويعتبر من حيث إنه نحو القائل تارة: أما الأول فمثل ما مرَّ في طريق المناقضة، وأما الثاني فمثل ما مرَّ من طريق التغير فإنه مما يمكنه دون السائل.
Then the respondent, when he objects to the questioner, either he objects to the proximate premises, or he objects to the premises that produce the proximate premises. The questioner either takes those premises as being after the manner of syllogism, or after the manner of induction and analogy. If he takes them for induction and the respondent has already assented to the particulars, he cannot refuse acceptance except by contradiction. And when the respondent adopts a position that is sound in common opinion and is measured against it by a syllogism that produces its opposite in appearance, it is known that there is falsehood in it. If one of the two premises is false and the other true, he should not refuse to accept the one that is true in common opinion, lest he be accused of obstinacy, especially when he has a way out; rather he should address the opposite by refuting the false one. But if falsehood combines in both of them, then although his denial falls upon the syllogism, the first thing he should deny is the major premise that contains the universal statement. For if he proves its falsehood by its opposite, the syllogism becomes invalid and also the truth of the conclusion in itself becomes invalid, such as when it is said 'Every J is B and every B is A' and it is impossible that anything of B be A; the syllogism is prevented. And also the truth of 'Is J A' is prevented; because if every J is B and nothing of B is A, it must be that nothing of J is A. As for denying the minor premise, although it removes the syllogism, it does not remove the syllogism and the conclusion together. Then the respondent, if the questioner's speech is repelled according to his position first, he may change that position in a way that is acceptable to the practitioners of dialectic, saying 'We mean by it such and such,' and the questioner's speech follows his own speech; so it is not far that his speech becomes a petition of the first principle. There is no shame on him then, for that is due to the respondent's speech. Then the discussion of objection is considered sometimes as being about the statement, and sometimes as being about the speaker. As for the first, it is like what was mentioned in the way of contradiction; as for the second, it is like what was mentioned in the way of alteration, for it is possible for him without the questioner.
Source pages p. 577

Commentary
This passage instructs the respondent in dialectical debate on how to object to the questioner's premises. The author first distinguishes two types of objection: objection to the immediate premises or to the premises that produced them. The questioner may present premises as based on syllogism, induction, or analogy. If the questioner uses induction and the respondent has already accepted the particulars, the respondent cannot reject the induction except by showing a contradictory example. The author then discusses case where the respondent holds a commonly accepted truth, and the questioner constructs a syllogism that apparently yields its opposite. The respondent should identify which premise is false. If one premise is false and the other true, he should not deny the true premise but attack the false one. If both premises are false, the respondent should deny the major premise (the universal statement) because denying it invalidates both the syllogism and the conclusion. An example is given: if the syllogism is 'Every J is B, Every B is A' but in reality no B is A, then the syllogism fails and the conclusion 'No J is A' follows. Denying the minor premise only removes the syllogism but not necessarily the conclusion. The passage then discusses that if the respondent's initial position is undermined, he may reinterpret his words in a way acceptable to dialecticians, even if it seems to beg the question—no shame since it is due to his own wording. Finally, objection is considered from two perspectives: the statement itself (contradiction) or the speaker (alteration of meaning), the latter being an option for the respondent only.

Key Terms
مانع
مقدمات قريبة
مناقضة

Checkpoint

A) Deny the induction outright
B) Demand a different proof
C) Provide a contradictory example
D) Attack the questioner's character

Key Takeaway
When the questioner uses induction and the respondent has accepted the particulars, the only valid objection is to present a counterexample.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In a debate, the questioner uses induction: 'Sales of food items are permissible when the item is present. Rice, dates, and wheat are all food items sold in this manner. Therefore, all food items are permissible when present.' As respondent, you have already accepted that rice, dates, and wheat are permissible when present. How would you use the contradiction strategy? Provide a counterexample from Islamic law.

Strategic Denial: Choosing the Right Premise in Syllogisms
Author on handling false premises and reinterpretation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to decide whether to deny the major or minor premise in a categorical syllogism when the conclusion conflicts with a known truth, and explain the superiority of denying the major when both premises are false.

Core Idea
Denying the major premise destroys both the syllogism and the false conclusion; denying the minor only destroys the syllogism.

When the questioner constructs a syllogism that appears to prove the opposite of a known truth, the respondent must find the false premise. This lesson teaches a crucial strategy: if both premises are false, deny the major premise first. The author also discusses the option of reinterpreting one's own position if the initial response fails.

Guided Path
1
Identify the known truth held by the respondent
The respondent has a position (وضع) that is sound in common opinion. Recognize this truth as the standard against which the syllogism's conclusion is tested.
This known truth will guide which premises are suspect.
2
Examine the questioner's syllogism
The questioner presents a syllogism that apparently yields the opposite of the known truth. Locate the major and minor premises and the conclusion.
The syllogism must contain at least one false premise.
3
Determine which premises are false
Check each premise against the known truth. If only one is false, attack that one. If both are false, prioritize the major premise (الكبرى) because it is a universal statement.
Denying a true premise leads to accusations of obstinacy (تعشف).
4
Apply the principle: deny the major when both are false
Use a counterexample (ضد) to show the major is false. This invalidates the syllogism and also the conclusion's apparent truth. The minor denial only removes the syllogism, not the conclusion.
The author illustrates with the example: if 'Every J is B' and 'Every B is A' but no B is A, denying the major 'Every B is A' is effective.
5
Consider reinterpretation as a last resort
If the respondent's initial position is repelled, he may reinterpret his wording (تغير الوضع) in a way acceptable to dialecticians, even if it seems to beg the question.
This is permitted because the respondent is responding to the questioner's speech.

Source Text

Dismissing Arguments for Form and Relevance (Types 1-2)
Author enumerates five cases where the opponent's argument should be silenced.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between an argument that is not syllogistic at all and one that produces a different conclusion than required, and explain why both deserve silence.

Core Idea
An argument is not worth hearing if it has no form or if it misses the target.

The first two defects are about the argument's relationship to the desired conclusion: either it has no valid logical structure (type 1), or it proves something else entirely (type 2). Recognizing these saves time in debate.

Guided Path
1
Understand ta’līf qiyāsī
A syllogistic composition is an arrangement of premises that yields a valid syllogism. The first type fails to be any syllogism at all, even potentially.
2
Identify Type 1: Non-syllogistic
If the opponent's statements cannot form a syllogism even with modification, they deserve silence. They have no logical form.
Example: 'The Quran is from Allah because it is miraculous.' That's not a syllogism.
3
Identify Type 2: Wrong Conclusion
The argument may be a valid syllogism, but it proves something other than the point under dispute. It is irrelevant.
Example: Proving the existence of a creator when the debate is about divine attributes.
4
Contrast with Valid Arguments
A valid syllogism in debate must have the right form and target the desired conclusion. Compare with the first two defects.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المواضع التي لها استحقاق التسكيت في القول بنفسه فهي خمسة: أحدها أن تكون الأقاويل لا تنتج أصلاً؛ إذ لا تكون تأليفا قياسيا لا بالفعل ولا بالقوة حتى يكون بحيث إذا زيد فيها شيء أو نقص يكون له صورة قياس ممكن؛ والثاني أن يكون منتجا ولكن لغير المطلوب؛ والثالث أن تكون قد تنتج المطلوب ولكن مما هو كذب وغير مشهور؛ والرابع أن يكون فيه فصل لا يحتاج إليه فإنه كان مشتركًا لما أنه يتعرض لمقدّمات لحصول الغرض الذي يحصل ذلك الغرض ببعض تلك المقدمات؛ والخامس أن تكون مقدماته صادقة ولكن هي أخفى من النتيجة.
As for the situations that have the right to be silenced in the statement itself, they are five: First, that the statements produce nothing at all; for they are not a syllogistic composition either actually or potentially, such that if something were added or subtracted a possible syllogistic form would result. Second, that it is productive but of something other than the desired [conclusion]. Third, that it may produce the desired [conclusion] but from what is false and not commonly accepted. Fourth, that there is in it a superfluous division, for it is redundant because it brings forward premises to obtain an objective that can be obtained by some of those premises. Fifth, that its premises are true but they are more hidden than the conclusion.
Source pages p. 578

Commentary
This passage lists five situations where an argument in a dialectical debate is so defective that it deserves immediate silence—meaning the opponent need not even respond. The first type is a non-syllogistic arrangement: the statements do not form a valid syllogism even potentially; no addition or subtraction can fix it. The second type is a syllogism that produces a conclusion different from the one required; it misses the target. The third type produces the desired conclusion but from premises that are false and not commonly known (i.e., not acceptable in dialectic). The fourth type includes unnecessary divisions or premises that are redundant; the goal could be achieved with fewer premises. The fifth type, though the premises are true, they are more obscure than the conclusion, making the argument useless because it does not clarify. The author does not provide examples, but in Islamic logic, one can imagine a fiqh argument where someone says 'Every intoxicant is forbidden, and this drink is intoxicating, therefore it is forbidden'—if the listener already knows the conclusion but the premise 'this drink is intoxicating' is hidden, it fails the fifth type.

Key Terms
تسكيت
تأليف قياسي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the defect of the second type of argument that deserves silence?
A) It has no syllogistic composition at all.
B) It produces a conclusion different from the one required.
C) Its premises are false and not commonly accepted.
D) It contains a superfluous division.

Key Takeaway
An argument can be dismissed without response if it lacks syllogistic form (type 1) or if it proves the wrong conclusion (type 2).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify which type (1 or 2) a statement falls into: In a debate about the permissibility of riba, an opponent says: 'Allah permitted trade, and riba is a type of trade; therefore riba is permitted.' What defect does this have?
?
Construct a jadal exchange where one party uses a type 1 argument (non-syllogistic) and the other invokes taskīt.

Dismissing Arguments for Premise Quality and Efficiency (Types 3-5)
Author enumerates five cases where the opponent's argument should be silenced.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when an argument should be silenced because its premises are false, redundant, or more obscure than the conclusion.

Core Idea
Even a valid syllogism fails if its premises are false, excessive, or harder to know than the conclusion.

The last three defects concern the premises themselves: their truth, their necessity, and their clarity. Even if an argument has the right form and target, it can be dismissed if its premises are unacceptable or if it uses more steps than needed or if it doesn't illuminate.

Guided Path
1
Identify Type 3: False and Non-Common Premises
The conclusion may be correct, but if it is derived from false and not widely accepted premises, the argument is defective in dialectic. Dialectic requires premises that are agreed upon or at least plausible.
Example: 'The Quran is created because it is temporal; everything temporal is created.' If the opponent denies the premise that the Quran is temporal, this fails.
2
Identify Type 4: Redundant Divisions
If the argument includes unnecessary premises or steps that are not needed to reach the conclusion, it is superfluous. It should be silenced because it wastes time.
Example: Proving that prayer is obligatory through a chain of three syllogisms when one would suffice.
3
Identify Type 5: Premises More Hidden than Conclusion
Even if premises are true, if they are less known or more obscure than the conclusion itself, the argument fails to clarify. It is pointless.
Example: Using a complex theological proof to prove the existence of Allah to a believer who already accepts it.
4
Practice Distinguishing Among Types 3-5
Given a short argument, determine which defect applies. Note that an argument could have multiple defects, but only the primary one is needed for taskīt.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المواضع التي لها استحقاق التسكيت في القول بنفسه فهي خمسة: أحدها أن تكون الأقاويل لا تنتج أصلاً؛ إذ لا تكون تأليفا قياسيا لا بالفعل ولا بالقوة حتى يكون بحيث إذا زيد فيها شيء أو نقص يكون له صورة قياس ممكن؛ والثاني أن يكون منتجا ولكن لغير المطلوب؛ والثالث أن تكون قد تنتج المطلوب ولكن مما هو كذب وغير مشهور؛ والرابع أن يكون فيه فصل لا يحتاج إليه فإنه كان مشتركًا لما أنه يتعرض لمقدّمات لحصول الغرض الذي يحصل ذلك الغرض ببعض تلك المقدمات؛ والخامس أن تكون مقدماته صادقة ولكن هي أخفى من النتيجة.
As for the situations that have the right to be silenced in the statement itself, they are five: First, that the statements produce nothing at all; for they are not a syllogistic composition either actually or potentially, such that if something were added or subtracted a possible syllogistic form would result. Second, that it is productive but of something other than the desired [conclusion]. Third, that it may produce the desired [conclusion] but from what is false and not commonly accepted. Fourth, that there is in it a superfluous division, for it is redundant because it brings forward premises to obtain an objective that can be obtained by some of those premises. Fifth, that its premises are true but they are more hidden than the conclusion.
Source pages p. 578

Commentary
This passage lists five situations where an argument in a dialectical debate is so defective that it deserves immediate silence—meaning the opponent need not even respond. The first type is a non-syllogistic arrangement: the statements do not form a valid syllogism even potentially; no addition or subtraction can fix it. The second type is a syllogism that produces a conclusion different from the one required; it misses the target. The third type produces the desired conclusion but from premises that are false and not commonly known (i.e., not acceptable in dialectic). The fourth type includes unnecessary divisions or premises that are redundant; the goal could be achieved with fewer premises. The fifth type, though the premises are true, they are more obscure than the conclusion, making the argument useless because it does not clarify. The author does not provide examples, but in Islamic logic, one can imagine a fiqh argument where someone says 'Every intoxicant is forbidden, and this drink is intoxicating, therefore it is forbidden'—if the listener already knows the conclusion but the premise 'this drink is intoxicating' is hidden, it fails the fifth type.

Key Terms
كذب
غير مشهور
فصل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The third type
B) The fourth type
C) The fifth type
D) None of the above

Key Takeaway
An argument can be dismissed if its premises are unacceptable, unnecessary, or unenlightening, even if it is formally valid and targets the right conclusion.

Evaluating Syllogisms in Dialectical Exchange
Author gives general guidance on evaluating syllogisms in debate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the quality of a syllogism used in debate and classify it as instructive, probative, or sophistical according to the author's criteria.

Core Idea
The worst syllogism aims at truth from falsehood.

This passage provides essential guidance for both questioner and respondent in a dialectical debate. The author explains that not every position is equally suited for syllogistic premises and that a productive syllogism can be worse than a non-productive one. He categorizes syllogisms into three types and warns that using falsehoods to reach truth is the worst form of reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two parties in dialectic
The author addresses advice common to both the questioner (السائل) and the respondent (المجيب). Recognize that both have responsibilities in evaluating arguments.
2
Understand the principle of appropriateness
The author states that not every position deserves the same syllogistic premises. Each position (وضع) has its own appropriate premise (مقدمة بحسبه). This means debaters must choose premises relevant to the proposition at hand.
3
Distinguish productive from non-productive
A productive syllogism (قول منتج) yields a conclusion. The author notes that a productive one can be worse than a non-productive one if it is composed of truths but used poorly, or worse if composed of falsehoods aiming at truth.
The key is not just productivity but the truth value of premises and the purpose.
4
Identify the worst kind of syllogism
The author explicitly says the worst is a syllogism composed of false premises (كاذبات) intended to produce a true conclusion (إنتاج صدق). Such a construction is not a genuine syllogism.
This emphasizes that false premises can never be acceptable, even if the conclusion is true.
5
Classify syllogisms into three types
Syllogisms are either instructive (تعليمية) for teaching, probative (احتجاجية) for argumentation, or sophistical (سوفسطائية) which appear demonstrative but are not. Also mentioned is skeptical reasoning (تشكيك) as a dialectical exercise.
The classification helps debaters anticipate the nature of the argument and respond appropriately.

Chapter: On the common advice for both [i.e., the questioner and the respondent]. Know that not every position is equal in deserving the bearing of syllogistic premises upon it; rather, every position has a premise according to it. Many times a productive statement is worse than a non-productive one. That is because sometimes a productive statement in its form is composed of truths, and sometimes through an addition intended for it, it becomes productive. The false is never suitable. The worst of all that is when it is composed of falsehoods intended to produce truth. In reality, this is not a proper syllogism about the thing. Syllogisms are either instructive, or probative, or sophistical—except those that are thought to be demonstrative but in reality are not. Commentary
This section gives general advice applicable to both parties in a dialectical exchange. The author states that not every position deserves to have syllogistic premises applied to it; each position has its appropriate premise. He observes that a productive argument (one that yields a conclusion) can be worse than a non-productive one. For example, a productive argument may be composed of true premises and still be inferior to a non-productive one with true premises. He emphasizes that falsehood is never acceptable, and the worst case is a syllogism composed entirely of falsehoods aiming to produce a true conclusion—such a construct is not a genuine syllogism. He then classifies syllogisms into three types: instructive (ta'līmiyya), probative (iḥtijājiyya), and sophistical (sūfastā'iyya). Those that are thought to be demonstrative but are not actually demonstrative fall under the sophistical kind. Finally, he mentions that the genus of skeptical reasoning (tashkīk) is part of dialectical exercise, achieved by presenting a syllogism on two opposites. The text is incomplete, but the author appears to be introducing a type of dialectical training.
