The Five Grounds for Silencing an Argument in Dialectical Debate
Enumeration of five faults triggering silencing in dialectical debate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify each of the five faults that make an argument subject to silencing in a dialectical debate and apply them to examples from Islamic juristic debates.

Core Idea
An argument can be silenced for being invalid, irrelevant, false, redundant, or obscure.

In dialectical debate (jadal), not every faulty argument merits a response. The tradition identifies five specific faults that give the opponent the right to demand silence. These faults are objective criteria rooted in the logical structure of the argument itself. Let's examine each one so you can recognize when and why an argument deserves to be silenced in a scholarly dispute.

Guided Path
1
Identify the fault type
For each of the five faults, locate the key Arabic phrase that defines it (e.g., 'لا تنتج أصلاً' for the first).
2
Understand the logical flaw
Explain in your own words why each fault undermines the argument's validity or dialectical acceptability.
3
Construct a counterexample
For each fault, create a simple syllogism from fiqh or kalam that exhibits that fault.
Use examples like the ones in the commentary.
4
Apply the rule
Given a sample argument, determine whether it qualifies for silencing and which fault applies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المواضع التي لها استحقاق التسكيت في القول بنفسه فهي خمسة: أحدها أن تكون الأقاويل لا تنتج أصلاً؛ إذ لا تكون تأليفا قياسيا لا بالفعل ولا بالقوة حتى يكون بحيث إذا زيد فيها شيء أو نقص يكون له صورة قياس ممكن؛ والثاني أن يكون منتجا ولكن لغير المطلوب؛ والثالث أن تكون قد تنتج المطلوب ولكن مما هو كذب وغير مشهور؛ والرابع أن يكون فيه فصل لا يحتاج إليه فإنه كان مشتركًا لما أنه يتعرض لمقدّمات لحصول الغرض الذي يحصل ذلك الغرض ببعض تلك المقدمات؛ والخامس أن تكون مقدماته صادقة ولكن هي أخفى من النتيجة.
As for the positions that deserve silencing in the statement itself, they are five: First, that the statements do not produce any conclusion at all; since they are not a syllogistic composition either actually or potentially, such that if something were added or removed, they would have a possible syllogistic form. Second, that it produces a conclusion but not the desired one. Third, that it may produce the desired conclusion but from something false and not commonly accepted. Fourth, that it contains an unnecessary division that is shared, because it introduces premises to achieve the purpose which could be achieved by some of those premises. Fifth, that its premises are true but more obscure than the conclusion.
Source pages p. 578

Commentary
This passage from the chapter on dialectical debate lists five specific faults that make a speaker's argument subject to being silenced (taskit). The first fault is when the statements do not constitute a syllogism at all, either actually or potentially; even if something were added or removed, they would not form a valid syllogistic figure. The second fault is when the argument produces a conclusion, but it is not the one under discussion—a classic non-sequitur. For example, if someone argues 'Every intoxicant is forbidden, and nabidh is an intoxicant, therefore nabidh is impure' (the conclusion should be 'forbidden'). The third fault is when the desired conclusion is reached, but from premises that are false or not commonly accepted (ghayr mashhur); in dialectical debate, premises must be acknowledged by the opponent. The fourth fault is including an unnecessary intermediate step (fasl) that splits the argument without need; the purpose could have been achieved with fewer premises. The fifth fault is when the premises are true but more obscure (akhfa) than the conclusion itself, which defeats the purpose of proof—the conclusion should be clearer or at least as clear as the premises. The teacher should explain each fault with examples from juristic (usul) or theological debates.

Key Terms
تسكيت
القول المنتج
فصل لا يحتاج إليه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Arguing 'The world is created, and everything created has a creator, therefore the world has a creator'
C) Arguing 'All humans are mortal, and Socrates is a human, therefore Socrates is mortal'
D) Arguing 'Every act is either obligatory or forbidden, and prayer is obligatory, therefore prayer is forbidden'

Key Takeaway
The five faults ensure dialectical debates remain focused on valid, relevant, and clear reasoning, preventing sophistry and empty disputes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Classification of Syllogisms and the Genus of Doubt
Classification of syllogisms and genus of doubt.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between educational, argumentative, and sophistical syllogisms, and identify the two types of dialectical arguments within the genus of doubt.

Core Idea
Not all syllogisms aim at certainty; some are for education, debate, or testing.

The author introduces a crucial classification: syllogisms are not all alike. Some are meant to teach, some to argue, and some are deceptive. Moreover, within dialectic, there is a 'genus of doubt' that includes practice and test arguments. Understanding these distinctions helps you choose the right type of argument for your purpose.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three main categories of syllogisms
Find in the Arabic the three terms: تعليمية (educational), احتجاجية (argumentative), سفسطائية (sophistical). Note their distinct purposes.
Sophistical ones appear demonstrative but are not.
2
Understand the purpose of each category
Educational syllogisms teach; argumentative syllogisms prove a point in debate; sophistical syllogisms deceive by seeming valid.
3
Recognize the genus of doubt (جنس التشكيك)
This is a subcategory of dialectic that produces doubt, not certainty. It includes two types: practice dialectic (جدلي ارتياضي) and test dialectic (جدلي امتحاني).
Practice dialectic examines both sides; test dialectic argues against the truth for training.
4
Distinguish practice vs. test dialectic
Practice dialectic brings syllogisms on two opposed things. Test dialectic brings syllogisms on the contrary of truth to test the opponent or student.
Example: the well-known syllogism of Alif on repelling motion used only for testing.
5
Note the worst kind of argument
The author warns that the most inferior syllogism is one composed of falsehoods intended to produce truth. This is not a proper syllogism.
This is the sophistical type in its worst form.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Chapter on the common advices between them [i.e., between the questioner and the answerer]. Many times the productive statement is inferior to that which does not produce, because the productive statement may in its form be composed of truths, and sometimes it is by an addition intended to become productive. The probable conclusion may occur as true and probable, or as two probables, or as something ugly that may be accepted as probable. The permanent is stronger, and among the non-permanent, some are more complete in existence and some are weaker in existence. Opinion does not occur in the permanent but in the other which is the possible majority.' Then the rest of the speech follows. This is among that which has verbosity that obscures and distracts from attention.
Source pages p. 578

Commentary
This passage from the chapter on common advices addresses the relationship between positions and premises, then classifies syllogisms. The author first warns that not every position deserves to carry the same premises; rather, premises vary according to the position. Often a valid syllogism composed of true premises can be inferior to a non-syllogism if it is misapplied. The worst case is composing from false premises to produce truth—this is not a proper syllogism. Syllogisms are then divided into three categories: educational (ta'limiyya) meant to teach, argumentative (ihtijajiyya) for debate, and sophistical (sufistaiyya) which appear demonstrative but are not. The author then discusses the 'genus of doubt' (jins al-tashkik) which includes two types of dialectical argument: practice dialectic (jadali riyadi) that puts forward syllogisms on opposite sides, and test dialectic (jadali imtihani) that uses syllogisms against the truth for testing purposes. An example is given: the well-known syllogism of 'Alif' on repelling motion (perhaps a historical reference) used only for testing. The probable conclusion can have various outcomes. The weakest form is one containing an unnecessary division that obscures the logical connection. The author illustrates this with an example: a speaker wants to prove that some opinion is stronger than another. Instead of a simple argument (e.g., 'some matters are stronger, so opinion of the stronger is stronger'), he unnecessarily divides matters into permanent and non-permanent, permanent being stronger, then further dividing non-permanent, etc. This verbosity (hashw) distracts from the point. The teacher should emphasize that in dialectic, simplicity and clarity are virtues; unnecessary divisions weaken the argument.

The Danger of Unnecessary Divisions in Arguments
Warning against unnecessary divisions in arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to detect unnecessary divisions in a logical argument and explain how they obscure the implication.

Core Idea
Unnecessary divisions are the weakest form of argument because they distract from the logical connection.

The author warns that the weakest argument in the genus of doubt is one that contains a division that is not needed. He gives an example of a speaker who wants to show that some opinion is stronger than another but unnecessarily divides matters into permanent and non-permanent, etc., obscuring the simple point. This lesson teaches you to avoid such verbosity.

Guided Path
1
Read the author's example
The speaker wants to prove that some opinion (ظن) is stronger than another. Identify the simple argument that would suffice.
The sufficient argument: 'Some matters are stronger than others; the opinion of the stronger is stronger than the opinion of the weaker.'
2
Spot the unnecessary division
The speaker adds: 'Among matters are what is permanent and what is non-permanent. The permanent is stronger, and among the non-permanent, some are more complete in existence, etc.' This division of matters into permanent/non-permanent is not needed for the conclusion.
The original point only needed a comparative statement; the extra categories are hashw (filler).
3
Understand how it obscures the implication
The unnecessary division hides the premise from which the implication comes (المقدمة التي عنها اللزوم). The listener gets distracted by the categories and loses the direct logical link.
The author says it 'obscures the aspect of implication' (يُعمي وجه اللزوم).
4
Recognize the principle
If a division is not needed for the conclusion, it weakens the argument. The speech is complete without it.
Always ask: Is this distinction necessary for the logical flow?

Chapter on the common advices between them [i.e., between the questioner and the answerer]. Know that not every position is equal in deserving to carry the premises of the syllogism; rather, for each position there is a premise according to it. Many times the productive statement is inferior to that which does not produce, because the productive statement may in its form be composed of truths, and sometimes it is by an addition intended to become productive. The false is not suitable in any case, and the worst of all is that it is composed of falsehoods intended to produce truth. In reality, this is not an appropriate syllogism for the thing. Syllogisms are either educational (ta'limiyya), or argumentative (ihtijajiyya), or sophistical (sufistaiyya) except those that are thought to be demonstrative, but in reality they are not. As for the genus of doubt (jins al-tashkik), from that is a dialectical practice that is completed by bringing a syllogism on two opposed things as you learned, and from that is a test dialectic (jadali imtihani) as when syllogisms are brought on the contrary of truth. The well-known like the syllogism of Alif on repelling motion when nothing other than testing is intended. The probable conclusion may occur as true and probable, or as two probables, or as something ugly that may be accepted as probable. Its ruling is a ruling between the two things, and the weakest in its domain is that it contains an unnecessary division, while the speech is complete without it. Then its entry obscures the aspect of implication and hides the premise from which the implication comes. For example, a speaker who wants to show that some opinion is stronger than another, and it would suffice for him to say, for instance, that some matters are stronger than others, and the opinion of what is stronger is stronger than the opinion of others. Thus this becomes clear. So he says, 'Among matters are what is permanent and what is non-permanent. The permanent is stronger, and among the non-permanent, some are more complete in existence and some are weaker in existence. Opinion does not occur in the permanent but in the other which is the possible majority.' Then the rest of the speech follows. This is among that which has verbosity that obscures and distracts from attention.
Source pages p. 578

Commentary
This passage from the chapter on common advices addresses the relationship between positions and premises, then classifies syllogisms. The author first warns that not every position deserves to carry the same premises; rather, premises vary according to the position. Often a valid syllogism composed of true premises can be inferior to a non-syllogism if it is misapplied. The worst case is composing from false premises to produce truth—this is not a proper syllogism. Syllogisms are then divided into three categories: educational (ta'limiyya) meant to teach, argumentative (ihtijajiyya) for debate, and sophistical (sufistaiyya) which appear demonstrative but are not. The author then discusses the 'genus of doubt' (jins al-tashkik) which includes two types of dialectical argument: practice dialectic (jadali riyadi) that puts forward syllogisms on opposite sides, and test dialectic (jadali imtihani) that uses syllogisms against the truth for testing purposes. An example is given: the well-known syllogism of 'Alif' on repelling motion (perhaps a historical reference) used only for testing. The probable conclusion can have various outcomes. The weakest form is one containing an unnecessary division that obscures the logical connection. The author illustrates this with an example: a speaker wants to prove that some opinion is stronger than another. Instead of a simple argument (e.g., 'some matters are stronger, so opinion of the stronger is stronger'), he unnecessarily divides matters into permanent and non-permanent, permanent being stronger, then further dividing non-permanent, etc. This verbosity (hashw) distracts from the point. The teacher should emphasize that in dialectic, simplicity and clarity are virtues; unnecessary divisions weaken the argument.

حشو
وجه اللزوم

The Decisive Statement: Conditions of Conclusive Argument
Conditions for a decisive syllogism

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three conditions of a decisive statement (al-qawl al-fāṣil) and test whether a given syllogism meets them.

Core Idea
A decisive statement requires evident premises, correct arrangement, and the intended conclusion – all tailored to the discipline.

Logic distinguishes arguments that settle a question from those that only appear to. In this passage, the author lays down the criteria for a decisive statement (al-qawl al-fāṣil): premises that are evidently true, a correct syllogistic structure, and a conclusion that follows. Even if the arrangement is imperfect, it can be fixed. Understanding these conditions helps you separate genuine proofs from flawed ones in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Extract the definition of al-qawl al-fāṣil
Find the phrase 'القول الفاصل' and note the three elements: premises evident in truth (ظاهر في الصدق), correct arrangement (ترتيبه في الإصابة بالفعل), and conclusion entailed (النتيجة لازمة).
2
Distinguish decisive from near-decisive
The author mentions a category whose premises are 'close to evident' (قريبة من تلك المقدمات). This is not fully decisive but acceptable in some contexts.
3
Understand the repair process
If the full syllogistic form is missing (عدم تمام هيئة التأليف), one must return to the required composition by adjusting order, adding, or removing steps.
This shows the decisive statement is not rigid; it can be fixed if the core conditions are present.
4
Apply to a sample syllogism
Take a standard kalam proof: 'The world is changing; everything changing is created; therefore the world is created.' Check if the premises are evident and the arrangement correct.
In this proof, the premises are considered evident from observation, and the form is a valid first-figure syllogism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والقول الفاصل ما تكون مقدماته ظاهر في الصدق. وكذلك ترتيبه في الإصابة بالفعل ويليه الذي تكون مقدماته قريبة من تلك المقدمات؛ والنتيجة لازمة منها. وإن كان قد عدم تمام هيئة التأليف فهو يجيب يعود إلى التأليف الواجب بتعليل تقديم أو تأخير وزيادة ونقصان. وغير الفاصل الذي هو من الرذائل ما يرى أنه منتج وليس بمنتج وهو لمشاغبي، أو هو منتج لكن غير الذي يراد إنتاجه. ومن الرذائل ما يكون منتجا للمطلوب لكن من مقدمات خارجة عن الصناعة غير مناسبة كمن يبرهن على الطب من مقدمات هندسية، أو على المطلوب الجدلي ببرهان تعليمي. ومنها ما إذا كان إنتاجه للصدق إنما هو عن الكذب لا على سبيل قياس الخلف، ولا لأن الكذب مشهور فيكون بالحري أن يلازم المستعمل لأمور هي مستحقة لا لأجل الكذب؛ فإن للكذب مدخلاً
The decisive statement (al-qawl al-fāṣil) is that whose premises are evident in truth, and likewise its arrangement is in correct actuality. Next to it is that whose premises are close to those premises, and the conclusion is entailed from them. If the complete form of composition is missing, then one must return to the required composition by explaining precedence, delay, addition, and subtraction. The non-decisive (ghayr al-fāṣil), which is among the vices (radhāʾil), is that which is seen to produce a conclusion but does not actually produce it, and it is for the contentious (mushāghibī), or it produces a conclusion but not the one intended. Among the vices is that which produces the desired conclusion but from premises outside the discipline and inappropriate, like someone who demonstrates medicine from geometrical premises, or the dialectical desired conclusion with a demonstrative proof. And among them is that its production of truth is from falsehood, not by way of reduction to absurdity (qiyās al-khalf), nor because falsehood is widely known, so that it would be appropriate for the user to use things that are worthy not because of falsehood; for falsehood has an entry...
Source pages p. 579

Commentary
This passage defines the decisive statement (al-qawl al-fāṣil) as one with premises that are evidently true and with a correct syllogistic arrangement that leads to the conclusion. If the arrangement is imperfect, one should adjust by reordering or adding/removing steps. The opposite, the non-decisive, falls into several vices: first, an argument that appears to produce a conclusion but does not—this is characteristic of the contentious person (mushāghib). Second, an argument that produces a conclusion but not the intended one—a type of irrelevance. Third, an argument that produces the desired conclusion but from premises that are extraneous to the discipline (khārija ʿan al-ṣināʿa), such as using geometrical premises to prove a medical point, or using a demonstrative (burhān) premise in a dialectical context. Fourth, an argument that produces truth from falsehood, but not through the legitimate method of reduction to absurdity (qiyās al-khalf), nor because the falsehood is widely accepted; rather, the falsehood is used in a way that is not proper. The teacher should explain each vice with relevant examples: e.g., using physics to prove a fiqh ruling would be extraneous. The mention of 'qiyās al-khalf' is important—it is the valid method of proving by assuming the opposite and deriving a contradiction.

Key Terms
القول الفاصل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Premises are evident in truth
B) Arrangement is correct
C) The conclusion is surprising
D) The syllogism's composition can be repaired if incomplete

Key Takeaway
A decisive statement must have evident premises, correct arrangement, and a conclusion that follows; if imperfect, it can be repaired by adjusting the composition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

The Vices of Non-Decisive Arguments in Dialectic
Classification of vices in argumentation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify the four types of vices (radhāʾil) in arguments and detect them in debated discourse.

Core Idea
Non-decisive arguments fail in four ways: they don't produce a conclusion, produce the wrong one, use extraneous premises, or misuse falsehood.

Not every argument that looks like a proof actually decides the issue. The author lists four vices (radhāʾil) that characterize non-decisive statements: pseudo-production, irrelevance, extraneous premises, and improper use of falsehood. Recognizing these vices protects you from being misled by seemingly persuasive but actually flawed reasoning in theological and legal debates.

Guided Path
1
Enumerate the four vices from the text
Read the passage and list: (1) argument that seems to produce but does not (لمشاغبي), (2) produces a conclusion but not the intended one, (3) produces the desired conclusion but from extraneous premises, (4) produces truth from falsehood improperly.
2
Analyze the first two vices: pseudo-production and irrelevance
The first is deception by the contentious person (mushāghibī). The second is a mismatch between what the argument proves and what was asked. Both fail to achieve the purpose.
These are common in sophistry.
3
Analyze the third vice: extraneous premises
Producing the desired conclusion but using premises from a different discipline (e.g., geometry for medicine) violates disciplinary boundaries. This includes using demonstrative proof in dialectic.
This shows that context matters: the same conclusion may be validly proven, but with inappropriate materials.
4
Analyze the fourth vice: improper use of falsehood
Producing truth from falsehood is only valid if done via reduction to absurdity (qiyās al-khalf) or if the falsehood is widely known (مشهور). Otherwise, the falsehood is misused.
Qiyās al-khalf is a legitimate method: assume the opposite, derive contradiction, thus prove the original.

Criteria of the Decisive Statement
Author defines the decisive statement and contrasts it with imperfect syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a decisive statement (al-qawl al-fāṣil) by verifying that its premises are manifestly true and its arrangement leads to correct conclusion, and distinguish it from an imperfect syllogism.

Core Idea
A decisive statement requires both manifestly true premises and correct arrangement.

In this passage, the author defines the 'decisive statement', which is the gold standard in dialectical reasoning. He then contrasts it with weaker forms that lack perfect compositional form. Understanding this distinction helps you evaluate the strength of arguments in Islamic scholarship.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises of the statement
Check whether the premises are manifestly true (ẓāhira fī al-ṣidq). This means their truth is obvious to the audience, requiring no further proof.
2
Examine the arrangement (tartīb)
See if the sequence of premises leads to actual correctness (al-iṣāba bi al-fi'l). The conclusion should follow naturally from the order given.
3
Contrast with imperfect syllogisms
If the statement lacks complete compositional form (hay'a al-ta'līf), it is not decisive. The author says it must then be adjusted by explaining precedence or delay, addition or omission.
4
Apply to an Islamic argument
Test a real argument from Kalam or Fiqh: e.g., 'The world is temporally originated; everything temporally originated has a cause; therefore the world has a cause.' Are the premises manifest? Is the order correct?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والقول الفاصل ما تكون مقدماته ظاهر في الصدق. وكذلك ترتيبه في الإصابة بالفعل ويليه الذي تكون مقدماته قريبة من تلك المقدمات؛ والنتيجة لازمة منها. وإن كان قد عدم تمام هيئة التأليف فهو يجيب يعود إلى التأليف الواجب بتعليل تقديم أو تأخير وزيادة ونقصان.
The decisive statement is that whose premises are manifest in truth. And similarly its arrangement in actual correctness. It is followed by that whose premises are close to those premises, and the conclusion is necessary from them. But if it lacks complete compositional form, then it must return to the required composition by explaining a precedence or delay, increase or decrease.
Source pages p. 579

Commentary
This passage defines the 'decisive statement' (al-qawl al-fāṣil) in dialectical reasoning. The author begins by stating that the decisive statement has premises that are clearly true ('ẓāhir fī al-ṣidq'). Moreover, its arrangement (tartīb) leads to actual correctness (al-iṣāba bi al-fi'l). Then he describes a weaker form that follows it: that whose premises are close to those first premises, and the conclusion necessarily follows from them. If such a statement lacks the perfect compositional form (hay'a al-ta'līf), then the speaker must return to the required composition by giving reasons for what is presented or delayed, or by addition or omission. This shows the author's concern for both the truth of premises and the correct logical structure.

Key Terms
القول الفاصل
مقدمات
هيئة التأليف

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) A high number of premises and a final conclusion.

Key Takeaway
A decisive statement requires both manifestly true premises and a correctly arranged syllogistic form.

The Five Vices of Non-Decisive Arguments
Classification of non-decisive argument types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify the five types of non-decisive arguments (al-radhā'il) and identify examples of each from Islamic dialectic.

Core Idea
Not every productive argument is sound; the vice lies in misapplication, deception, or improper premises.

In dialectic, not all arguments that appear to produce a conclusion are sound. This lesson examines five 'vices' (radhā'il) in reasoning: arguments that are deceptive, misdirected, use premises from the wrong discipline, depend on falsehood improperly, or confuse a particular instance with a general claim. Understanding these vices sharpens your ability to critique weak arguments in Islamic scholarly debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first vice: Sophistry
Look for arguments that appear productive but are not actually productive at all. The author attributes these to the sophist (mushāghibī).
These are deliberate deceptions, not mere errors.
2
Identify the second vice: Misdirected production
Some arguments are productive, but not for the intended conclusion. The conclusion they prove is not the one the arguer set out to prove.
This is a mismatch between aim and outcome.
3
Identify the third vice: Extraneous premises
Arguments that are productive but use premises from outside the relevant discipline. Example: proving a medical claim using geometrical principles.
The premises are true but not appropriate for the field.
4
Identify the fourth vice: Truth from falsehood improperly
Arguments that reach a true conclusion but only through false premises, and not via proof by contradiction (khulf) nor through common acceptance of falsehood in dialectic.
The falsehood is not justified by dialectical conventions.
5
Identify the fifth vice: Particular masquerading as general
Arguments that appear to prove a general conclusion but actually only prove a particular instance, taken in conjecture as if it were the general.
This conflates a specific case with the universal rule.

وغير الفاصل الذي هو من الرذائل ما يرى أنه منتج وليس بمنتج وهو لمشاغبي، أو هو منتج لكن غير الذي يراد إنتاجه. ومن الرذائل ما يكون منتجا للمطلوب لكن من مقدمات خارجة عن الصناعة غير مناسبة كمن يبرهن على الطب من مقدمات هندسية، أو على المطلوب الجدلي ببرهان تعليمي. ومنها ما إذا كان إنتاجه للصدق إنما هو عن الكذب لا على سبيل قياس الخلف، ولا لأن الكذب مشهور فيكون بالحري أن يلازم المستعمل لأمور هي مستحقة لا لأجل الكذب؛ فإن للكذب مدخلاً في باب الجدل كما مر . ومنها ما يكون على المطلوب بالحقيقة على ما عرف، أو بحسب الظن وهو الذي يخالف حداه في الحقيقة جزئي المطلوب لكنه يؤخذ في الظن مكانه.
And the non-decisive, which is among the vices: that which is thought to be productive but is not productive, and it is for the sophist; or it is productive but not for what is intended to be produced. And among the vices is that which is productive of the desired conclusion but from premises outside the art, inappropriate, like one who demonstrates medicine from geometrical premises, or the dialectical desired conclusion with a demonstrative proof. And among them is that whose production of truth is only from falsehood, not as a proof by contradiction, nor because falsehood is famous so that it would be more appropriate that the user adhere to things that are deserved not because of falsehood; for falsehood has an entry in dialectic as was mentioned. And among them is that which is upon the desired conclusion in reality as known, or according to conjecture, and it is that which contradicts its limit in reality being particular to the desired conclusion but it is taken in conjecture in its place.
Source pages p. 579

Commentary
This passage classifies non-decisive (ghayr al-fāṣil) arguments, which are considered vices (al-radhā'il). The author lists several types: First, arguments that appear productive but are not—these belong to sophistry (al-mushāghibī). Second, arguments that are productive but not for the intended conclusion—these are misdirected. Third, arguments that are productive for the desired conclusion but use premises from outside the proper discipline, e.g., proving medical matters using geometrical premises, or using a demonstrative proof for a dialectical question. Fourth, arguments that produce truth but only from falsehood, neither as proof by contradiction nor because falsehood is commonly accepted; such arguments misuse falsehood. Fifth, arguments that target the real desired conclusion in reality or according to conjecture, but which contradict the true definition of the conclusion in that they take a particular instance in place of the general. This classification shows the author's thorough analysis of fallacious reasoning in dialectic.

Key Terms
الرذائل
مشاغبي
مقدمات خارجة عن الصناعة

Key Takeaway
Non-decisive arguments may appear productive but fail due to deception, misdirection, extraneous premises, improper use of falsehood, or confusion of particular with general.

Identifying the Types of Begging the Question in Dialectical Reasoning
Author details begging the question and its subtypes in dialectical reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and classify the different types of begging the question (al-muṣādara) as described in the classical Islamic logical tradition, using examples from jadal and munazara.

Core Idea
Begging the question is not one fallacy but many—each substitutes something else for the very point at issue.

In dialectical debates (munāẓara), one must guard against assuming the very point that needs proof. The classical logicians dissected begging the question into distinct subspecies, each with its own logical flaw. In this passage, the author enumerates these variants, from substituting broader terms to taking necessary concomitants for the things themselves. Understanding these distinctions equips you to detect when a debater is merely restating the conclusion rather than proving it.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the four main subtypes
The passage lists four ways of begging the question: taking al-aʿamm (the more general) for al-akhāṣṣ (the more specific), its reverse, taking the claim part by part, and taking al-lāzim (the necessary concomitant) instead of the thing itself. Identify each in the Arabic text.
Look for the signal phrases 'منها أن يأخذ الأعم مكان الأخص' etc.
2
Examine the examples given
For each subtype, the author provides a concrete example: opposites vs. contradictories, medicine as knowledge of health and disease separately, rib as separate because it does not participate. Translate the logical structure into ordinary language.
The examples are from the Aristotelian tradition but are standard in Islamic logic.
3
Identify the logical relationship between substituted and original
Determine whether the substitution is broader/narrower, part/whole, or necessary concomitant/thing. This clarifies why the argument is fallacious.
For example, 'knowledge of opposites' is broader than 'knowledge of contradictories'.
4
Apply to a new Islamic dialectical scenario
Given a claim and a questioner's move, identify if and which type of begging the question occurs. Use the categories from the passage to classify the fallacy.
Will be practiced in the 'apply_the_tool' section.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمصادرة التي تجب الظن على وجوه منها أن يأخذ الأعم مكان الأخص كما يقال إن علم المتقابلات واحد مكان قوله إن علم المتضادات واحد ومنها أن يأخذ الأخص مكان الأعم وهو الذي يكون على العكس في المثال الأول ومنها أن يكون الدعوى جملة فيأخذ السائل في المصادرة على جزء جزء منه كمن يريد أن يبين أن الطب معرفة بحال الصحة والمرض؛ فيقول إن الطب معرفة بحال الصحة والطب معرفة بحال المرض. ومنها أن يأخذ اللازم بدل الشيء كمن يقول إن الضلع مباين لأنه غير مشارك. والمصادرات على المتقابلات على وجوه أيضًا إما الحقيقي المذكور، وإما أن يأخذ بدل النقيض محمولا مضادا، وإما أن يوجب في الجزئي مقابل ما أوجب الكلي؛ فإما أن يصادر على ضد لازم ما وضع في المقدمات، أو لازم ضده، أو على ما يلزمه ضد لازم الموضوع والخطأ في المصادرة على المطلوب الأول هو باعتبار النتيجة ومراعاتها. والخطأ في القياس على مقابل المطلوب فذلك باعتبار مناسبة المقدّمات بعضها إلى بعض.
And begging the question that induces conjecture has types: among them is taking the more general in place of the more specific, as it is said that knowledge of opposites is one in place of his saying that knowledge of contradictories is one. And among them is taking the more specific in place of the more general, which is the opposite of the first example. And among them is that the claim is a whole, so the questioner takes in begging the question a part by part of it, like one who wants to show that medicine is knowledge of the state of health and disease, so he says that medicine is knowledge of the state of health and medicine is knowledge of the state of disease. And among them is taking the necessary concomitant in place of the thing, like one who says that a rib is separate because it does not participate. And the sources of begging the question concerning opposites have types as well: either the real one already mentioned, or taking in place of the contradictory a contrary predicate, or affirming of the particular the opposite of what was affirmed of the universal. So either he begs the question on the contrary of the necessary concomitant of what is placed in the premises, or on the necessary concomitant of its contrary, or on what follows from the contrary of the necessary concomitant of the subject. And the error in begging the question concerning the first desired conclusion is in consideration of the conclusion and observing it. And the error in the syllogism concerning the opposite of what is desired is in consideration of the suitability of the premises to one another.
Source pages p. 580

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed classification of begging the question (al-muṣādara) in dialectic. The author lists multiple ways a questioner can assume what needs to be proven. First, substituting a broader term (al-aʿamm) for a narrower one (al-akhāṣṣ), e.g., claiming 'knowledge of opposites is one' instead of 'knowledge of contradictories is one'. Second, the reverse—taking the narrower for the broader. Third, when the claim is a whole, the questioner takes it part by part, e.g., proving medicine is knowledge of health and medicine is knowledge of disease, rather than showing it is knowledge of both together. Fourth, taking a necessary concomitant (al-lāzim) instead of the thing itself, e.g., saying a rib is separate because it does not participate, when non-participation is a necessary property of being separate. Then the author discusses types of begging the question concerning opposites (mutabāqilāt): using the real opposite, replacing a contradictory with a contrary predicate, or affirming of the particular the opposite of what was affirmed of the universal. Finally, he notes that the error in begging the question relates to the conclusion, while the error in a syllogism that aims at the opposite of what is desired relates to the suitability of premises to each other.

Key Terms
al-muṣādara
al-aʿamm wa al-akhāṣṣ
al-lāzim

C) Claiming 'a rib is separate because it does not participate'.
D) Claiming 'the whole is greater than its part'.

Key Takeaway
Begging the question (al-muṣādara) is a family of fallacies in which a premise assumes the conclusion, often by substituting a related but logically equivalent notion.

Acquiring Dialectical Skill Through Practice
Author advises practicing conversion and studying disputed matters to gain dialectical ability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the two recommended practices—syllogism conversion by contradiction and opposition, and studying controversial issues—to strengthen your dialectical ability.

Core Idea
Dialectical skill is forged through practice: convert syllogisms and engage with disputed matters.

The author provides practical advice for those seeking to become skilled in dialectic (jadal). Rather than just memorizing rules, you must actively transform syllogisms and immerse yourself in real debates. This lesson unpacks these two methods.

Guided Path
1
Locate the two practices
Read the Arabic passage and identify the two verbs that describe what the seeker must do: 'يتعوّد عكس القياس بالنقيض والضد' (practice converting the syllogism by contradiction and opposition) and 'البحث في الخلافيات المشهورة' (research famous controversial issues). These are the core drills.
2
Understand conversion by contradiction and opposition
Conversion by contradiction (naqīḍ) means deriving the contradictory of a premise or conclusion; conversion by opposition (ḍidd) means deriving the contrary. This process expands your ability to generate arguments, as the author says 'يتوسع في إنشاء القياسات' (he expands in constructing syllogisms). Practice by taking a simple syllogism from kalam and producing its contradictory and opposite versions.
The contradictory of a proposition is its negation; the contrary is a stronger opposite.
3
Consider the value of studying disputed matters
The author recommends studying 'الخلافيات المشهورة' (famous controversial issues), especially the 'التسكيت الضرورية' (necessary silencers) that either you invented or are from pure dialectics. These are arguments that force silence. Engaging with real debates helps you internalize dialectical strategy. Think of a known theological dispute (e.g., ru'ya Allah) and imagine how a 'necessary silencer' might be constructed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن أراد أن يحصل له ملكة للجدل فعليه أن يتعوّد عكس القياس بالنقيض والضد؛ فيتوسع في إنشاء القياسات. وربَّما اقتدر على أن يلطف متى شاء في ذلك؛ فنقيض القياس من نفسه. ومن جملة ما يعينه عليه هو البحث في الخلافيات المشهورة على الخصوص من التسكيت الضرورية التي اخترعها أو هي من الجدليات المحضة.
And whoever wants to obtain a faculty for dialectic must practice converting the syllogism by contradiction and by opposition, so he expands in constructing syllogisms. And perhaps he will be able to refine whenever he wishes in that; for the contradictory of the syllogism is from itself. And among what helps him in that is research in famous controversial issues, especially from the necessary silencers that he invented or are from pure dialectics.
Source pages p. 580

Commentary
This passage offers practical advice for acquiring a dialectical faculty (malaka li al-jadal). The author recommends two practices: first, habitually converting syllogisms by contradiction (naqīḍ) and by opposition (ḍidd). This involves transforming syllogisms by negating or opposing their conclusions or premises, which expands one's ability to construct arguments. He notes that the contradictory of a syllogism can be derived from the syllogism itself. Second, studying famous controversial issues (al-khilāfiyyāt al-mashhūra), especially the 'necessary silencers' (al-taskīt al-ḍarūriyya) that either the student invented or are from pure dialectics. These silencers are arguments that leave the opponent speechless. The emphasis is on practice and engagement with real dialectical material.

Key Terms

عكس القياس بالنقيض والضد
التسكيت الضرورية

Key Terms from Source Passage

The Four Benefits of Dialectic
Author enumerates four benefits of dialectic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list and explain the four benefits of the art of dialectic as given in this passage.

Core Idea
Dialectic sharpens the mind in four ways: building arguments, refuting them, countering them, and finding premises.

This brief passage from Mantiq Kabir outlines the core strengths that studying dialectic (jadal) provides. The author states four distinct outcomes: acquiring syllogisms, contradiction, opposition, and obtaining premises. These cover the full cycle of argumentation—from construction to refutation to counter-argument and preparation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the purpose of the passage
The author begins with 'And know that the art of dialectic gives strength in...' This is an explicit statement of benefits. The student should note that it's enumerative.
2
Extract the four items
List them in order: (1) acquiring syllogism, (2) contradiction, (3) opposition, (4) reaching premises.
These are numbered in the original Arabic.
3
Define each term in context
Define: Acquiring syllogism (ability to construct deductive arguments), contradiction (munāqaḍa: ability to refute by exposing inconsistency), opposition (muʿāraḍa: ability to present counter-argument), reaching premises (ability to find or derive necessary premises).
4
Reflect on how they form a complete toolkit
The first is constructive, the second and third are critical, the fourth is preparatory. Notice how each benefit complements the others for effective debate.
Later sections will elaborate on each benefit.

These four aspects cover the core dialectical activities: building arguments, refuting opponents, presenting counterarguments, and finding the right premises to support one's position.

Definition and Early Types of Musadara in Dialectic
Author enumerates initial types of musadara in dialectic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify four forms of musadara (begging the question) in dialectical arguments and explain how each shifts the conclusion into the premises.

Core Idea
Musadara smuggles the conclusion into the premises – learn its disguises.

In dialectical debate, one common fallacy is to assume what needs to be proven. This lesson examines the specific types of musadara that appear in al-munazara, from using a general term in place of a specific one to dividing a claim into parts. Recognizing these patterns sharpens your critique of weak arguments.

Guided Path
1
Define musadara
Understand that musadara (begging the question) occurs when the conclusion or its equivalent is taken as a premise, either explicitly or implicitly.
The passage distinguishes musadara 'in reality' and 'according to opinion'.
2
Identify the four forms
List the four forms: (1) taking the general (a'amm) in place of the specific (akhass), (2) taking the specific in place of the general, (3) taking a composite claim and asking for each part separately, (4) taking the necessary (lazim) instead of the thing (malzum).
Example for form 1: 'knowledge of opposites' (general) instead of 'knowledge of contraries' (specific).
3
Analyze the logical shift
For each form, determine how the conclusion is hidden. For instance, in the general-for-specific form, the premise includes a broader category that already contains the specific claim, so the argument is circular.
In the medicine example, the composite claim is split, assuming the parts already prove the whole.
4
Practice spotting musadara
Examine given dialectical arguments and identify which form (if any) of musadara is present. Use the examples from the passage as templates.
Look for premises that are just restatements of the conclusion or that presuppose the point at issue.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في باب الجدل كما مر . ومنها ما يكون على المطلوب بالحقيقة على ما عرف، أو بحسب الظن وهو الذي يخالف حداه في الحقيقة جزئي المطلوب لكنه يؤخذ في الظن مكانه. والمصادرة التي تجب الظنَّ على وجوه منها / أن يأخذ الأعم مكان الأخص كما يقال إن علم المتقابلات واحد مكان قوله إن علم المتضادات واحد ومنها أن يأخذ الأخص مكان الأعم وهو الذي يكون على العكس في المثال الأول ومنها أن يكون الدعوى جملة فيأخذ السائل في المصادرة على جزء جزء منه كمن يريد أن يبين أن الطب معرفة بحال الصحة والمرض؛ فيقول إن الطب معرفة بحال الصحة والطب معرفة بحال المرض. ومنها أن يأخذ اللازم بدل الشيء كمن يقول إن الضلع مباين لأنه غير مشارك. والمصادرات على المتقابلات على وجوه أيضًا إما الحقيقي المذكور، وإما أن يأخذ بدل النقيض محمولا مضادا، وإما أن يوجب في الجزئي مقابل ما أوجب الكلي؛ فإما أن يصادر على ضد لازم ما وضع في المقدمات، أو لازم ضده، أو على ما يلزمه ضد لازم الموضوع والخطأ في المصادرة على المطلوب الأول هو باعتبار النتيجة ومراعاتها. والخطأ في القياس على مقابل المطلوب فذلك باعتبار مناسبة المقدّمات بعضها إلى بعض. ومن أراد أن يحصل له ملكة للجدل فعليه أن يتعوّد عكس القياس بالنقيض والضد؛ فيتوسع في إنشاء القياسات. وربَّما اقتدر على أن يلطف متى شاء في ذلك؛ فنقيض القياس من نفسه. ومن جملة ما يعينه عليه هو البحث في الخلافيات المشهورة على الخصوص من التسكيت الضرورية التي اخترعها أو هي من الجدليات المحضة.
In the chapter on dialectic as previously mentioned. Among them is what is upon the desired [conclusion] in reality as known, or according to opinion, and it is that which differs from its limit in reality being a particular of the desired but is taken in opinion in its place. The musadara (begging the question) that necessitates opinion has various forms: among them is taking the general in place of the specific, as when it is said 'the knowledge of opposites is one' instead of saying 'the knowledge of contraries is one'; among them is taking the specific in place of the general, which is the reverse of the first example; among them is that the claim is a whole and the questioner takes in the musadara part by part, like one who wants to show that medicine is knowledge of the state of health and disease; so he says 'medicine is knowledge of the state of health' and 'medicine is knowledge of the state of disease'; among them is taking the necessary instead of the thing, like one who says 'the side is distant because it does not share'. The musadara on opposites also has various forms: either the real mentioned, or taking instead of the contradictory a contrary predicate, or affirming in the particular the opposite of what was affirmed in the universal; so either he begs on the contrary of the necessary of what was placed in the premises, or the necessary of its contrary, or on what necessitates the contrary of the necessary of the subject. The error in musadara on the first desired [conclusion] is with respect to the result and observing it. The error in the syllogism on the opposite of the desired is with respect to the suitability of the premises to each other. And whoever wants to acquire a faculty for dialectic must practice converting the syllogism by contradictory and contrary; so he expands in constructing syllogisms. Perhaps he may be able to be subtle when he wishes in that; for the contradictory of the syllogism comes from itself. Among what helps him in that is research in famous controversial issues, especially the necessary refutations that he invented or are from pure dialectical practices.
Source pages p. 580

Commentary
This passage is a classification of types of musadara (begging the question) in dialectical reasoning. The author first defines musadara: it is when the desired conclusion is taken as a premise either in reality or in opinion. Then he lists several forms: (1) taking the general in place of the specific (e.g., saying 'knowledge of opposites is one' instead of 'knowledge of contraries is one'), (2) taking the specific in place of the general (the reverse), (3) taking a composite claim and asking for each part separately (like dividing 'medicine is knowledge of health and disease' into two claims), (4) taking the necessary instead of the thing (e.g., 'the side is distant because it does not share'). There are also forms related to opposites: either real opposition, or taking a contrary predicate instead of the contradictory, or affirming in the particular the opposite of what was affirmed in the universal. Errors in musadara depend on the result and its observance, while errors in the syllogism depend on the suitability of premises. The author then advises the dialectician to practice converting syllogisms by using contradictory and contrary, and to study controversial issues to develop skill.

الظن
الأعم
الأخص
اللازم

D) Taking the necessary instead of the thing

Key Takeaway
Musadara has four common forms: general-for-specific, specific-for-general, part-by-part, and necessary-for-thing, all smuggling the conclusion into the premises.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Classify the following musadara as general-for-specific or part-by-part: 'The knowledge of opposites is one' instead of 'the knowledge of contraries is one'.
?
In the medicine example, explain why asking separately for health and disease constitutes musadara by showing how the premises already contain the conclusion.

Advanced Forms and Error Analysis in Musadara
Author discusses errors in musadara and syllogisms and advises conversion practice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish errors in musadara from errors in syllogism construction and explain how converting syllogisms helps avoid musadara.

Core Idea
A sharp dialectician converts syllogisms to spot hidden assumptions.

The author not only classifies musadara but also diagnoses errors depending on whether they occur in the result or in the premises' compatibility. He then advises practice in converting syllogisms by contradictory and contrary. This lesson unpacks these advanced aspects to refine your dialectical skills.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two error contexts
Note the author's distinction: error in musadara on the first desired conclusion concerns the result (natija) and its observance, while error in the syllogism on the opposite of the desired concerns the suitability (munasaba) of the premises to each other.
The first error is about begging the question; the second is about logical structure.
2
Understand syllogism conversion
The author recommends converting (aks) the syllogism by contradictory (naqid) and contrary (didd). This means taking the original syllogism and forming a new one with the contradictory or contrary of the conclusion or one premise.
Conversion reveals whether the conclusion is genuinely entailed or just assumed.
3
Apply conversion to an example
Take a simple categorical syllogism: 'Every created thing has a cause; the world is created; therefore the world has a cause.' Convert by contradictory: 'The world does not have a cause' (contradictory conclusion) – if the premises still produce this, the original is not valid.
This technique helps detect musadara because if the premises already imply the opposite, the original might be circular.
4
Recognize forms of musadara on opposites
The passage lists further forms: taking a contrary predicate instead of the contradictory, affirming in the particular the opposite of what was affirmed in the universal, etc. Understand these as variants of shifting the logical relationship.
These are more subtle and often appear in technical theological debates.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في باب الجدل كما مر . ومنها ما يكون على المطلوب بالحقيقة على ما عرف، أو بحسب الظن وهو الذي يخالف حداه في الحقيقة جزئي المطلوب لكنه يؤخذ في الظن مكانه. والمصادرة التي تجب الظنَّ على وجوه منها / أن يأخذ الأعم مكان الأخص كما يقال إن علم المتقابلات واحد مكان قوله إن علم المتضادات واحد ومنها أن يأخذ الأخص مكان الأعم وهو الذي يكون على العكس في المثال الأول ومنها أن يكون الدعوى جملة فيأخذ السائل في المصادرة على جزء جزء منه كمن يريد أن يبين أن الطب معرفة بحال الصحة والمرض؛ فيقول إن الطب معرفة بحال الصحة والطب معرفة بحال المرض. ومنها أن يأخذ اللازم بدل الشيء كمن يقول إن الضلع مباين لأنه غير مشارك. والمصادرات على المتقابلات على وجوه أيضًا إما الحقيقي المذكور، وإما أن يأخذ بدل النقيض محمولا مضادا، وإما أن يوجب في الجزئي مقابل ما أوجب الكلي؛ فإما أن يصادر على ضد لازم ما وضع في المقدمات، أو لازم ضده، أو على ما يلزمه ضد لازم الموضوع والخطأ في المصادرة على المطلوب الأول هو باعتبار النتيجة ومراعاتها. والخطأ في القياس على مقابل المطلوب فذلك باعتبار مناسبة المقدّمات بعضها إلى بعض. ومن أراد أن يحصل له ملكة للجدل فعليه أن يتعوّد عكس القياس بالنقيض والضد؛ فيتوسع في إنشاء القياسات. وربَّما اقتدر على أن يلطف متى شاء في ذلك؛ فنقيض القياس من نفسه. ومن جملة ما يعينه عليه هو البحث في الخلافيات المشهورة على الخصوص من التسكيت الضرورية التي اخترعها أو هي من الجدليات المحضة.
In the chapter on dialectic as previously mentioned. Among them is what is upon the desired [conclusion] in reality as known, or according to opinion, and it is that which differs from its limit in reality being a particular of the desired but is taken in opinion in its place. The musadara (begging the question) that necessitates opinion has various forms: among them is taking the general in place of the specific, as when it is said 'the knowledge of opposites is one' instead of saying 'the knowledge of contraries is one'; among them is taking the specific in place of the general, which is the reverse of the first example; among them is that the claim is a whole and the questioner takes in the musadara part by part, like one who wants to show that medicine is knowledge of the state of health and disease; so he says 'medicine is knowledge of the state of health' and 'medicine is knowledge of the state of disease'; among them is taking the necessary instead of the thing, like one who says 'the side is distant because it does not share'. The musadara on opposites also has various forms: either the real mentioned, or taking instead of the contradictory a contrary predicate, or affirming in the particular the opposite of what was affirmed in the universal; so either he begs on the contrary of the necessary of what was placed in the premises, or the necessary of its contrary, or on what necessitates the contrary of the necessary of the subject. The error in musadara on the first desired [conclusion] is with respect to the result and observing it. The error in the syllogism on the opposite of the desired is with respect to the suitability of the premises to each other. And whoever wants to acquire a faculty for dialectic must practice converting the syllogism by contradictory and contrary; so he expands in constructing syllogisms. Perhaps he may be able to be subtle when he wishes in that; for the contradictory of the syllogism comes from itself. Among what helps him in that is research in famous controversial issues, especially the necessary refutations that he invented or are from pure dialectical practices.
Source pages p. 580

Commentary
This passage is a classification of types of musadara (begging the question) in dialectical reasoning. The author first defines musadara: it is when the desired conclusion is taken as a premise either in reality or in opinion. Then he lists several forms: (1) taking the general in place of the specific (e.g., saying 'knowledge of opposites is one' instead of 'knowledge of contraries is one'), (2) taking the specific in place of the general (the reverse), (3) taking a composite claim and asking for each part separately (like dividing 'medicine is knowledge of health and disease' into two claims), (4) taking the necessary instead of the thing (e.g., 'the side is distant because it does not share'). There are also forms related to opposites: either real opposition, or taking a contrary predicate instead of the contradictory, or affirming in the particular the opposite of what was affirmed in the universal. Errors in musadara depend on the result and its observance, while errors in the syllogism depend on the suitability of premises. The author then advises the dialectician to practice converting syllogisms by using contradictory and contrary, and to study controversial issues to develop skill.

Key Terms

الظن
القياس
النقيض
الضد

The Five Benefits of Dialectic
Author lists the benefits of studying dialectic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the five benefits of dialectic as identified by the author and explain their significance in Islamic debate (jadal).

Core Idea
Dialectic empowers you with syllogisms, contradiction, opposition, premise discovery, and question assessment.

The author begins by stating that the art of dialectic gives power in five specific ways. These are not just theoretical but practical tools for any dialectician. Understanding them helps you see why this art is valuable for Islamic sciences like kalam and fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the five benefits from the Arabic text
Locate the list: 'القوة على اكتساب القياس، وعلى المناقضة، وعلى المعارضة، والتوصل إلى المقدمات، والشعور بصحة السؤالات وعدم الصحة'. Note the conjunction 'و' linking each benefit.
The benefits are: (1) power to acquire syllogisms, (2) power to contradict, (3) power to oppose, (4) arriving at premises, (5) sensing correctness and incorrectness of questions.
2
Explain the first benefit: power to acquire syllogisms
Dialectic trains you to construct valid syllogisms, which are central to logical reasoning in Islamic theology.
For example, in kalam, proving the existence of God uses categorical syllogisms.
3
Explain the second and third benefits: contradiction and opposition
Contradiction (naqd) means refuting the opponent's argument by showing internal inconsistency. Opposition (mu'arada) means presenting a counter-argument. These are key in debates.
Distinguish: naqd attacks the opponent's own reasoning; mu'arada provides an alternative reasoning.
4
Explain the fourth and fifth benefits: arriving at premises and sensing correctness of questions
Dialectic helps you reach the underlying premises of arguments and judge whether a question is valid or invalid.
This skill is crucial in munazara to avoid fallacious questions.
5
Reflect on how these benefits interconnect
Each benefit builds on the others. Syllogisms require premises; contradiction and opposition use the ability to assess questions.
Together they form a comprehensive toolkit for the dialectician.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن صناعة الجدل يفيد القوة على اكتساب القياس، وعلى المناقضة، وعلى المعارضة، والتوصل إلى المقدمات، والشعور بصحة السؤالات وعدم الصحة فيجب على الجدلي أن لا يزال بطلب الدرية بالاحتجاج للشيء الواحد من المواضع المذكورة بما لاح له من الحجج ؛ ثم يعود ويحتج بمقابله من مواضع أخرى فتارة يعاند مقدمات تلك الحجج، وتارة مقدمات هذه الحجج التي يقابلها : فإن ذلك يخرجه خصوصا إذا كان جيد الطبع دائم الفكرة في الأشياء فإنه خليق لمثل هذه الصناعة؛ وبالله التوفيق.
Know that the art of dialectic gives power: to acquire syllogisms, to contradict, to oppose, to arrive at premises, and to perceive the correctness of questions and their incorrectness. So it is incumbent upon the dialectician to continually seek skill by arguing for the same thing from the mentioned positions with whatever arguments appear to him; then he returns and argues with its opposite from other positions, sometimes contradicting the premises of those arguments, sometimes the premises of these arguments that oppose it. That will bring him out, especially if he is of good nature, constantly thinking about things, for he is fit for such an art. And with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 580

Commentary
This passage explains the benefits of dialectic. The author states that this art strengthens the practitioner in five ways: (1) acquiring syllogisms, (2) contradiction (naqd), (3) opposition (mu'arada), (4) arriving at premises, (5) sensing the correctness or incorrectness of questions. Then he advises the dialectician to continuously train by arguing for a single thesis using various arguments from the mentioned positions, and then to argue for the opposite thesis using other positions, sometimes contesting the premises of one set, sometimes the other. This practice refines his skill, especially if he is naturally inquisitive and constantly thinking. The art of dialectic thus requires persistent practice and a questioning nature.

Key Terms
المناقضة
المعارضة
الجدلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Dialectic provides five essential powers: syllogism, contradiction, opposition, premise discovery, and question assessment.

How to Practice Dialectic: Argue Both Sides
Author advises the dialectician to practice by arguing both sides.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the dialectician's method of arguing for a thesis and then its opposite to strengthen reasoning, as prescribed by the author.

Core Idea
True dialectical skill comes from arguing both sides of an issue repeatedly.

After listing the benefits, the author advises the dialectician on how to train. The method is simple yet profound: argue for a thesis, then argue for its opposite, and test the premises of each. This practice hones your ability to handle objections and refine arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's advice: argue for the same thing from various positions
The author says: 'يحتج للشيء الواحد من المواضع المذكورة بما لاح له من الحجج'. This means the dialectician should first gather multiple arguments for a single thesis from different angles.
For example, if the thesis is 'the world is created', find arguments from temporality, need for a cause, etc.
2
Understand the next step: then argue with its opposite from other positions
Then he should argue for the opposite thesis using different arguments: 'ثم يعود ويحتج بمقابله من مواضع أخرى'.
The opposite here is 'the world is eternal'. The dialectician must present the best case for that side.
3
Recognize the two ways to challenge premises
The dialectician sometimes contradicts the premises of the first set of arguments, sometimes the premises of the opposing set: 'فتارة يعاند مقدمات تلك الحجج، وتارة مقدمات هذه الحجج التي يقابلها'.
This means he attacks the foundations of each side alternately, not just defending one.
4
Consider the character traits that help
The author says this method works especially for one who is 'جيد الطبع دائم الفكرة' – of good nature and constantly thinking. Persistent reflection is key.
Intellectual curiosity and dedication are prerequisites for mastering dialectic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Know that the art of dialectic gives power: to acquire syllogisms, to contradict, to oppose, to arrive at premises, and to perceive the correctness of questions and their incorrectness. So it is incumbent upon the dialectician to continually seek skill by arguing for the same thing from the mentioned positions with whatever arguments appear to him; then he returns and argues with its opposite from other positions, sometimes contradicting the premises of those arguments, sometimes the premises of these arguments that oppose it. Source pages p. 580

Commentary
This passage explains the benefits of dialectic. The author states that this art strengthens the practitioner in five ways: (1) acquiring syllogisms, (2) contradiction (naqd), (3) opposition (mu'arada), (4) arriving at premises, (5) sensing the correctness or incorrectness of questions. Then he advises the dialectician to continuously train by arguing for a single thesis using various arguments from the mentioned positions, and then to argue for the opposite thesis using other positions, sometimes contesting the premises of one set, sometimes the other. Four Genera of Dialectical Exchanges and Parts of Contentious Industry
Author enumerates four types of dialectical exchanges and lists the first three parts of contentious industry.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four genera of syllogistic exchanges and list the first three parts of contentious industry.

Core Idea
Dialectical exchanges are classified into four genera; contentious industry has five parts.

This passage introduces a classification of logical debates into four types based on their purpose and method. The author also begins detailing the contentious industry, which uses fallacious reasoning in a structured way. Understanding this classification helps you recognize the nature of different debates and how to engage in them properly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four genera
The author lists four genera of syllogistic exchanges: demonstrative (burhāniyya), dialectical (jadaliyya), examinatory (imtiḥāniyya), and contentious (mushāghabiyya). Remember that 'syllogistic exchanges' refers to debates based on logical reasoning about universal matters.
These are distinct from sophistical exchanges, as the author distinguishes them.
2
Recall the distinction between sophistical and contentious
The author notes that the fallacious (mughāliṭī) encompasses all genera, but contentious is a specific type. You have previously learned the difference; here the author emphasizes that contentious is not fully understood yet.
3
List the parts of contentious industry
The author begins enumerating the five parts of contentious industry. Only three are given in this passage: (1) fallacious silencing (al-taskīt al-mughāliṭī), (2) castigation (al-tashnī‘) by using what the opponent concedes, and (3) the flow of speech (sawq al-kalām). The remaining two are missing from the surviving text.
Memorize these three parts as a foundation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونقول: إن أجناس المحاورات القياسية المتعلقة بالأمور الكلية أربعة: [1] البرهانية [ب] والجدلية، [ت] والامتحانية، [ث] والمشاغبية. وقد عرفتها من قبل، وعرفت الفرق بين السوفسطائية والمشاغبية، وعرفت أن المغالطي يجمعها جميعًا لكن ما عرفت المشاغبية كما ينبغي. فنقول إن أجزاء الصناعة المشاغبية خمسة [أ] أحدها التسكيت المغالطي؛ [ب] وثانيها التشنيع بما يتسلم مما يسلمه أو يقوله المخاطب؛ [ت] وثالثها سوق الكلام
And we say: The genera of syllogistical exchanges concerning universal matters are four: demonstrative, dialectical, examinatory, and contentious. You have known them before, and you have known the difference between sophistical and contentious, and you have known that the fallacious comprises all of them, but you have not known the contentious as it should be. So we say: The parts of the contentious industry are five: first, fallacious silencing; second, castigation by what is taken from what the opponent concedes or says; third, the flow of speech...
Source pages p. 582

Commentary
This passage classifies the types of dialectical exchanges (muḥāwarāt) into four genera: demonstrative (burhāniyya), dialectical (jadaliyya), examinatory (imtiḥāniyya), and contentious (mushāghabiyya). The author notes that the fallacious (mughāliṭī) encompasses all of these but emphasizes that the nature of contentious has not been fully explained. He then begins to enumerate the five parts of the contentious industry (ṣinā‘a mushāghabiyya). Only three parts are given in the surviving text: (1) fallacious silencing (al-taskīt al-mughāliṭī), (2) castigation (al-tashnī‘) by using what the opponent concedes or says, and (3) the flow of speech (sawq al-kalām). The remaining two parts are missing from the OCR window. This classification helps students understand the structure of contentious arguments and how they differ from other forms of logical exchange.

Classifying Debaters: Dialectical vs Eristic
Author categorizes debaters and their treatment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a debater as dialectical or eristic based on their adherence to dialectical rules and determine the appropriate response.

Core Idea
Not every opponent is worth the same response — distinguish dialectical from eristic.

In Islamic dialectical tradition, not all debaters follow the rules. The author distinguishes between those who argue according to dialectical laws (al-jadaliyyūn) and those who deviate into eristic (al-mushāghabiyyūn). Knowing this distinction helps you choose the correct approach: whether to impose dialectical rules, teach, refute, or disengage.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of opponent
Determine if the debater is dialectical (follows accepted premises and rules) or eristic (deviates from rules). Dialectical ones can be engaged using dialectical methods.
The author says: 'الجدليون يحاورون على قوانين الجدلية فألزموها.'
2
Assess intention if eristic
If the debater is eristic, check if the deviation is unintentional (they studied the rules but slipped) or intentional (they aim to confuse).
Unintentional: 'لم يكن ذلك منه بقصد'; intentional: 'قاصدًا إلى المشاغبة قطعًا'.
3
Respond based on intention
For unintentional eristic, teach them the truth. For intentional eristic, do not continue teaching but refute effectively ('يرمي عن قوته').
4
Handle those ignorant of laws
If the opponent does not understand logical rules at all, it is preferable not to engage them in debate.
الأولى أن لا يشتغل معه بما لا تحدى.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الجدلي والمشاغبي من الأقوام؛ قوم لا يمكن أن تجري المخاطبة معهم على قوانين الحكمة والأصول الحقيقية؛ إذ لذلك نوع من المخاطبة غير الذي يمكن أن يفهمه أولئك بل يجب أن تجري مجرى المحاورة معهم على ما هم عليه؛ فالجدليون يحاورون على قوانين الجدلية فألزموها. وأما إذا حادوا عنها وشاغبوا فإن كانوا من جملة من نظروا في القوانين ثم استعملوها فحادوا عنها لم يخل من أن يكون المخاطب منه: إما أن يكون من قوة أن يفهم إذا فهم، ويرجع إلى الواجب إذا أبصر وهذا؛ فيكون مثله ممن قر له إن كان مشاغبيا لم يكن ذلك منه بقصد. وإما أن يكون قاصدًا إلى المشاغبة قطعًا. وإن فهم الحق فكان له قدرة أن يفهم؛ فليس يتبع معه الاشتغال بتفهيم الحق فيجب أن يرمي عن قوته. وأما الذي لا يفهم القوانين؛ ولو فهمها فإما أن لا يحاور أصلا، وإما أن يكون لداع من الدعاوي وعلة من العلل: والأولى أن لا يشتغل معه بما لا تحدى. أو لا يفهم مثلا إذا قال قائل «أ» ليس الضعف ضعفًا للنصف فالنصف له ضعف؛ فيكون الضعف إذن ضعف ما له ضعفين: وهذا هذيان فإذن ليس الضعف ضعفًا للنصف. وإنما وقع هذا لأنه لم يُعلم أن الهذيان غير الباطل. وإن الهذيان يجعل ما يجب عنه هذيانا مثله لا باطلا؛ فقوله الضعف ضعف النصف هذيان فإنه لا ضعف إلا ضعف النصف. فإذا فهمنا الضعف لم يكن لنا فائدة في أن يقول إنه ضعف النصف، وعلى هذا فيما يكون من مثل هذا الكلام.
Then the dialectical and eristic among the people: a group with whom discourse cannot proceed according to the laws of wisdom and true principles; for that there is a type of discourse other than what those can understand, but it must proceed in the manner of dialogue with them according to what they are upon. The dialecticians dialogue according to dialectical laws, so impose them. But if they deviate from them and become eristic, then if they are among those who looked into the laws and then used them and deviated from them, it is not free from the interlocutor being either of such strength that he understands when he understands and returns to the obligation when he sees it; thus his like is one for whom it is said that if he was eristic, it was not intentional. Or he is intentionally aiming at eristic absolutely. And if he understands the truth and has the ability to understand, then engagement in teaching him the truth should not follow; rather, one should throw from his strength. As for the one who does not understand the laws, even if he understood them, either he does not dialogue at all, or he does so due to some claim and some cause: the preferable is not to occupy oneself with what is not limited. Or he does not understand, for example, when someone says 'A is not the double of half, so half has a double; then the double is the double of what has two doubles': this is nonsense, so the double is not the double of half. This occurred only because it was not known that nonsense is other than falsehood. And nonsense makes what follows from it nonsense like it, not false. So his statement 'the double is the double of half' is nonsense, for there is no double except the double of half. If we understand 'double', we have no benefit in his saying it is the double of half, and likewise for what is of this sort of speech.
Source pages p. 594

Commentary
This passage discusses the practical handling of fallacious debaters. The author identifies two categories: dialectical (جدلي) and eristic (مشاغبي). Dialectical debaters can be engaged using dialectical rules. If they deviate and become eristic, we further distinguish: some who have studied the rules but unintentionally deviate, and others who intentionally aim to cause confusion. For the unintentional, we help them see the truth; for the intentional, we should not continue to teach them but rather 'throw from his strength' meaning to refute him effectively. Then there are those who do not understand logical rules at all; it is preferable not to engage them. The author gives an example of nonsense about 'double' and 'half' which is meaningless (هذيان) rather than false. The example illustrates a person saying 'A is not double of half' and then drawing absurd conclusions. The author explains that this is not false but mere nonsense because it doesn't follow logical definitions.

Key Terms
الجدلي
المشاغبي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, what should you do with a debater who has studied the logical rules but intentionally deviates from them to cause confusion?
A) Teach them the truth patiently
B) Refute them effectively and move on
C) Ignore them completely
D) Appeal to their emotions

Key Takeaway
Classify your opponent before choosing your response: dialectical, eristic (intentional or unintentional), or ignorant of laws.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
1. Consider a debate about whether the world is created. Classify the opponent who says 'If the world is created then its creator must be created too' as dialectical or eristic, and explain your reasoning.
?
2. Construct a scenario where an opponent is intentionally eristic and describe how you would refute them according to the author's advice.

Distinguishing Nonsense from Falsehood
Author illustrates nonsense and distinguishes it from falsehood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify an argument as nonsense (hadhyān) rather than false (bāṭil) using the author's criterion of meaninglessness.

Core Idea
Nonsense is not false — it is meaningless, and it poisons any argument it touches.

The author gives an illustrative example of a nonsensical argument about 'double' and 'half'. He distinguishes between nonsense (هذيان) and falsehood (باطل). Nonsense is speech that does not follow logical definitions, making it impossible to evaluate as true or false. Recognising nonsense is crucial in debate to avoid being drawn into meaningless discourse.

Guided Path
1
Read the example
The example: 'A is not the double of half, so half has a double; then the double is the double of what has two doubles.' This is the statement that the author labels as nonsense.
Arabic: 'أ ليس الضعف ضعفًا للنصف فالنصف له ضعف؛ فيكون الضعف إذن ضعف ما له ضعفين'
2
Identify the logical flaw
The argument violates the definition of 'double' which inherently relates to 'half'. It creates an infinite regress of 'doubles of doubles' without coherent meaning.
3
Understand the author's distinction
The author says this is nonsense (هذيان), not falsehood (باطل). Nonsense does not have truth value; it is meaningless because it does not adhere to logical definitions.
الهذيان غير الباطل
4
Apply the principle
Once you recognize nonsense, you should not attempt to refute it as false; instead, point out that it is meaningless and dismiss it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
