Dialectical Roles and Classification of Fallacies
Author explains dialectical context and classifies sophistical arguments as material or formal fallacies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a sophistical syllogism as invalid in matter or form and explain the roles of the questioner and respondent in a dialectical exchange.

Core Idea
Fallacies arise either from false premises or flawed structure; the respondent may use probable premises to counter sophists.

In this passage, the author discusses how logical principles apply to both the questioner and respondent in debate. He distinguishes between material and formal fallacies and explains the respondent's strategy of using commonly accepted premises to refute sophists. This lesson will help you analyze dialectical exchanges and identify where an argument goes wrong.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dialectical context
Note that the author addresses both sides: questioner (sā'il) and respondent (mujīb). The goal is to manifest truth or conceal it via sophistry.
The respondent may abandon truth for commonly accepted premises to counter the sophist.
2
Distinguish material vs formal invalidity
A syllogism can be invalid because its premises are false (material) or because its logical form is flawed (formal). The author lists both possibilities.
Material fallacy: false premises. Formal fallacy: invalid structure, even if premises are true.
3
Determine the resolver's approach
If the fallacy is material, examine and refute the premises. If formal, recognize that it is not a valid syllogism despite its appearance and analyze its form.
The resolver must also check the conclusion for falsity, which alerts to error.
4
Apply to a real dialectical scenario
Imagine a kalam debate: a sophist argues 'The world is eternal because it has no cause.' Identify whether the fallacy is material (false premise that everything has a cause) or formal (non sequitur from 'no cause' to 'eternal').
Use the classification to determine the appropriate refutation strategy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المنطقية معتبرة من طرفي السائل والمجيب فإن إظهار الحق كما يكون باعتبارها فكذلك إخفاء الحق وهو اتفاق الشبهة قصدًا. فالقياس من الجانبين قد يكون تارة صادقًا ومن صوادق، وقد يكون تارة بخلافه كما لا يكون صادقًا ولا من صوادق بل يكون بحسب الظنِّ مثلا. وأما المجيب في حل الشبهة تارة ينبغي أن يترك فيه المشهور بالحق، وتارة أن يترك الحق بالمشهور والمظنون؛ فإن الغرض في مفاوضة السوفسطائي ليس أن يقاس عليهم بالحق بل أن يُجازوا عن المراء مراء. فلا يبعد أن ينحرفوا عن الحق إلى المشهور والمظنون والقياس قد يكون باطلًا في نفسه؛ والسوفسطائي يستعمله مع علمه بأنه باطل مكانه، أو بأن يعتقد أنه صحيح في نفسه لجهله بصحة القياس. وعلى الغير أن يبين وجه البطلان بالبرهان؛ وذلك بوجوه؛ إذ القياس قد يكون باطلا بحسب مادته وقد يكون باطلا بحسب صورته، وقد يكون باطلا بحسب مادته وصورته والكل يعرف من بعد. وعلى هذا إذا كان القياس مغالطيّا فإنه قد يكون مغالطيا لمادته، وقد يكون مغالطيّا لصورته. والأول ينقض من جهة مقدماته، والثاني وإن كان يشبه في صورته القياس فإنه ليس بقياس؛ والحل قد يكون فيه من الوجهين إذا كانت المقدّمات كاذبة، فعلى الحال أن ينظر في صورته أيضًا، ويحل الشبهة منها وينظر أيضًا في النتيجة فإنه النتيجة إذا كانت كاذبة كانت متنبهة على القياس، وما فيه من الغلط. والغلط قد يكون باشتراك المفهوم على وجوهه فإنها إما أن تكون لأن السؤال يكون كثيرًا، وإما أن تكون الكثرة في النتيجة. وتلك الكثرة يكون الحق في بعضها موجودًا وفي بعضها ليس بموجود كما إذا قيل هل الساكت متكلم فإنه تغليط في النتيجة؛ فينتج نتيجتين، ولا يشعر باشتراكه وهو مقدمة بعد.
The logical considerations are observed from both sides of the questioner and the respondent. For manifesting the truth may be by means of them, and likewise concealing the truth, which is intentionally agreeing upon a sophism. So the syllogism from both sides may sometimes be true and from true premises, and sometimes otherwise, not being true nor from true premises, but being according to opinion, for example. As for the respondent in resolving the sophism, sometimes he should abandon the well-known for the truth, and sometimes abandon the truth for the well-known and the opined; for the purpose in debating the sophist is not to measure against them by truth, but to repulse them from wrangling with wrangling. So it is not far that they deviate from the truth to the well-known and the opined. And the syllogism may be invalid in itself; the sophist uses it knowingly that it is invalid in its place, or believing it is correct in itself due to ignorance of the validity of the syllogism. And it is upon another to clarify the aspect of invalidity by demonstration; and that by ways. For the syllogism may be invalid according to its matter, and may be invalid according to its form, and may be invalid according to its matter and its form, and all this is known later. And upon this, if the syllogism is sophistical, then it may be sophistical in its matter, and may be sophistical in its form. The first is refuted from the side of its premises, and the second, even if it resembles in its form the syllogism, it is not a syllogism. And the solution may be in it from both sides: if the premises are false, then the resolver should also look into its form, and resolve the sophism from it, and also look into the conclusion, for if the conclusion is false, it alerts to the syllogism and the error in it. And the error may be due to ambiguity of concept in its aspects: either because the question is multiple, or the multiplicity is in the conclusion. And that multiplicity: truth exists in some of them and does not exist in some, as when it is said: 'Is the silent one speaking?' This is a deception in the conclusion; it produces two conclusions, and one is not aware of its ambiguity, and it is a premise later.
Source pages p. 596

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical discussion on the roles of the questioner (sā'il) and respondent (mujīb) in logical debate, specifically when dealing with sophists. The author explains that logical principles apply to both sides, as truth can be revealed or concealed by them. The syllogism used may be true or false; the respondent may need to abandon truth for commonly accepted or probable premises to counter the sophist, since the goal is not to measure by absolute truth but to refute the sophist's argument. The syllogism can be invalid in matter (premises) or form (structure), or both. A sophistical syllogism is one that is invalid in either aspect. The resolution involves examining the premises if the fallacy is material, and if formal, even if it resembles a valid syllogism, it is not one. The resolver must also check the conclusion. The author introduces a specific fallacy: ambiguity (ishtirāk al-mafhūm), which can cause multiple questions or conclusions. An example is 'Is the silent one speaking?' – this produces two possible conclusions, and the ambiguity hides the error. The passage ends with a fragment suggesting this will be a premise later.

Key Terms
السائل
المجيب
المادة
الصورة
المغالطة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, how is a sophistical syllogism that is invalid in its form resolved?
A) By examining the premises and showing they are false.
B) By looking at the conclusion only.
C) By recognizing that it resembles a syllogism but is not one, and analyzing its form.
D) By ignoring it and using a different argument.

Key Takeaway
A syllogism can be invalid in its matter or form; resolving a formal fallacy requires recognizing its structural defect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In a kalam debate, a sophist argues: 'The world is eternal because it has no beginning; everything temporal has a beginning; therefore the world is not temporal.' Classify this as a material or formal fallacy and explain how the respondent should resolve it.

The Fallacy of Ambiguity in Dialectical Arguments
Author introduces ambiguity (ishtirāk al-mafhūm) as a source of fallacy in dialectical arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the fallacy of ambiguity (ishtirāk al-mafhūm) and distinguish its occurrence in questions or conclusions.

Core Idea
Ambiguity hides multiple meanings under one term, leading to deceptive conclusions in debate.

The author introduces a specific type of fallacy: ambiguity of concept. This occurs when a term has multiple meanings, causing confusion either in the question or the conclusion. Using an example from the passage, we will learn to detect and resolve such fallacies in Islamic scholarly debates.

Guided Path
1
Locate the ambiguous term
In the example 'Is the silent one speaking?', identify the term that is ambiguous—'speaking' (متكلم) can mean uttering words or communicating in general.
The ambiguity is in the concept (mafhūm), not just the word.
2
Determine where ambiguity occurs
The author says it can be in the question or the conclusion. In the example, it's in the conclusion: 'تغليط في النتيجة'.
The multiplicity (كثرة) of meanings leads to multiple possible conclusions.
3
Analyze the multiple interpretations
The question yields two different conclusions: if 'speaking' means uttering sounds, the silent one is not speaking; if it means logical communication, silence can be a form of speaking.
Truth exists in some interpretations but not in others.
4
Resolve by disambiguating
Clarify the intended meaning and evaluate each interpretation separately. The argument is fallacious because it conflates the meanings.
The resolver must expose the equivocation and reframe the argument with clear definitions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المنطقية معتبرة من طرفي السائل والمجيب فإن إظهار الحق كما يكون باعتبارها فكذلك إخفاء الحق وهو اتفاق الشبهة قصدًا. فالقياس من الجانبين قد يكون تارة صادقًا ومن صوادق، وقد يكون تارة بخلافه كما لا يكون صادقًا ولا من صوادق بل يكون بحسب الظنِّ مثلا. وأما المجيب في حل الشبهة تارة ينبغي أن يترك فيه المشهور بالحق، وتارة أن يترك الحق بالمشهور والمظنون؛ فإن الغرض في مفاوضة السوفسطائي ليس أن يقاس عليهم بالحق بل أن يُجازوا عن المراء مراء. فلا يبعد أن ينحرفوا عن الحق إلى المشهور والمظنون والقياس قد يكون باطلًا في نفسه؛ والسوفسطائي يستعمله مع علمه بأنه باطل مكانه، أو بأن يعتقد أنه صحيح في نفسه لجهله بصحة القياس. وعلى الغير أن يبين وجه البطلان بالبرهان؛ وذلك بوجوه؛ إذ القياس قد يكون باطلا بحسب مادته وقد يكون باطلا بحسب صورته، وقد يكون باطلا بحسب مادته وصورته والكل يعرف من بعد. وعلى هذا إذا كان القياس مغالطيّا فإنه قد يكون مغالطيا لمادته، وقد يكون مغالطيّا لصورته. والأول ينقض من جهة مقدماته، والثاني وإن كان يشبه في صورته القياس فإنه ليس بقياس؛ والحل قد يكون فيه من الوجهين إذا كانت المقدّمات كاذبة، فعلى الحال أن ينظر في صورته أيضًا، ويحل الشبهة منها وينظر أيضًا في النتيجة فإنه النتيجة إذا كانت كاذبة كانت متنبهة على القياس، وما فيه من الغلط. والغلط قد يكون باشتراك المفهوم على وجوهه فإنها إما أن تكون لأن السؤال يكون كثيرًا، وإما أن تكون الكثرة في النتيجة. وتلك الكثرة يكون الحق في بعضها موجودًا وفي بعضها ليس بموجود كما إذا قيل هل الساكت متكلم فإنه تغليط في النتيجة؛ فينتج نتيجتين، ولا يشعر باشتراكه وهو مقدمة بعد.
The logical considerations are observed from both sides of the questioner and the respondent. For manifesting the truth may be by means of them, and likewise concealing the truth, which is intentionally agreeing upon a sophism. So the syllogism from both sides may sometimes be true and from true premises, and sometimes otherwise, not being true nor from true premises, but being according to opinion, for example. As for the respondent in resolving the sophism, sometimes he should abandon the well-known for the truth, and sometimes abandon the truth for the well-known and the opined; for the purpose in debating the sophist is not to measure against them by truth, but to repulse them from wrangling with wrangling. So it is not far that they deviate from the truth to the well-known and the opined. And the syllogism may be invalid in itself; the sophist uses it knowingly that it is invalid in its place, or believing it is correct in itself due to ignorance of the validity of the syllogism. And it is upon another to clarify the aspect of invalidity by demonstration; and that by ways. For the syllogism may be invalid according to its matter, and may be invalid according to its form, and may be invalid according to its matter and its form, and all this is known later. And upon this, if the syllogism is sophistical, then it may be sophistical in its matter, and may be sophistical in its form. The first is refuted from the side of its premises, and the second, even if it resembles in its form the syllogism, it is not a syllogism. And the solution may be in it from both sides: if the premises are false, then the resolver should also look into its form, and resolve the sophism from it, and also look into the conclusion, for if the conclusion is false, it alerts to the syllogism and the error in it. And the error may be due to ambiguity of concept in its aspects: either because the question is multiple, or the multiplicity is in the conclusion. And that multiplicity: truth exists in some of them and does not exist in some, as when it is said: 'Is the silent one speaking?' This is a deception in the conclusion; it produces two conclusions, and one is not aware of its ambiguity, and it is a premise later.
Source pages p. 596

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical discussion on the roles of the questioner (sā'il) and respondent (mujīb) in logical debate, specifically when dealing with sophists. The author explains that logical principles apply to both sides, as truth can be revealed or concealed by them. The syllogism used may be true or false; the respondent may need to abandon truth for commonly accepted or probable premises to counter the sophist, since the goal is not to measure by absolute truth but to refute the sophist's argument. The syllogism can be invalid in matter (premises) or form (structure), or both. A sophistical syllogism is one that is invalid in either aspect. The resolution involves examining the premises if the fallacy is material, and if formal, even if it resembles a valid syllogism, it is not one. The resolver must also check the conclusion. The author introduces a specific fallacy: ambiguity (ishtirāk al-mafhūm), which can cause multiple questions or conclusions. An example is 'Is the silent one speaking?' – this produces two possible conclusions, and the ambiguity hides the error. The passage ends with a fragment suggesting this will be a premise later.

اشتراك المفهوم
المغالطة

Key Takeaway
Ambiguity of concept leads to multiple possible conclusions, allowing deception when the different meanings are conflated.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In a fiqh debate, someone argues: 'The narration is authentic because it is widely transmitted (mutawatir).' The term 'mutawatir' is ambiguous—it can mean linguistically mass-transmitted or technically satisfying conditions of tawatur. Identify the ambiguity and show how it leads to a fallacy.

Dialectical Refutation: Objection and Response in Islamic Logic
Author lists ten objections and provides responses for the first seven.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to deconstruct a dialectical objection-response pair, identify the logical fallacy in the objection, and assess the validity of the response.

Core Idea
A dialectical response often denies a premise rather than the conclusion.

In the Islamic logical tradition, munazara (dialectical debate) is a rigorous method for testing arguments. The author here presents a series of objections (naqd) against a position and then offers responses (jawab). This lesson focuses on the seventh objection and its response, typical of how a logician defends a position by clarifying the nature of the relation between terms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection and its premises
Read the seventh objection carefully. It claims that a thing cannot be real unless it is real, because its reality is either due to itself (and opposites are equal in essence) or due to another (and opposites are equal in relation). The conclusion is that it is impossible for one opposite to be real without the other.
2
Identify the intended logical flaw
The objector tries to show that if a thing is real, its opposite must also be real, leading to a contradiction. This relies on the assumption that both opposites have the same grounding (self or other).
3
Analyze the author's response
The author responds: 'If a thing's reality is due to itself, that thing cannot be but one.' This means when reality is self-subsistent, there is no equality with an opposite—the opposite cannot have the same claim to reality.
The response distinguishes between essential reality (which is unique) and relational reality (which can be shared).
4
Evaluate the response's validity
Check whether the response genuinely blocks the objection. By clarifying that self-subsistent reality excludes an equal opposite, the author shows that the objector's assumption of equality is false in this case, so the contradiction does not arise.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الخامسة فيقال حدوث العالم لا يكون مستلزما نقيض الواقع، وكل ما يكون مستلزما نقيض الواقع فهو واقع؛ أما الصغرى فلأنه إذا كان مستلزما كان منتفيًا بالضرورة، ويلزم منه أنه إذا كان متحققا فلا يكون مستلزما. وأما الكبرى فلأنه إذا لم يكن مستلزما كان واقعا وإلا لكان مستلزما بالضرورة. ولا يقال كيف يكون مستلزما لنقيض الواقع وهو بعينه نقيض الواقع؛ فإن نقيض الواقع أعم منه. [ح] وأما السادسة فيقال إنه إذا كان حادثا فحدوثه لا يخلو من أن يكون حادثا؛ وإنه محال؛ إذ الكلام في ذلك الحادث نحو الكلام في هذا، أو لا يكون حادثا؛ وإنه محال أيضًا فإنه يلزمه منه إما القدم وإما العدم: والكل باطل. [خ] وأما السابعة فيقال إن الشيء لا يمكن أن يكون واقعاً إلا و أن يكون :واقعا وذلك لأن وقوعه إما لذاته؛ وإنهما في الذات على السواء، وإما لغيره؛ وإنهما في النسبة إلى ذلك الغير على السواء فلا يمكن أن يكون هذا واقعا دون ذلك، وبالعكس. / [د] وأما الثامنة فيقال اجتماع الضدين وهما [٣٥٠,] الصدق والكذب في كلام واحد واقع ؛ وذلك لأنه إذا قال واحد إن تكلمت من بعد فذلك صادق ثمَّ قال كذبت، فهذا الكلام إن كان صادقا فالكلام الأول صادق وكاذب معًا، وإن كان كاذبًا فكذلك وهذا ظاهر. [i] وأما التاسعة فيقال إذا كان الشيء ممكن الوجود كان من اللوازم أن لا يكون ممكن الوجود؛ وهذا محال وإنما قلناه؛ وذلك لأنه إذا كان ممكن الوجود فوجوده و عدمه ممكن لا محالة. ومن المحال أن يكون كذلك ؛ وقد كان أحدهما قطعي الوجود، والآخر قطعى العدم دائما. [ر] وأما العاشرة فيقال ما من شيء إلا ويكون ذلك الشيء مستلزما لنقيضه في الجملة؛ وذلك لأنه إذا كان متحققا مع النقيض كان متحققا، وكلَّما كان متحققا مع النقيض كان النقيض متحققا فقد يكون إذا كان متحققا كان النقيض متحققا. أما الجواب؛ فنقول [1] في الأولى: إن الجسم لا يكون إلا وأن يكون قابلا للعدم فلو كان الشيء جسمًا وليس قابلا للعدم لكان ذلك الشيء قابلا للعدم ولا يكون قابلا للعدم أيضًا لكن المقدم محال ؛ والتالي كذلك فإنه لا يمتنع أن يكون المحال مستلزما للمحال. [ب] وفي الثانية نقول: إنه في حيز المنع لما أن المتّصلة الجزئية في القياس الاستثنائي لا ينتج. [ت] وفي الثالثة إن الإمكان علة للاحتياج إلى المؤثر بالضرورة وإلا يمكن أن يكون الشيء ممكنا ولا يكون محتاجًا إلى المؤثر في وجوده، وذلك لا يمكن فإنه إذا لم يكن محتاجا لا يكون ممكنا؛ والكلام في كونه ممكنا. [ث] وفي الرابعة لا نسلم بأن الجسم من حيث هو الجسم لا يمكن أن يكون جزئياً؛ وإنما لا يمكن إذا كان الجسم من حيث هو الجسم يجب أن يكون كليًّا، وليس كذلك ؛ إذ الجسم من حيث هو الجسم متحقق في الأجسام الكلية والجزئية بالضرورة. [ج] وفي الخامسة إن الكبرى في حيز المنع لما أن الشيء إذا كان محتمل الثبوت والانتفاء لا يكون مستلزما نقيض الواقع؛ ولا يلزم أن يكون واقعا بل يمكن أن يكون واقعا، ويمكن أن لا يكون. [ح] وفي السادسة إن الحدوث من الأمور الإضافية؛ والتسلسل إلى غير النهاية في الأمور الإضافية لا يكون ممتنعا أو هو في الوهم لا في الحقيقة. [] وفي السابعة إن وقوع الشيء إذا كان لذات ذلك الشيء لا يمكن أن يكون ذلك الشيء إلا واحدًا كما
As for the fifth: It is said that the temporal origination of the world does not entail the opposite of reality, and everything that entails the opposite of reality is real. As for the minor premise, because if it entails, then it is necessarily negated, and it follows that if it is actual then it does not entail. As for the major premise, because if it does not entail, it is real, otherwise it would necessarily entail. And it is not said: how can it entail the opposite of reality when it is itself the opposite of reality? For the opposite of reality is more general than it. [H] As for the sixth: It is said that if it is originated, its origination must either be originated, which is impossible because the discussion of that originated is like the discussion of this, or not be originated, which is also impossible because it would entail either pre-eternity or non-existence, and all are false. [Kh] As for the seventh: It is said that a thing cannot be real unless it is real, because its reality either is due to itself, and the two are equal in essence, or due to another, and they are equal in relation to that other, so it is impossible for one to be real without the other, and vice versa. / [D] As for the eighth: It is said that the combination of two opposites, namely truth and falsehood, in one statement is real; because if one says 'I spoke afterwards, that is true,' then says 'I lied,' this statement if it is true, the first statement is both true and false together, and if it is false, likewise, and this is obvious. [i] As for the ninth: It is said that if a thing is possible of existence, it is necessary that it not be possible of existence; this is impossible, and we say it because if it is possible of existence, its existence and non-existence are both possible inevitably. And it is impossible for it to be like that when one of them is certain existence and the other certain non-existence always. [R] As for the tenth: It is said that there is nothing except that it entails its opposite in general; because if it is actual with the opposite, it is actual, and whenever it is actual with the opposite, the opposite is actual, so it may be that if it is actual, the opposite is actual. As for the answer, we say: [1] For the first: The body is nothing but that it is receptive to non-existence; so if a thing is a body and not receptive to non-existence, then that thing would be receptive to non-existence and not receptive to non-existence also, but the antecedent is impossible, and the consequent likewise, for it is not impossible that the impossible entails the impossible. [B] For the second: We say: It is in the position of denial because the particular conjunctive conditional in the exceptional syllogism does not produce a result. [T] For the third: Possibility is the cause of the need for the effecter necessarily; otherwise it would be possible that a thing is possible and not need an effecter in its existence, and that is impossible because if it does not need, it is not possible, and the discussion concerns its possibility. [Th] For the fourth: We do not concede that the body as body cannot be particular; it is impossible only if the body as body must be universal, and that is not so, for the body as body is realized in universal and particular bodies necessarily. [J] For the fifth: The major premise is in the position of denial because if a thing is probable of affirmation and negation, it does not entail the opposite of reality; and it is not necessary that it be real, rather it may be real or may not be. [H] For the sixth: Temporality is among relational matters, and infinite regress in relational matters is not impossible, or it is in estimation not in reality. [] For the seventh: If a thing's reality is due to itself, that thing cannot be but one, as...
Source pages p. 602

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exchange where the author presents a series of objections (naqd) against a logical position and then provides his responses (jawab). The objections follow a pattern: each begins with 'وأما الخامسة فيقال' etc., indicating they are arguments from an opponent. The objections range from the fifth to the tenth (the first four were presumably on earlier pages). Each objection targets a specific paradoxical claim. For example, the fifth objection argues that if the origination of the world entails the opposite of reality, then the opposite of reality must be real; but the author's response denies the major premise, noting that a thing with probable truth and falsehood does not entail its opposite. The sixth objection attempts to show that origination leads to infinite regress or contradiction, but the author replies that temporality is relational and infinite regress in relational matters is not impossible—it may be merely a mental estimation. The seventh objection argues that if a thing's reality is due to itself, its opposite would also be real, but the response says that if reality is due to essence, the thing can only be one. The remaining objections (eighth to tenth) are given without responses in this passage; they involve the combination of opposites, the impossibility of possibility, and universal entailment of opposites. The response section then gives answers for earlier objections (first through seventh), each identified by a letter. The answers often employ logical tools: denial of a premise, pointing out a fallacy in the syllogism, or distinguishing between categories. For instance, the second answer notes that a particular conjunctive conditional in an exceptional syllogism yields no result, thus the objection fails. The overall purpose is to defend a coherent logical position against sophistical attacks.

Blocking Invalid Inferences in Dialectical Exchange
Full passage of ten answers to objections on modality and temporal origination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and evaluate the logical steps in a dialectical refutation of an objection, focusing on blocking invalid syllogisms and clarifying entailment.

Core Idea
Dialectical responses in Islamic logic turn on precise logical relations, not mere assertion.

This lesson examines the first two answers from the passage, where the author refutes objections by exposing invalid syllogistic forms and the logical possibility of impossibility entailing impossibility. These moves are fundamental in kalam debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection and the author's response
Read the first objection (about something being necessarily existent and the other necessarily non-existent) and the author's answer. Note the structure: the author accepts the antecedent but denies the conclusion by showing it leads to an impossibility entailing an impossibility.
The objection likely claims a contradiction; the author responds that the impossible can entail the impossible, so no problem.
2
Analyze the logical form of the second objection
The second objection uses a particular conditional in an exceptive syllogism. The author rejects it by stating that a particular conditional yields no conclusion. Verify why: in a conditional syllogism (istithna'i), if the conditional is particular (not universal), the exception may not produce a certain conclusion.
This is a standard rule: only a universal conditional gives certainty in exceptive syllogisms.
3
Compare the logical moves
In the first answer, the author uses the concept of entailment (mustalzim) and holds that impossibility can entail impossibility, so the objection is not effective. In the second, he blocks the argument by pointing out an invalid syllogistic form. Note how each move relies on a logical principle.

Source Text

كان أحدهما قطعي الوجود، والآخر قطعى العدم دائما. [ج] وفي الخامسة إن الكبرى في حيز المنع لما أن الشيء إذا كان محتمل الثبوت والانتفاء لا يكون مستلزما نقيض الواقع؛ ولا يلزم أن يكون واقعا بل يمكن أن يكون واقعا، ويمكن أن لا يكون. [ح] وفي السادسة إن الحدوث من الأمور الإضافية؛ والتسلسل إلى غير النهاية في الأمور الإضافية لا يكون ممتنعا أو هو في الوهم لا في الحقيقة. [] وفي السابعة إن وقوع الشيء إذا كان لذات ذلك الشيء لا يمكن أن يكون ذلك الشيء إلا واحدًا فإن مقتضى الطبيعة الواحدة لا يكون إلا واحدا (١) كما مر. [د] وفي الثامنة أن الاتحاد في الزمان من اللوازم؛ وليس كذلك فيما ذكرتم؛ إذ الكلام الأول بالنسبة إلى نفسه في زمان وبالنسبة إلى الكلام الثاني في زمان آخر. [ذ] وفي التاسعة إذا كان الشيء ممكن الوجود كان وجوده وعدمه ممكناً بالنسبة إلى علمنا لا في نفس الأمر. [ر] وفي العاشرة أنه من جملة ما قد مرَّ الكلام فيه فلا نعيده مرة أخرى.
One of them was necessarily existent, and the other was necessarily non-existent always. [R] As for the tenth, it is said: There is nothing except that that thing entails its opposite in some respect; because if it is realized together with the opposite, it is realized, and whenever it is realized together with the opposite, the opposite is realized; so it may be that if it is realized, the opposite is realized. As for the answer: We say [1] regarding the first: The body is only such that it is receptive to non-existence; so if the thing were a body and not receptive to non-existence, then that thing would be receptive to non-existence and also not receptive to non-existence, but the antecedent is impossible, and the consequent likewise, for it is not impossible that the impossible entails the impossible. [B] Regarding the second: We say: It is in the realm of prevention because the particular conditional in the exceptional syllogism yields no conclusion. [T] Regarding the third: Possibility is necessarily a cause for needing an effecter; otherwise a thing could be possible and not need an effecter for its existence, and that is not possible, because if it does not need, it is not possible; and the discussion is about its being possible. [Th] Regarding the fourth: We do not accept that the body insofar as it is a body cannot be particular; it is only impossible if the body insofar as it is a body must be universal, and that is not so, because the body insofar as it is a body is realized in universal and particular bodies necessarily. [J] Regarding the fifth: The major premise is in the realm of prevention, because if a thing is susceptible to affirmation and negation, it does not entail the opposite of the actual; nor does it necessitate that it is actual; rather it can be actual and can be not. [H] Regarding the sixth: Temporal origination is among relative matters; and infinite regress in relative matters is not impossible, or it is in the imagination, not in reality. [D] Regarding the seventh: If the occurrence of a thing is due to the essence of that thing, that thing cannot be but one, for the requirement of a single nature is only one, as has passed. [Z] Regarding the eighth: Unity in time is among necessary concomitants; but it is not so in what you mentioned, because the first speech relative to itself is in one time and relative to the second speech is in another time. [R] Regarding the ninth: If a thing is possible in existence, its existence and non-existence are possible relative to our knowledge, not in reality. [R] Regarding the tenth: It is among the matters whose discussion has already passed, so we do not repeat it again.
Source pages p. 603

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical response to ten objections raised against various logical and metaphysical claims. Each objection is stated briefly by the opponent, and the author provides a concise refutation. The structure follows the typical jadal format of claim (objection) and answer. The tenth objection is given as an example: the claim that everything entails its opposite in some sense. The answer for the first objection clarifies that impossibility can entail impossibility, so the objection is rejected. For the second, the author notes that a particular conditional in an exceptive syllogism yields no conclusion, so it is blocked. The third argues that possibility necessitates a cause; otherwise the thing would not be possible. The fourth denies that body as such cannot be particular; it is realized in both particular and universal bodies. The fifth states that if a thing is merely possible, it does not entail the opposite of what actually is; it may or may not occur. The sixth treats temporal origination as a relative matter, so infinite regress in relatives is not impossible; the objection imagines a problem where none exists. The seventh, referring to a previous discussion, states that if a thing's occurrence is due to its essence, it must be one; a single nature yields only one effect. The eighth distinguishes temporal unity: two speeches are in different times relative to themselves. The ninth restricts possibility to relation to our knowledge, not reality. The tenth answer refers back to an earlier discussion without repeating it.

Modality and Possibility: Defending Causal Necessity
Full passage of ten answers to objections on modality and temporal origination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a refutation of an objection about possibility and causal need using the concept of entailment from the author's answers.

Core Idea
If something is possible, it necessarily requires a cause; otherwise, it would be impossible.

The author defends the principle that possibility entails a need for an effecter (cause). This lesson examines answers 3, 5, 7, and 9 from the passage, which clarify the relationship between possibility, necessity, and actuality. These arguments are central to kalam proofs for God's existence.

Guided Path
1
Extract the principle in answer 3
Read answer 3: 'Possibility is necessarily a cause for needing an effecter... if it does not need, it is not possible.' Identify the logical entailment: possibility → need for cause. The author argues that otherwise possibility would be impossible.
This is a foundational principle in Islamic cosmology: contingent beings require a necessary cause.
2
Analyze answer 5 on entailment of opposites
Answer 5 states that if a thing is possible (susceptible to affirmation and negation), it does not entail the opposite of what is actual. The author blocks the objection that possibility leads to a contradiction. Understand that possibility does not force actuality or non-actuality.
This avoids the fallacy of thinking that possibility implies actuality of the opposite.
3
Understand answer 7 on essence and unity
Answer 7 says if a thing's occurrence is due to its essence, it must be one; a single nature yields only one effect. This relates to the principle of causality: the cause's nature determines the effect uniquely.
Used in arguments about divine simplicity and uniqueness.
4
Apply the principle in answer 9
Answer 9 restricts possibility to our knowledge, not reality. If something is possible, its existence and non-existence are both possible from our perspective, but in reality, one is actual. This clarifies that epistemic possibility does not contradict ontological determinacy.

The Relationship Between Rhetoric and Dialectic
Author explains the two ways rhetoric and dialectic are similar and differ.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish rhetoric from dialectic in terms of purpose, subject, and type of premises.

Core Idea
Rhetoric and dialectic share purpose and subject but differ in the certainty of their premises.

In this passage, the author clarifies how rhetoric and dialectic relate to each other. They are not completely separate arts; they share two important aspects—purpose and subject matter—while differing in the nature of their principles. Understanding this relationship is crucial for anyone engaging in argumentation, whether in theological debate or public persuasion.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the two types of similarity
The passage introduces two relational terms: musharaka (participation) and mushakala (resemblance). Identify what each refers to. Musharaka covers shared features; mushakala covers a specific similarity with a difference.
Musharaka is about identity in purpose and subject; mushakala is about both using commendable premises, but with different certainty.
2
Analyze the sharing in purpose
Both rhetoric and dialectic seek victory in discussion. However, dialectic achieves victory through logical necessity (ilzam), while rhetoric achieves it through disconnection (infisal). Note that rhetoric also aims at persuading assent.
Disconnection means breaking the opponent's argument, not necessarily proving it false.
3
Analyze the sharing in subject
Neither art has a specific subject matter; they can treat any topic. However, dialectic primarily deals with universals, while rhetoric deals with particulars. This is because the common people (for whom rhetoric is intended) cannot handle abstract universals.
This distinguishes them from demonstrative sciences, which have fixed subjects.
4
Identify the similarity in principles
Both use commendable premises (mahmudat), but dialectic's premises are certain (haqiqiyya), while rhetoric's are opinion-based (zanniyya). This is the key difference in their epistemic status.
Commendable premises are widely accepted, but certainty varies.
5
Examine the faculty types
The passage distinguishes between a habitual faculty (malaka i'tiyadiyya) and an artistic faculty (malaka sina'iyya) for rhetoric. The 'pillar' of rhetoric is the core argument, while 'helpers' are supporting statements. The passage begins to discuss these but ends mid-sentence.
This anticipates a deeper analysis of rhetorical structure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الخطابة قد تشارك الجدل باعتبار وتشاكله باعتبار. [١] أما المشاركة فمن جهتين: إحديهما في القصد والثانية في الموضوع؛ أما في القصد فلأن كل واحد منهما يروم الغلبة في المفاوضة؛ أما القايس فبالإلزام، وأما الآخر فبالانفصال وإن كان في الخطابة غرض آخر هو غرض القايس وهو إقناع التصديق، وكان الآخر لا يكفيه في كمال فعله أن يقاوم المقدمات والقياس فقط بل وإن تعود قياسًا على مقابل نتيجة الخصم، فيتبين كذبه. وأما في الموضوع فلأنه ليس لواحد منهما موضوع يختص به نظيره، وأما الجدل فقد علم أمره وأما الخاطبة فلأن العامة لا يهتدون إلى تمييز الموضوعات بعضها عن بعض، وتخصيص الكلام في موضوع على مبادئ تليق به وحده على ما توجبه الصناعة البرهانية بل الخطابة في ذلك كالجدل وإن كان الجدل التقاؤه الأول إلى الكليات، والخطابة التقاؤها بها الأول إلى الجزئيات؛ فهذه هي المشاركة. [٢] وأما المشاكلة فلأن مبادئها جميعًا المحمودات لكن الجدل محمود؛ وإنه حقيقية، والخطابة محموداتها ظنية. ولما كان كل واحد من الجدل والخطابة متعرضًا لكل موضوع صار كل واحد منهما مشاركًا للعلوم البرهانية في موضوعاتها من وجه؛ فحصل أيضًا بينهما وبين العلوم مناسبة ومشاكلة. وهذه الصناعة يتعاطى أفعالها كل إنسان ويجري بينهم فيها مفاوضات من المدح والذم والشكاية والاعتذار وغير ذلك؛ فمنهم من له ملكة اعتيادية، ومنهم من له ملكة صناعية، ومنهم من يجمع إلى الملكة الاعتيادية ملكة صناعية. وقد ذكر المعلم الأوَّل: «إن السلف كان مقامهم في الخطابة مقام من له ملكة اعتيادية فلم يتعرضوا لملكة صناعية، ولم يتكلموا فيها؛ إذ كان أكثر ما تكلموا فيه هو النظر في الأمور الخارجة عن عمود الخطابة»، وذلك لأن الخطابة لها عمود ولها أعوان. أما العمود فالقول الذي يظن أنه ينتج بذاته المطلوب. وأما الأعوان فأقوال أيضًا، وأقوال خارجة عن ذلك العمود؛ وذلك
Then rhetoric shares with dialectic in a respect and resembles it in a respect. [1] As for sharing, it is from two aspects: one in purpose and the second in subject. As for purpose, because each one of them seeks victory in discussion: the measurer (the dialectician) by making necessary, and the other (the rhetorician) by disconnection, although in rhetoric there is another purpose which is the purpose of the measurer, which is persuading assent, and the other is not sufficient for the perfection of his action to resist only the premises and syllogism, but rather he takes a syllogism against the opponent's result, so its falsehood becomes clear. As for subject, because neither of them has a subject specific to it. As for dialectic, its matter is known, and as for rhetoric, because the common people are not guided to distinguish subjects from each other and to specify speech on a subject with principles befitting it alone according to what the demonstrative craft requires, but rhetoric in that is like dialectic, although dialectic's first encounter is with universals, and rhetoric's first encounter is with particulars. So this is the sharing. [2] As for similarity, because their principles are all commendable, but dialectic's commendable premises are real, and rhetoric's commendable premises are opinion-based. Since each of dialectic and rhetoric deals with every subject, each one becomes sharing with the demonstrative sciences in their subjects from a certain aspect; so there also occurs between them and the sciences appropriateness and similarity. This craft is practiced by every person, and discussions take place among them involving praise, blame, complaint, apology, and other things. Among them are those who have a habitual faculty, and those who have an artistic faculty, and those who combine the habitual faculty with an artistic faculty. The First Teacher mentioned: 'The predecessors were in rhetoric in the station of one who has a habitual faculty, so they did not engage in an artistic faculty, and did not speak about it; since most of what they spoke about was consideration of matters outside the pillar of rhetoric.' That is because rhetoric has a pillar and helpers. As for the pillar, it is the statement that is thought to produce the desired result by itself. As for the helpers, they are also statements, and statements outside that pillar; and that is...
Source pages p. 606

Commentary
This passage analyzes the relationship between rhetoric (khataba) and dialectic (jadal) under two headings: participation (musharaka) and similarity (mushakala). First, participation: (1) in purpose: both seek victory, but by different means – dialectic by logical necessity, rhetoric by disconnection; however, rhetoric also aims at persuading assent, while dialectic also refutes the opponent. (2) in subject: neither has a specific subject matter; they can discuss any topic, unlike demonstrative science. However, dialectic primarily deals with universals, and rhetoric with particulars. Second, similarity: both use commendable premises (mahmudat), but dialectic's are certain (haqiqiyya) while rhetoric's are opinion-based (zanniyya). This leads to rhetoric being a universal art practiced by everyone, with either habitual or artistic skill. The author quotes Aristotle on the distinction between the 'pillar' (the core argument) and the 'helpers' (external supporting statements).

Key Terms
مشاركة
مشاكلة
محمودات
ملكة صناعية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what distinguishes dialectic from rhetoric in terms of premises?
A) Dialectic uses universal premises, rhetoric uses particular premises.
B) Dialectic's premises are certain, rhetoric's are probable.
C) Rhetoric's premises are commendable, dialectic's are not.
D) Both use the same kind of premises.

Key Takeaway
Rhetoric and dialectic share purpose and subject but differ crucially in the certainty of their premises, and rhetoric can be practiced either habitually or as a trained craft.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Distinction Between Dialectical and Rhetorical Use of Accepted Premises
Author distinguishes how dialectician and rhetorician handle premises accepted by the public.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish how a dialectician and a rhetorician treat accepted premises according to the author's criteria.

Core Idea
Accepted premises are used logically by dialecticians, but rhetorically by custom.

This passage defines a key type of premise (al-mahmudat) and shows how different reasoning arts handle it. Understanding this distinction is crucial for grasping the Islamic classification of arguments (the five arts). We will parse the author's definitions and contrast the approaches of the dialectician and rhetorician.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition of accepted premises (المحمودات)
Find the sentence where the author defines 'al-mahmudat' (the accepted premises). Note the phrase 'bi-ḥasab al-ẓann' (according to opinion) and the description of how the public accepts them before examination.
The definition emphasizes social acceptance, not logical certainty.
2
Identify the three types of premise users mentioned
The author distinguishes three users: the dialectician (al-jadalī), the demonstrator (al-burhānī), and the rhetorician (al-khaṭābī). Note how each relates to acceptance and truth.
The demonstrator accepts the premise as accepted but doubts its truth.
3
Contrast the dialectician's and rhetorician's treatment
The dialectician uses accepted premises according to 'shurūṭ al-manṭiq' (conditions of logic). The rhetorician uses them according to 'al-rusūm al-muʿtāda' (customary norms). Identify the author's exact phrasing for each.
This is the core distinction: logic vs. custom.
4
Analyze the case of the probable premise (al-maẓnūna)
The author notes that some accepted premises lose acceptance upon scrutiny, not only due to falsehood but also due to the Law. Consider why a premise might be rejected for non-logical reasons.
This reinforces that rhetorical acceptance is context-dependent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمحمودات بحسب الظنِّ هي التي من شأن الجمهور أن يكون تسلموها قبل النظر فيها والتعقب لها. والجدلي إنما يأخذها محمودة لأنها عند الجمهور محمودة والبرهاني أيضًا يرونها محمودة لا يشكون في أنها محمودة لكنهم إنما يشكون في أنها صادقة والمظنونة منها ما إذا تعقبت زال حمدها لا لأجل ظهور الكذب فقط بل لأجل الشريعة مثلاً. فيكون الخطابي وإن استعمل المحمود الحقيقي فإنما يستعمله من جهة أنه محمود في الظاهر؛ فقد بان إذن أن الجدلي يتصرف في المحمودات على شروط المنطق، والخطابي يتصرف فيها على الرسوم المعتادة بل الرسوم المعتادة في مادة القياس وفي صورته حينئذ.
The premises accepted according to opinion are those that the public is accustomed to accept before examining them and scrutinizing them. The dialectician takes them as accepted because they are accepted among the public, and the demonstrator also sees them as accepted – they do not doubt that they are accepted, but they doubt whether they are true. And the probable ones among them, when scrutinized, lose their acceptance not only because of the appearance of falsehood but also because of the Law, for example. So the rhetorician, even if he uses the true accepted premise, uses it only from the aspect that it is apparently accepted. It has become clear, then, that the dialectician deals with accepted premises according to the conditions of logic, while the rhetorician deals with them according to customary norms, indeed customary norms in the matter of the syllogism and its form.
Source pages p. 617

Commentary
This is a definitional passage that classifies types of premises (al-mahmudat) and distinguishes how two arts—dialectic (jadal) and rhetoric (khataba)—use them. The author begins by defining 'accepted premises' (al-mahmudat bi-hasab al-zann) as those that the public (al-jumhur) tend to accept prior to examination or critical scrutiny. He then explains that the dialectician (al-jadali) takes these premises as accepted precisely because they are accepted by the public, while the demonstrator (al-burhani) also sees them as accepted—they do not doubt their acceptance, but do doubt their truth. The probable (al-maznuna) ones, when critically examined, lose their acceptance not only due to manifest falsehood but also due to the Law (al-shari'a), for example. Then the author clarifies that even if the rhetorician (al-khatabi) uses a truly accepted premise, he uses it only insofar as it is apparently accepted. The conclusion is that the dialectician operates on accepted premises according to the conditions of logic (shurut al-mantiq), while the rhetorician operates on them according to customary norms (al-rusum al-mu'tada), even in the matter and form of the syllogism.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, how does the dialectician (al-jadali) treat accepted premises (al-mahmudat)?
A) He uses them according to customary norms without logical conditions.
B) He uses them according to the conditions of logic.
C) He only uses them if they are certainly true.
D) He never uses accepted premises.

Key Takeaway
The key distinction is that dialecticians use accepted premises under logical conditions, while rhetoricians use them under customary norms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify a premise accepted by the Muslim public (e.g., 'The Quran is the word of God'). Then explain how a dialectician would use it in a debate about the Quran's inimitability (i'jaz) according to logical conditions, versus how a rhetorician would use it in a sermon based on customary acceptance.
?
Consider the statement 'Pilgrimage (hajj) is obligatory on every able Muslim.' Classify its treatment by a dialectician (usul al-fiqh argument) and a rhetorician (khutba).
